It publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from the older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
the “Argon ut” is
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar tinge, but an idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors :
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
the most readable

ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
American newspaper.

upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well

THE RED PAGE.
P. Chalmers Mitchell, in Eng. Saturday Re-
view, has fallen on the familiar local idea that
body fights brain, and the intenser the genius the
greater the disease and the shorter the life. But
his interesting article has been received with un-
familiar astonishment. He writes—
At some Oxford colleges it is the custom, when
the college “eight” or “Torpid” is in training,
for any undergraduate who chooses to invite the
crew to breakfast. The host writes his name
against a vacant ,date on a list kept in some con-
venient place, and at half-past eight on the
appointed morning the routine training-breakfast
arrives in his rooms from the kitchen, and the
crew, glowing from the morning tub and the
morning run round Christ Church meadow, tum-
ble into their places. Naturally the members of
a crew, chosen for the modern aptitude for
rowing of a college boat, are very different people.
There are different sorts and conditions of mental
ability; in the crews which I in my college career
had .the advantage of entertaining, there were
some who were stupid, some who in our college
society passed as brilliant, and there were some
who in the greater arena of the world are coming
to distinction. But who that has played host to a
crew can forget the fatuity of the talk, his forlorn
attempts to rally ihterest about anything other
than the vagaries of Brown’s liver and the excoria-
tion of the skin over Smith’s i/lutceus. And these
men were in the flush of youth and in the pink of
condition, taking even their exercise in modera-
tion, as, the muscular training for inter-college
races is much less arduous than that necessary for
the University boat-race.
* * * *
It must have happened to many readers of
these lines, as to the writer of them, that,
in the middle of a spell of work, the memory
of wild nature comes down like an obses-
sion. The clean, sweet downs, the odorous forest,
the mountains musical with falling water, or the
green plains with their white, poplar-lined roads
call, and are not to be denied. And so, with a
bag pressed full of convenient work, and with
bicycle, or rod, or gun, you are off to your
favourite haunts. Eight hours to sleep, eight
hours to eat and play, eight Hours to work, and,
after a delightful fortnight, you are to return with
the bag of work exploited and your pulses attuned
to health. The ozone eats through the tissues of
brain and body, purging them as fire burns out
the dross; the kind wind, the plumping rain and
the vigorous sun pervade you with health, your
cheek browns, your body straightens, your lungs
expand, and the fickle irregularities of your blood-
stream become transformed to a tumultuous but
evenlymrdered pulse. And your work remains
undone. As you dip your pen, ideas vanish ; your
notes for the next chapter seem random jottings ;
the masses of facts you were to tabulate and co-
ordinate become a horde of mutually repelling,
unrelated atomies. When you have come back to
town, and the old stream has resumed you in its
eddying, buffeting flood, the glow fades from your
cheek, your shoulders become round, and your
heart capricious again ; and then, and not until
then, you pick up the threads of your work.
m ' * ♦ - . *
These familiar instances are brought forward as
the introduction to a proposition or generalisation
which, once made, appears tome to be self-evident.
The proposition is that, in the vast majority of
cases, the best brain-work of which individuals of
average or of unusual ability are capable is per-
formed under conditions of imperfect health. So
far from there being an obvious truth in the
familiar tag, mens sana in corpora sano, the case is
precisely the reverse, and, unless we are to give to
the term sana a preposterous meaning, the mind
at its best is to be found in a body that is not at
its best. I have no reasoned theory to account for
this apparent paradox; but the suggestion lies
ready to hand—that the higher kind of mental-
work is the most recent historical acquisition
of man, and that the conditions favorable to it
have not yet become an integral portion of the
fibre of brain and body. In the course of the
long struggle for existence, man has been moulded
by the forces of nature to robuster purposes
than chopping logic, writing poetry, or developing
theories, and these higher functions of the brain can
flourish only m the secluded garden of an artificial
civilisation. So also, we know, the wall-fruit
bringing to perfection its luscious droplets of con-
densed sunshine is less healthy than its sour ances-
tor in the woods. Take from it its sheltering back-
ground of brick and artificially stimulated soil, and
though for a time it may flourish in a new luxuri-
ance of shoot and leaf, it will certainly fall short,
in quality and quantity, of its golden produce.
• * * *
I am on surer ground, however, when I turn
from theoretical explanation to marshalling of
supporting facts. I do not think it can be doubted
that as classes, country clergymen, army men, and
country gentlemen enjoy a ruder health, and have
a less frequent resort to doctors and drugs than
barristers, journalists, and medical men. The
natural conditions of their lives are more near the
aboriginal environment, and their open-air habits,
with moderate exercise, regular rest, and easy,
regular employment conduce to perfect health. I
do not think it can be doubted that, as classes,
clergymen, army men and country gentlemen are
characterised by brains less active in their higher
intellectual functions than the brains of the less
healthy professional classes. I am speaking, of
course, of average results, as the influence of uni-
form conditions on masses of individuals can ap-
pear only as average results.
* * • •
The case for my proposition becomes even
stronger when pre-eminent brain-work is con-
sidered. I cannot, at the present moment, recall
a single great poet, man of letters or man of
science, in fact, any person greatly distinguished
by the product of his brain, who was a type of
good health. Examples to the contrary surge
into the mind. Most people will remember in
this connection the attempts of Lombroso and
of others to prove a connection between genius
and insanity, or between genius and the stig-
mata of organic degeneration. These attempts
have always seemed to me unscientific and
exaggerated ; but they are at least strong enough
to establish a general connexion between fine
brain-work and ill-health. Consider Newton,
always an invalid; Clerk Maxwell, who died
young after a life of ill-health ; Darwin who, after
he reached adult life, was probably never well for
three consecutive days. Consider Poe and Pope,
Chatterton, Keats, Shelley, Byron, Heine and a
thousand other poets. Consider Gibbon and Car-
lyle, De Quincey—it is needless to prolong the
catalogue : but I would ask readers to think over
the distinguished people they know. It is diffi-
cult to mention the names of living, distinguished
persons, but for my own part I am certain
that I do not know a single person whose
intellect I respect greatly, who enjoys robust
health. The most brilliant soldier, the ablest
politician, the most talented journalist, the greatest
novelist, the most skilful surgeon, the most far-
seeing physician, and the most remarkable man of
science I know, have all, to say the least of it,
something fragile about their bodily constitutions.
The coincidence is most remarkable if the con-
sideration be limited to men under, say, five-and-
fifty. If a man of any ability at all survive that
period, he attains a kind of distinction by the
accretion of years, just as a soldier who survives
many campaigns acquires medals and stages in
military rank. But, among younger people, an
arrangement in the scale of intellectual ability
would certainly show, on the broad average, a
coincidence of brain-work and imperfect health.
' .
Writes Victor Daley—The more I read of recent
literature, the more am I impressed with its
Byzantinism. The Byzantines grew tired of the
stately lonic and Doric and Corinthian columns,
and constructed twisted monstrosities of pillars,
which are architectural dreadfulnesses even to
this day. Yet the intention of the pillar-builders
was, and is, plain enough. They wanted to ex-
press in cold, impassive stone tile corroding Ache
of Life to be more than it is, or can ever be in its
present environment. This futile wrestling with
Omnipotent Fate is always a feature of decadent
ages. Few men in these latter days seem to really
believe that this present life on this particular
small planet is merely an infantile prologue
to the great drama of Man’s everlasting career.
Hectic hurry is the key-note of almost all
later literature. The writers- wish to sing
their song or tell their tale because they know-
or seem to think that they know that
they will soon and most sin 1/ die. There is a
haggard suggestion of anxiety about everything
they produce. We have no patience now. Our
inspired cough must be delivered quickly lest, per-
adventure, we should fade into Infinity without
having given to the world our consumptive mes-
sage. Swinburne’s Meieager expresses the tired,
haggard feeling fairly enough—
“ And now for God's sake kiss me once and twice ,
And let me go, for the night gathers me,
And in the night shall no man gather fruit.”
We have no patience, I say again. With all our
attempted modern revivalism of the Augustan
age we cannot better Sir Philip Francis’s
translation of Horace. We cannot, in fact
adequately translate Horace at all. His “ curiosa
felicitas” is inexpressible in hasty transiterms.
The portly little bard settled on his S ibine farm,
listening—with a cup full of his own wine at his
elbow, and the comfortable knowledge that there
was more of the same vintage not far away—to
the cheerful patter of the warm rain upon his own
grape-vinos, while the great thunder of the Roman
destiny went rolling by : how are his odes to be
fitly expressed in modern forms of speech ?
He took time over those odes. The sosii did not
worry him for “copy ” —or if they did th H fact is
not recorded. And the noble Lucumo, M ecenas
—the sad-eyed, purple-olad magnificent man !
saw to it that tfie little bon vivant was never com-
pelled to sing for his But nowadays
the post is waiting and the world yearn-
ing for our “copy.” And that copy must
go or else we shall awake m the morning and find
ourselves not famous —a tragedy which would
dislocate the Universe! Sometimes I like to re-
member the following facts in this connection :
(1) An absurd old Medici once shut himself up
in a tower for ten years in order to temper a sword
which would cut p >rphyry. And the legend states
that he succeeded. Milton carved in enduring
marble. We have now hardly enough spare time
to make swords that will cut soap.
(2) Thomas Love Peacock stated it as his calm,
carefully-considered opinion that nothing written
since the days of Boethius was worth the Atten-
tion of a Man of Cultured Taste. He did nob
except even his own writings.
(3) The wonderful coral-insect poem—islands
fair and inhabitable springing without hurry,
without pause from the ever-vital womb of In-
numerous Death.
(4) Milton’s line—
“ They also serve who only stand and wait.''
(5) The ancient Irish poets who had to carve
their songs in wood—the casual cynic who says
that their descendants, out of their own heads, do
the same thing to this day is hereby scoffingly an-
ticipated—and if they did not carve them to suit
the humour of the Chief of the Clan got instead of
the usual Usguebaugh Honorarium the unkindly
Axe of the Period. This qualified condition made
them think before they wrote.
(6) Addison, who never would sit down to write
essays if his sleeve-ruffles were nob immaculate.
We write in our shirt-sleeves now.
And finally (as an anti-climax) the old Irish
ballad “ Aisy, O Woman of Three Cows ! ”
“H.S.R.”: There is room for a little more ex-
planation as to the way of building a symbolist poem.
For example. A favorite topic of the symbolists
is the beauty of woman. Woman’s bosom is white
and soft, therefore it must, be described in words;,
which are white to the eye and soft-to the ear, like
blanche and douce. Her lips are red and sweet,
and sweet, red words must picture them—like
rose and rond. And the white and soft,' or sweet
and red, words must be combined and added to
so as to give a general atmosphere of whiteness and
softness, or redness and sweetness; so that the
reader will have a white, soft emotion, or a red,
sweet emotion —that is, so that he may feel as
nearly as possible as if he saw the bosom, or kissed
the lips. Meanwhile, in the poem, the words are
conveying some sort of sense—perhaps telling what
the woman does, or how she looks.. Presently the.
poem is over, and the ordinary reader has assimi-
lated its sense. But all this time, maybe, the poet
has been typifying essential, eternal Beauty in the
guise of a particular woman, and the trained reader
sees the symbol and receives the message. If he
doesn’t see it, he hunts for it—it is the property of
the symbolist poem to unfold like-a flower; and
sometimes he gets it, sometimes he doesn’t get it,
and sometimes he doesn’t know whether he has
got it or not. But he is never happy till he gets
it. This attempt at explanation does not quite
accurately represent Mallarme’s own method, but it
gives a fair general idea of the method of his school.
“J.M.L.”: Henley’s collection of Wilfrid
Blunt’s poems takes no account of “ The Wind and the
Whirlwind,” a booklet which contains Blunt’s verses re-
garding England's attitude to Egypt. The denunciation
in the following lines recalls Francis Adams’s verses in
similar vein—a vein which is not found in Henley, author
of the proud invocation to “ England, my England "
O insolence of strength, O boast of wisdom,
O poverty in all things truly wise !
Thinkest thou, England, .God can be outwitted
For ever thus by him who sells and buys.
Thou sellest the sad nations to their ruin.
What hast thou bought ? The child within the womb,
The son of him thou slayest to thy hurting,
Shall answer thee, “ An empire for thy tomb.”
Thou hast joined house to house for thy perdition.
Thou hast done evil in the name of right.
Thou hast made bitter sweet, and the sw.eet bitter,
And called light darkness, and the darkness light.
Thou hast deserved men’s hatred : they shall hate thee.
Thou hast deserved men’s fear : their fear shall kill.
Thou hast thy foot upon the weak : the weakest
With his bruised head shall strike thee on the heel.
Thou wentest to this Egypt for thy pleasure ;
Thou shalt remain with her for thy sore pain.
Thou hast possessed her beauty. Thou wouldst leave her—
Nay, thou shalt lie with her, as thou ha# liin.
“A. ” : Sydney Public Library catalogues are
simply—or rather most complexly—bewildering to the
visitor seeking library information ; the experienced per-
son resorts to them when he is in a mood to kill time by
dabbling in elaborate puzzles. There is, to begin with, a
general catalogue of the works accumulated from 1869 to
1887, or, to state the case more accurately, an alphabeti-
cal list of the authors of those works. This catalogue is
supplemented by some smaller ones, made up to the same
anciept date, and supposed to be “ subject catalogues,”
though they are not really slifih, as poetry, for in-
stance, published in “collections” of an author’s
works, is not mentioned in the “ poetry ’’ list.
There is no self-advertising indication of how many and
what classes there are, and how many and what “ subject
catalogues” there should be-there are of the latter just
three discoverable, without application to an attendant,
viz .those for A, C, and F. Next, there is a catalogue,
on tbe autbors > names system, of the acquisitions during
1888-1892, and anothei for 1593-95, these being unaccom-
panied by any “ subject catalogues,” so that, in order to
find out what historical works (for example) i here may be
in the library, it is necessary to read, beside °tb er

of j ’ ur i spnldenc . e; poet r V , geography, politics, travels,

acquisitions for 1896-7. (This unexpectedly contains at
the back a welcome re-entry of the 1896-7 books under
classified headings and by titles.) And then theie are

Reckoning these together as one, there are no tew ei man

js in tbe ij brarVi and a ]l of which any have to be eon-

A KINGFISHER.
[FOR THE BLLETIN.]
A doubt, a guess, swaying in sunlit air !
A sudden flash, a shimmering swirl ©f blue ;
Then lightly drops, all sappbire-hued and tense,
A bird ? A gem ? A spirit ?
O wilderment of beauty gleaming there !
A childhood’s aching fantasy comes true—
My dream of flame with sight and flower with
sense
Takes living form and colour !
All fervent moods from Sunlands everywhere,
The tropics of the Heart’s rich ardours too,
Thrill in your miniature magnificence—
Concentrate Austral Summer!
M. A. Robertson.
HYMNE.
A la tres-chere, a la tres-belle
Qui remplit mon cceur de clarte,
A l’ange,a l’idole immortelle,
Salut en immortalite ! \
Elle se repand dans ma vie
Comme un air impregne de sel,
Et dans mon ame inassouvie
Verse le gout de l’Eternel.
Sachet toujours frais qui parfume
L’atmosphere d’un cher reduit,
Encensoir oublie qui fume
En secret k travers la nuit.
Comment, amour incorruptible,
T’exprimer avec verite ?
Grain de muse qui gis, invisible,
Au fond demon eternite !
A la tres-bonne, a la tres-belle
Qui fait ma joie et ma sante,
A l’ange, a l’idole immortelle,
Salut en immortalite!
Ch. Baudelaire.

JUBILEE YEAR.
Provident Society.
With the close of the year 1898, the Australian Mutual Provident Society will complete FIFTY YEARS of its
existence. Members are invited to make this a RECORD YEAR by taking out additional policies themselves, and by
inducing their friends who are not already assured to become members. ALL POLICIES ISSUED before
the end of the year will share in the division of profits to be made as at 31st December next. Amount of Cash Surplus
divided amongst the members during the 49 years ending 1897 was £7,726,170. The Cash Bonus distributed amongst
the Policyholders for the single year 1897 was £115,235. EVERY YEAR A BONUS YEAR.
Accumulated Funds, £11,179,578. Annual Income, £2,091,706.
Directors:— The Hon. Sir J. P. ABBOTT, K.C.M.G., M.L.A., Chairman; THOS. LITTLEJOHN, Esq., Deputy-
Chairman ; A. W. MEEKS, Esq. ; BENJAMIN SHORT, Esq. ; J. T. WALKER. Esq. ; JOSEPH ABBOTT,
Esq.; R. J. BLACK, Esq. General Manager and Actuary: RICHARD TEECE, F.1.A.,F.F.A., F.S.S.

Some Verses and Others.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ed. Bulletin. —Misstated (B. 10/9/’9B)
that Father John Murphy brought out a, consign-
ment of convicts in the Lord Dalhousie in ’52.
Neither lags nor professional devil-dodger on
board that ship that trip, though there were eight
of Sidney Herbert’s boys, in charge only of ship’s
officers, and consigned to influential settlers in
Vic.—J.F.
In the Gardens.
Up in the dome of the drooping myrtle
Small birds were wooing, trilling and twittering;
Now and then one of them catching the sun on
him
Went through the leaves with a sudden gold
glittering.
And standing under there
These the birds’ words to me:
“ What do you ponder there
Under our myrtle tree,
Have you no green leaves, sir,
Standing dejectedly ? ”
Then I beheld her and thrilled to behold her :
She had such pretty feet, pacing so tenderly ;
Was that smile meant for me and that glance sent
for me,
Did she say hope to me even so slenderly ?
And looking after her
Her eyes came round again,
Mischief and laughter there—■
Turned on the ground again:
“ Have you no daring, sir,
Gazing dejectedly ? ”
White as the white rose and red as red roses,
Under green arches where blossoms hung
swoonfully;
Bright as a gleam of gold, sweet as a dream of old,
She took her way, and the waters sang tunefully.
Then, at her passing slow,
I in a sweet distress
Heard the bees humming low
Deep in the leafiness :
“ Seek you not honey, sir,
Thirsting dejectedly ? ”
Yes—l will speak to her—here by the poppies :
“ Sweetheart, what moves you to such gentle
pondering ? ”
Alas, the surprise to me! she turned her eyes from
me,
Left me all hot and cold, tremulous, wondering !
Then said the poppies red :
“ So—it is better so;
Forget! ” the red poppies said,
“ Sleep and forget her so.
Love is a torture, sir,
Musing dejectedly! ”
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Winter.
The season of sun is over,
Is over, ended, and better so.
I could not see my star-way
So blinding bright was the summer’s glow.
The season of song is over,
Has ceased, is silent, and better so.
Your voice outrang my heart-beats ;
Oh, voice, immortally sweet and slow !
The season of spring is over,
Is browned and withered with all the rest.
And now I look to winter —
The years are long since I loved her best.
Her silences hear my heart beat ;
I listen weary, and learn to know.
Her cold and dark are warders
That guard the way I alone must go.
N.S.W.
LOUISE MAOK.
In Memoriam.
Death took the husk; but Love has stored the
essence,
The fragrance of her being, in the shrine,
And in my heart her worship never lessens.
Death took the shape ; but Memory is mine.
Like yonder vase—l open it and scatter
A thousand peerless roses through the air ;
Their perfumed soul is here ; what does it matter
If the wilt petals blew I know not where ?
Q.
VON KOTZE.
I Told Her True.
I whispered to her that I wanted her:
Methinks I told her true ?
Had I not felt the very blood of Love
Thrill through my being as I made pretence
To stay the wind-tossed robe enfolding her ?
Quick came the incense from her glorious hair :
The sweet odorament she breathed for me :
The kiss, that I might drink deep from her
mouth :
The rich expectancy, full well I knew
Would hold*me quivering on the brink of bliss :
The touch of flesh and flesh. That rare amaze !
The awesome look from heart to answering heart;
And then the trustful love-glow, stealing through
The gentler welling of her glorious eye.
Had I not tasted of my Beautiful,
And left my life for ever at her feet!
And so I told her that I wanted her.
My God, I told her true !
N.S.W.
LESLIE OAKES.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 12, 1898.
Sneaking in Protection.
Last Wednesday New South Wales Premier
and Treasurer Reid delivered a memorable
financial statement. It contained two notable
admissions. One was that New South
Wales, after all the weary brag about the
bogus Reid surplus, can’t pay its way with-
out more taxation. The other was that the
Reid Government doesn’t care a straw about
Freetrade after all; and so the old semi-
Protective, semi-Revenue-Tariff system of
the old Parkes days is to be restored. The
duty of £3 per ton on sugar is to be
maintained, and the duties on biscuits,
jam, confectionery, and fruit are to be put
on again, and revenue duties are to be levied
on tea, coffee, chocolate, cocoa, and rice.
The sugar, biscuit, jam, and confectionery
duties are strictly protective in their char-
acter, and the beginning, middle and end of
the Reid doctrine is that Protection is
utterly horrible, and that it has a mission
from the country to put down that hydra-
headed monster, and it lives and gets
its screw by that profession. The tea,
coffee, and cocoa duties will simply in-
crease the price of these articles—when
duties are levied on articles which can’t be
locally produced there is no local output to
compete with the imported goods, and the
natural result is an increase of price. There-
fore, the much-boomed “load” that was
“taken off the backs of the people ” is being
put on again ; and the free breakfast-table
has gone broke on all four legs. Also the
Government, whose whole life has been one
long boast about how the burden was taken
off the poor and put on the rich, is found
taxing the necessaries of the poor the very
first time it is hard pressed for cash, when it
might just as easily levy import-duties on
luxuries and let mere necessaries go free.
Last year this same Government attempted
to fill the gap in kits finances by direct taxa-
tion, mainly on the rich, in the shape of in-
creased probate-duties, and when the Council
threw r out its proposals its promises that it
would reform that body off the face of the
earth if necessary were something tremen-
dous. This year it makes no attempt to fill
the void by renewing its probate-duty scheme.
Ihe Wriggler Government drops direct taxa-
tion, and its policy of putting the burdens on
the rich, and its Council reform programme,
all in one act, and the Nominee House is
left master of the situation.
* » * *
For the financial year ended 30th June,
1898, the Reid Ministry, it now appears
from its own statements, had a deficit after
all; the previous report that it had at last got
its accounts to balance being merely the usual
cram. The year’s revenue was £9,489,718,
and the expenditure £9.507,202 ; shortage,
£17,484. Next year, despite the levying
of £176,000 new customs taxation, Mr.
Reid anticipates another deficit : Revenue,
£9,609,004 ; expenditure, £9,681,421 ; short-
age, £72,417. Yet the brassy political be-
guiler talks about his surplus ”as usual,
ihe following is the revenue and expendi-
ture account of the Reid Government since
it presented its first set of financial pro-
posals at the beginning of 1895. The figures
for 1895-6-7 are taken from the latest issue
of Coghlan s official “ Wealth and Progress
of New South Wales,” pages 861 and 862.
The figures for 1898 and the estimates for
1899 are from the commercial column of
Premier Rkid’s own organ, Sydney Daily
Telegraph, November 3 :
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit.
1895 £4,478,515 £4,844,299 £365 784
year ’
9,074,778 9,523,057 448,279
1897 9,107,496 9,140,625 33 199
1898 9,489,718 9,507,202 17,484
£32,150,507 £33,015,18? £864,676
estimate. ’
1899 9,609,004 9,681,421 72,417
£41,759,511 £42,696,604 £937,093
During this same period Premier Reid has
paid £600,000 off the old deficit (said
£600,000 being included among the expendi-
ture), so he has gone to the bad, according
to official figures and his own estimates,
£337,093.
* * * *
. According to a table published in the last
issue of Coghlan’s “ Wealth and Progress
of New South Wales,” page 860, the New
South Wales accumulated deficit, at 30th
June, 1897, after Premier Reid had been put-
ting the finances straight for 2| years, was
larger than it ever was before in the history of
the province. At 30th June, 1897, there were
five accumulated deficits among the Seven
Provinces, and during the two years ending
at that date that of New South Wales was
the only one which showed an increase.
Premier Reid alleged in his financial state-
ment that he had paid off £1,100,000 14 with-
out creating a fresh deficiency of any kind,”
which, from the man who swelled the accu-
mulated shortage to the highest figure it
ever attained, is especially brilliant. He
also sets down his present difficulties to the
drought and alleges that New South Wales
has lost, in the four dry years, 293,000
horses, 1,500,000 cattle and 50,000,000 sheep.
Four years ago there were only 57,000,000
sheep in New South Wales altogether, and,
according to Coghlan, they have since de-
creased by nine millions—not fifty millions,
but that discrepancy is a mere drop in the
vast ocean of Reid’s capacity for saying
the thing that isn’t.
* » * *
Last year New South Wales spent
£1,707,792 of borrowed money—the biggest
record since 1893 ; and for the first "four
months of the present financial year the
expenditure has been at the rate of
£1,900,000 a year. But it isn’t the
amount of expenditure, bad as that is, so
much as the way the money is expended that
shows the incapacity of the Reid Govern-
ment’s finance. That Government has put
up a record, such as none of its predecessors
ever approached, for charging ordinary daily
expenditure to loans—expenditure for works
that are neither permanent nor productive,
and for things which are not works at all.
Up to 1894, the year when the Reid Govern-
ment took office, New South Wales had
wrongfully spent and misapplied £8,255,000
of its borrowed moneys on unproductive
works which should have been charged to
revenue. This was the result of 40 years’
bad finance, and represented 15 per cent, of
the total loans used in making bogus sur-
pluses. At 30th June, 1898, the amount
thus misapplied was £10,199,909, or 17 per
cent, of the whole. During the four years
in question the amounts of loan-money spent
otherwise than on permanent and unproduc-
tive works by the Australian provinces was :
New South Wales ... ... ...£1,944,478
Victoria ... ... ... ’ y’igs
Queensland... ... 77e’761
South Australia ... ... ... 922’081
Westralia ... ... ... ..." i,614pi8
Tasmania ... ... ... ... ’336’360
This means that, in the last four years, New
South Wales has misapplied more of its loan-
moneys, to make its accounts look square,
than any other province, and more than
Queensland, South Australia, and Victoria
put together. Also, it has been misapplying
its loan-moneys on a larger scale than at any
previous period in its history.
* * ♦ *
And in the same period, New South Wales
borrowed more money than any other pro-
vince, spendthrift Westralia not excepted,
though it beat that remarkable locality only
by a hair’s-breadth. The additions made to
the public debts of the Australian provinces
between 1894 and 1898 were :
New South Wales ... ... ...£6,083,741
Victoria ... ... ... ••• 1,719,149
Queensland... ... ... ... 2,733,638
South Australia ... ... ... 2,084,532
Westralia ... ... ... ... 6,056,663
Tasmania ... ... • ... ••• 383,883
* » » *
Of coarse, bad as these figures are, they
might be a great deal worse. Australian
finance is so utterly rotten and thriftless
that to be a little worse than any other con-
temporary financier is only to be a trifle
blacker than the pot, after all. But George
Reid has posed as the one strictly honest
and square and capable financier of New
South Wales. He has heaped the brutal
scorn of a deafening and blatant fat man on
predecessors whose monetary doings were
vastly wiser than his own. He has been a
loud scoffer and a dull jester and a sicken-
ing braggart all through his career. Partly
because of his brag and partly because he is
at the head of the allegedly richest province
—the one that can best afford to go straight
—in Australia, the country expected some-
thing better than usual from him. Yet it
appears, by the official State statistics of his
own province, and from the figures of his
own official organ, that his surplus has been
a sham all along, and that there has been
instead a constant deficit. Also, that his
Freetrade and direct taxation are a sham ;
at the very first serious pressure he abandons
the policy which he says makes the country
rich, and looks for aid to a policy which, he
says, makes it poor. His loud threats of sup-
pressing the insolence of the nominee Coun-
cil are another sham, for he peacefully
knuckles-under to its rejection of his pro-
bate-duties scheme ; and his policy of re-
moving the burden off the backs of the poor
is bogus, for all his new revenue duties are
put, not on luxuries, but on necessaries.
And the New South Wales official statistics
show him as the hardest borrower in Aus-
tralia, and as the financier who diverted a
larger proportion of his borrowings to pro-
duce a bogus aspect of prosperity in the
revenue than any of his neighbors, or any
of his own predecessors. Beyond these and
a few other matters, and his slanders
upon the Treasurers who preceded him, there
isn't so very much to complain of in a
financial sense, after all.
What Next?
Now that the Reid Government has got its
Federal resolutions through N.S.W. Assembly
with great labor and excitement, it begins to be
about time to inquire what is to happen next.
For the present Federal situation is mainly a blur
and a smudge, and the future situation is a mys-
tery. At present the Assembly is engaged dis-
cussing the proposed amendments of private
members, but these are not likely to make much
difference in the result. Then the resolutions have
to run the gauntlet of the Council, and, supposing
that body does any serious damage to them, it
isn’t very clear what will happen. The Govern-
ment has never explained whether it will fight the
unreformed Council, if need be, to get its resolu-
tions carried in their entirety, or whether it gives
its avowed provincialist, Want, and his col-
leagues a free hand in the matter. But the
amendments, as passed by the Assembly, being all
saturated with the narrowest provincial spirit, the
probability is that the Council will accept them as
they stand, merely regretting that they are not
worse. And when they have got through both
Houses—what is to happen next ? On that subject
there is, so far, no information to hand.
* * m *
The first point is to confer, in some way or other,
with the other provinces. Is it proposed to ask
these other provinces to hold another formal con-
ference, and, if so, is it intended that that conference
is to be composed, like the last one, of delegates
elected by the people ? Or is it proposed that the
people shall have no voice in the matter, and that
Parliament shall appoint the delegates, and, if so,
is the appointment to be made by the Assembly-
only, or by both Houses conjointly ? How many
delegates are there to be, and are they to be simply
nominated by the Reid Government, probably from
among the bitter provincialists of its party, and
then appointed by the dead weight of the party
majority ? Or is it proposed to leave Parliament
out of the business as well as the people, and for
the Reid Ministry to simply nominate itself, or
part of itself—perhaps Jack Want and Sunday
Brunker and Potato Young —to run the show?
And, in that case, how will it look, on democratic
principles, when the men who have no direct
authority from the people proceed to undo the
work of the men who had such direct authority,
and whose work was accepted by the popular vote
—the vote which Parliament cancelled when it
altered the minimum on which the business started
and broke the compact on which the people went
into, the movement ?
* * * *
Three Premiers, anyhow— Turner, Braddon,
and Kingston —hardly dare consider any serious
alterations in the original Constitution except in a
popularly-elected conference. At least, they cer-
tainly dare not promise, on their own responsi-
bility, to throw their weight and that of their
Governments into any movement for largely
altering the original compact. They no more
dare do so than Premier Reid dare introduce
a protective tariff. In each of their cases the
people signified by a very strong majority their
acceptance of the constitution framed by their
elected representatives, and the Premiers who
attempt to reverse the decision of their own
majorities to please the minority in the next pro-
vince—especially the Jack Want minority which
studiously insulted them and called them wolves
and such things—is likely to get into trouble.
Supposing the other Premiers offer to have new
delegates elected to consider the proposed amend-
ments, and refuse to deal with them in any other
way, what will be Reid’s attitude? And sup-
posing they ask Reid to send along his crowd of
popularly-elected delegates on the ground that
their dealings so far have been with the people of
N.S.W.—not merely with the Free trade party
in Parliament—and that they want to know, per
medium of a popular vote, whether the people of
N.S.W. are, or are not, in favor of the new pro-
posals before they consider them what will
happen? Seeing that the N.S.W. majority ac-
cepted the old proposals, the other provinces
might reasonably ask for some proof that that
majority has changed its mind, and if it sent along
delegates who supported the new proposals that
would be proof. Otherwise, the other provinces
have no evidence that N.S.W. is in earnest about
these new suggestions, or that it doesn’t really
desire something very different.
• * • •
Supposing a conference of some kind is held,
and an amended Constitution of some kind is
drafted, will Premier Reid submit it to the people
in any case, and let the people decide ? Or will
he simply submit it to the people if it
suits him; and, if it doesn’t suit him, will
he refuse to let the people say whether it
suits them or not? There is nothing definite on
any of these points. Last time Parliament started
with a clear agreement (which it afterwards broke),
about how the delegates to the Federal Conference
were to be appointed, and how their work was to
be submitted to the people. This time they may
be appointed anyhow, and their work may be sub-
mitted to the people anyhow, or not submitted at
all, just as the Reid and Jack Want and Potato
Young party pleases. How does the Reid
Government propose to fake the ballot this time ?
Last time Reid found himself overshadowed by
Barton, so a Bill was allowed to pass through the
Assembly (the Reid Government refusing to use
its influence against it), declaring that if 79,999
people voted for the Barton constitution and only
one against it, it should be lost. There was
a majority for the Barton constitution,
and the Reid Government refused to recognise it.
This time the same gang of political adventurers
may, if the new Draft Constitution doesn’t suit
it or its private interests, fake the ballot
again by enacting that if there are 119,999
votes for it, and only one against it,
it shall be lost. Or it may force an objectionable
Bill on the people by enacting its acceptance per
medium of its party majority in Parliament, and
have no referendum at all ? It studiously avoids
pledging itself to any course, so how does the
Reid-Want Government propose to fake the
ballot this time ?
* * » *
A subordinate member of the Reid Government
has promised that if there is any kind of confer-
ence, and any new agreement, and that agreement
is submitted to the people, it will be done squarely
and honestly, and that the majority shall decide.
The boss member of the Reid Government makes
no definite promises. Last time when the agree-
ment for a 50,000 minimum was broken by a gang
of disgraceful political Iscariots, another subor-
dinate member of the Reid Government offered
the very thin explanation that the Ministry
couldn’t rally up the party to prevent this
public repudiation of a solemn pact. He said
that the Bill embodying the proposed fraud
was the work of a private member, and for
the Government to interfere and make it
a party question would be an impossible out-
rage on the rights of private members. Which
of these Ministers is a liar, or if neither of them is
one how do the two contradictory tales hang
together ? If the one who says there will be no
minimum next time, whatever rights of private
members may come in the way, told the truth,
then the Government could have interfered to
prevent the breach of faith last time, and didn’t;
and, having wrecked Federation by a wilful breach
of faith last time, it may do it again. If the other
fellow told the truth, then the Reid Government
can’t prevent the imposition of some impossible
minimum next time, any more than it could last
time, and the Minister who promises that this
time the majority shall be allowed to decide is
promising what he can’t perform. And supposing
a new Draft Constitution is arrived at, and the
people of N.S. .Wales are allowed by the per-
mission of the Reid Government to vote on
it, and they formally accept it, what is to
happen then? Supposing the gang in office
doesn’t like the Bill though the people do (which
was exactly the condition at last poll on June 3),
will the Reid Government proceed to do its utmost
to make the Bill law ? Or will it simply drop it
under the table and take no notice of the popular
majority—as it did last time.
* * * *
All these are urgent questions. The Reid Go-
vernment is carefully reserving to itself power to
fake the new Federal movement in so many ways
that its conduct is full of suspicion. It doesn’t
pledge itself to give the people a voice in the elec-
tion of the delegates who are to draft the new
Bill; or to submit that new Bill, when it is drafted,
to the people at all; or to accept the people’s de-
cision on it if it is submitted. It can approach
the other provinces in any way it pleases. It can
make Federation impossible by sending, as the
N.S.W. delegates, a crowd who don’t want
Federation under any circumstances at all, and
who will break up all negotiation by making
impossible demands; or it can make it possi-
ble by sending some other kind of crowd.
And supposing it does go straight, which it has
never done yet, and puts its new Bill before the
people with no minimum, and it is accepted by (say)
40,000 against 36,000 —what then? Will the Bill
which gets a 4000 majority be accepted while the
one which got over 5000 majority wasn’t ? That is
how the law will stand anyhow, but what does the
Reid Government think of itself when it makes
laws under which such things are possible ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Under the Crown.
The framers of the Convention Bill, by insert-
ing in the first lines of our national charter the
words “ under the crown,” have piously con-
templated, and, as far as lay in their power,
have endeavoured to perpetuate the good old
slavish bond between Australia and that noble
dynasty to which we owe so much. The
debates thereon were an anguished competition
in loyal slobber. The happiest delegate was he
who could most fervently express his humble
desire that we might ever be permitted to
abase ourselves before the Throne. In Britain
everything happened for the best in the best of
all possible nations. Might we ever be British,
and, if possible, more British than the British
themselves ! Might we be sheltered through
the ages by Britain’s motherly arms from rude
contact with the demoralising touch of the
unregenerate foreigner ! In the infinite wisdom,
goodness and mercy of Britain could we alone
find salvation ; apart from her the ravening
maw of the wicked threatened us with per-
dition, ruin, and death. However much it may
be desirable in the present condition of affairs
to maintain our present arrangements with
Britain from motives of expediency and con-
venience, the pusillanimous timidity which
regards the British connection as vital to our
own nationality is not only ignorant of the
meaning of national life, but is false to the
very spirit of nationality. National manhood
is in its essence a growth of independence,
not a result of tutelage. A people nursed
on pap and led by apron strings never can
become a nation, but only a helpless hobble-
dehoy. We may gain all the wealth of


Ormuz—of Ind, but unless we win at the same
time by sturdy self-reliance, by steadfast loyalty
to our own self-realisation, an indepen-
dent niche in the comity of the world’s
counsels, we are pampered children ; and
apart from these general reflections we must
recognise that the hand of nature has marked
us for a destiny as far removed as the poles from
the civilization of Britain. We, Australians,
are not the phlegmatic inhalers of a humid
fog. The titanic conquest of our arid interior,
the genial influence of our sunny skies, are
calling for, and are at the same time creating a
racial type which is sui generis. Perhaps, in
years to come, we shall be less like our Teutonic
forefathers than to that ancient people whose
climate and surroundings were similar to our
own—that mysterious race which built the
pyramids and taught civilization to Europe.
Certainly our social organism, our intellectual
and artistic creations are bound to depart enor-
mously from the traditions and standards of
Britain. To recognise this is not to fall into
the ravings of anarchistic treason. To ignore
it, to attempt to stifle our individuality by a
servile submission to British ideals, is the worst
treachery to our national destiny.

The Maoriland Old-Age Pensions Law.
The first Old-Age Pension system in the Aus-
tralias will be fairly afloat in Maoriland next
year. It has been established by ten years’
hard toil and agitation. The country has been
solemnly warned that the principle is ruinous ;
that it will destroy the beautiful virtues of
thrift and self-reliance—as if the spectacle of a
whole nation making provision with its own
money for its old age isn’t the most unanimous
case of thrift and self-reliance on record ; also,
that it shows a Godless spirit, and a lack of
faith in Providence, and a regrettable looseness
in the old belief in the damnation of unbaptised
infants, or something equally irrelevant. The
subject was approached with intense caution
and with so many limitations that only the
most ancient and godly individual will be able
to crowd through. He (or she) must be 65
years old or more, and have resided in Maori-
land for the previous 25 years, with not over
two years’ absence during that period. No
Chinese or imported nigger need apply under
any conditions. No foreigner need apply un-
less, in addition to his other qualifications,
he has been five years a naturalised citizen.
If the aged citizen has been imprisoned four
times, or four months in all, during the last
12 years of his life, he needn’t apply. If he
has been imprisoned for as much as five years
in the last quarter-century of his life he
needn’t apply. All he can do is to wait till
these misdeeds get far enough in the back-
ground of his biography to make him eligible.
A wife who has deserted her husband or a hus-
band who has deserted his wife,- without just
cause, for a period of six- months, or neglected,
without good cause, to maintain his children,
is merely wasting good stationery and ink by
applying. Also, the applicant must have
been sober and moral and not addicted to
the pursuit of the guileful Flossie for the last
five years—but then most people are more or
less moral after 60. No person with more than
.£320 worth of property, or with an income of
£52 a year or more, is in the scheme anywhere.
The full pension is £lB a year, and anybody
with an income ranging from nothing to £34
per annum is eligible to receive the whole
amount. Any qualified person with an income
over £34 a year will receive enough to raise
that income to £52. The Act expires after the
second session of next Parliament, so Maori-
land can then revise it and make what-
ever amendments seem necessary. There is
such intense caution in every line of the scheme
that even in Maoriland, which is painfully sober
and moral, and where people live to the age of
the legendary patriarchs of Genesis, it is ex-
pected that the cost will amount to not more
than £90,000 or £lOO,OOO a year. Three years
hence it will probably be decided that the plan
requires amendment in two directions. It will
have to be enlarged so that the applicant who
is a little lower than the angels can struggle
through the narrow orifice of inspection ; if the
individual who once ran after a girl at the age
of 60 is shut out too rigidly, the Old Age Pen-
sion Act will leave a very large residuum of
pauperism after all. And it will some day be
found absolutely necessary to devise some
sources of revenue which can be devoted ex-
clusively to maintaining the Pension Fund, so
that it won’t be left dependent on the unre-
liable surplus. But with all limitations there
remains the great fact that Maoriland has got
the first beginning of its Old Age Pension
scheme in working order.

The Man Who Refuses to be Drowned.
The Bulletin has been considering the case of
the American ship Republic, which, the other
day, made an attempt to take about 1500 tons
of coal from Newcastle (N.S. W.) to Honolulu,
and, after travelling 300 miles, had to return—
leaky—to the port of departure. After being
“ repaired,” she made a second start, got about
the same distance, or rather less, this time, and
put in to Sydney, again leaky. No unusual
weather struck the newly-repaired ship this
time—only “ a nasty cross sea, the result, the
captain thinks, of a heavy blow to the north-
ward.” And she was making water to the ex-
tent of fully one foot per hour—equal to eight
yards of water in a day and a night. At
this rate it would certainly appear that
only constant pumping could keep the
newly-repaired ship afloat, and there was
a long voyage ahead, with chances of very
much worse weather, in the course of which
the ship might possibly take in three feet
of water per hour—there is no knowing. So
the crew insisted on putting back, and the
newly-repaired wooden ship, Republic, 29 years
of age, and lately bought at an S.A. port for
some £4OO or £5OO, made her second sudden
retreat to port. The captain was of opinion
that “ the men were a bit scared because the
ship had previously put back to Newcastle,” so
it appears that under ordinary circumstances
sailors are not expected to object to a vessel
which leaks at the rate of one foot of water per
hour. Of course this sort of thing doesn’t help the
N.S. W. coal-export industry along. And if these
miserable nautical wretches actually presume to
put their paltry lives in the scale against
“ rapid industrial progress,” and “ free de-
velopment of trade,” and “great commercial
interests,” and other “very fine and large”
matters of that sort, The Bulletin really
doesn’t know what is to be done, unless the
Marine Board or somebody likes to wake up
and see that the middle-aged ship goes to sea
a little more like a ship and a little less like a
sieve. It was lately recorded that the Marion
Inglis (barque), bought for £5500, had returned
30 per cent, on her year’s trading. Then, what
profit might have been expected from a year’s
work of the Republic, of nearly equal tonnage,
and having cost less than a tenth of the money?
But these malevolent mariners apparently cared
nothing for that; any more than they cared for
the fact that their indecent and ridiculous
solicitude for their personal safety will, prob-
ably, involve discharge of the ship’s cargo for
further “ repairs.” Plainly, something will
have to be done. Either the mariner who
objects to being drowned must be Suppressed
by law—or else the Newcastle coal-ship must
be overhauled in a new way and repaired in
quite a different fashion.

Where the Majority Comes In.
In discussing the various ways of amending the
Federal Constitution Act, should that measure
require amendment, N.S.YV. Parliament of
1898 behaves very much like the Leg. Council,
which, nearly 50 years ago, prepared and passed
the Constitution Act of N.S. Wales. Went-
worth stood out boldly for making the Consti-
tution Act as difficult of amendment as possible.
Deas Thomson, who had a mathematical mind,
delighted in making the amendment subject to
majorities of three-fourths or seven-eighths of
the members of both Houses. Others aired
crotchets of different kinds. Finally, apparently
for want of talent to think out further crooked
ways, the Bill passed, providing that a majority
of two-thirds of both Houses should be neces-
sary to sanction a change in the Constitution.
When the Bill went to England, the Imperial
Parliament quietly changed the two-thirds to a
bare majority, and so passed it. For a time,
all sorts of evil was prophesied. This is the
period of Wentworth’s life which justified the
late Parkes in always denouncing him as a
Tory. But the mathematically-minded Parlia-
mentarians were as bitterly disappointed at the
rejection of their arithmetical adjustments as
Wentworth himself. Yet the Constitution
Act, with its “ bare majority of both
Houses,” triumphed over all, and has satis-
factorily served its purpose till the present day.
And all the other provinces travel safely
enough on the same principle. In 1898, the
Federal Convention decided that to amend the
Commonwealth Constitution there must be a
majority of both Houses, a majority of the
people, and a majority of the States, which
isn’t so bad as the old Wentworth idea,
though it is difficult enough. But the N.S. W.
Assembly is busy with suggestions for chaining
up the Constitution and mooring it securely to
the biggest rock it can find. McGowen’s pro-
posal, just accepted, suggests that any amend-
ment which doesn’t enlarge the powers of the
Commonwealth may be carried by a bare
majority. But any one which does enlarge the
Commonwealth’s powers needs the assent
of a majority in both Federal Houses and
the assent of every province—if all the
provinces except Tasmania decide that it
is absolutely essential to enlarge the Federal
authority in any given direction, and a majority
of one in Tasmania says no, that one lone, lorn
vote down in Van Dobson Land carries the
day. This is proposed “to protect the sacred
interests of N.S.W.” But N.S.W. will some
day be more anxious to enlarge the Federal
power than any other province, because N.S. W.
will hold a bigger share of it than any other
province. And when it finds a small majority
in the smallest of the States reversing the de-
cision of all the rest of the Commonwealth,
then it will make unpleasant remarks about
M'Gowen and co.
The Vic. Nigger Importation Bill.
Victoria is still waiting to learn what kind of
fight the Turner Government is going to make
over the Council’s amendments on the Immi-
gration Restriction Bill. As the Assembly
passed the measure it was a genuine restriction
law with an educational test, after the Natal
pattern, to keep out the coffee-colored illiterate.
Then the Council proceeded to make exceptions.
It provided that any ship may come to Victoria
with a nigger crew, and discharge them in a Vic-
torian port, and leave them there without ques-
tion. No tonnage-limit was provided; and the
amount of nigger alleged crew which any ship
may drop ashore is unlimited. Also, the Coun-
cil enacted that any employer may import five
niggers every year on giving his bond for £2OO
that each consignment will be sent back
at the expiry of five years. The only
requisite for an importation of 120,000
niggers a year is the establishment of an
agency to look after details at the Vic.
end, and to produce, with each five, some
unemployed or other who will (for ss. or so)
swear that he is an employer and sign the
necessary bond. Of course, the niggers
wouldn’t be returned at the end of five years if
they didn’t find it convenient to go, for no one
could find and identify them; and the “em-
ployer” would also be missing; and the £2OO
mentioned in the bond would be nowhere.
The Vic. Leg. Council transferred the Nigger
Restriction Bill into a scheme for the wholesale
importation of niggers, and the feeble, mild-
mannered Cuthbert, who represents the Turner
Governmental the Council, didn’t even kick
once against the insult. He never does kick
hard enough to remove a fly off an envelope
against the Council’s mutilation of any demo-
cratic measure. And there is no person in the
Council, except Melville, who is willing to
kick in the democratic cause any harder than
Cuthbert does.
The Tarrawing'ee Tram.
If the N.S.W. Government buys the little
Tarruwingee railway (Broken Hill) it is esti*
mated that the annual loss will be .£1720.
That is on the assumption that the Silver-
ton railway company is paid to work the
line. The Silverton co. offers to work it at
7s. 6d. per train mile for ordinary and 15s. per
train mile for special traffic, not including cost
of repairs, renewals, &c. On the N.S. W.
Govt, lines the cost of working (including
repairs and renewals) is 3s. per train
mile ; Victoria, 3s. sd. ; Queensland, 2s. lOd. ;
S.A., 3s. sd. ; Tasmania, 3s. 4d. According to
the estimates supplied to Parliament, the
Tarrawingee line would be a solvent specu-
lation if the traffic was worked at the ordi-
nary N.S.W. rates. The trouble is that it
joins on to the Silverton private line, and
the Silverton line joins on to the S.A.
system, and the N.S.W. system doesn’t touch
either of them. If the N.S.W. Govern-
ment would resume the Silverton line at
the earliest possible date it could run the
combined railways at a profit and yet charge
the local residents much lower fares and
freights than they pay at present, for the rates
of the Victorian co., which now runs the busi-
ness, are hideously exorbitant, and it grinds 47
per cent, dividends out of the unfortunate
people of the silver city. To buy the Tarra-
wingee line with a view' to resuming the
Silverton line also, and making them the be-
ginnings of a Government railway system in
the West, would be good business. To buy it
with a view to leaving the Silverton line per-
manently in the hands of a private syndicate
would be a dead loss. But then there is no
earthly reason why the Silverton line should be
left in the hands of its syndicate one day longer
than is absolutely necessary.

Premier Reid on the Suffield case :
How can we, who were not there, who did not see the
Warrens, Suffield, or this younsr woman in the box
under cross-examination, say that the jury was wrong ?
.... I was not there, and no man can form a true esti-
mate of a case unless he is present.
And on that same logic nobody now living can
say that Judge Jeffreys didn’t conduct all his
cases in a spirit of absolute fairness. Nobody
can say that there was anything unjust about
the methods of the Spanish Inquisition. And
when the Red Indian tried his casual prisoner
in the woods and sentenced him to be scalped
and burned, it is impossible to say that his
method of conducting the case wasn’t wholly
impartial. And why has the Christian world
been objecting all these 1866 years to the
methods of a certain trial which took place in
Jerusalem very long ago? It is impossible to
say there was anything unfair about any of
these legal proceedings. We weren’t there.

Several Vic. Leg. Councillors were much
shocked when the second reading of the Crema-
tion Bill came on. Johnny Wallace “could not
see any reason for wasting the time of the
House over the matter. If a man wanted to get
roasted he could get roasted.” Nick Fitzgerald
“ regarded the Bill as an invasion of Christian
ideas,” which was altogether irrelevant, as
Christ said nothing for or against cremation.
Later, the brewer-man grew very earnest on
the subject, in that his real God —Property—was
attacked. “ Property in the neighborhood of
crematoria in England had greatly decreased in
value.” “ The necessary plant should be estab-
lished at some place where Property could not
be damaged.” “Property in the neighborhood
of cemeteries should not be allowed to be
damaged by the suspicion that noxious gases
might be precipitated at times.” Not a word
about the lives sacrificed and disease and suffer-
ing caused by the “noxious gases” now
exhaled by hundreds of cemeteries all over the
land, or of the germs carried away and spread
at large by drainage-water from most of these
burying-grounds, and especially from that cess-
pit of corruption—Melb. Gen. Cemetery.
And, coming back to “ noxious gases,” when
did the House of bricks-and-mortar ever raise
its voice against the fatal gases generated in
certain trades —smelting, silver-mining, coal-
mining, chemical works, lead works, and so on ?
But in these matters it is only the lives of
workers that are “ damaged.”

“Bush Notion” on a N.S.W. State school-
teacher’s grievance :
Dear Bulletin. —An example of the mangy little
nippings and clippings by which N.S.W. Public Service
Board is striving to fake up a surplus for Treasurer Reid.
Hitherto a married public school teacher, on being
appointed, say, to an Bth class school, has been paid £l4B
plus £3O rent allowance, and if there was no vested
residence the department paid him his £3O and let him
rent any premises he liked. Now, however, the P.S.B.
has ukased that where no residence is provided by
Govt., only the rent actually paid and shown in
monthly vouchers by the landlord’s receipt, will be
paid. Thus, if I have to camp in a barn and pay
Is. a week for the luxury, my salary, fixed by the Educa-
tion Act at £l7B, becomes £l4B plus 525., or £l5O 12s.
And if, for the joy of independence, I sleep under a wattle
and eat under a yellow-butt, I receive £l4B plus nothing,
as I have no landlord’s receipt to show that I have lived
at all. On the other hand, if on taking charge I find an
official humpy which the Dept, is pleased to style a
“ vested residence,” £3O is deducted from my salary as
rent; and if I apply for re-assessment on ground that the
crib is in wretched condition, the Board may actually
sanction the construction of an additional “room,”
Bxlo, or thereabouts. But I must pay every cent of the
£3O, in order to help along that surplus, although where
there is no “ vested residence ” I won’t get the £3O.
Logically and morally, if the Board has the right to re-
duce a teacher’s rent-salary, it can reduce or regulate his
whole salary in proportion to his circumstances ; and it
will not be surprising if Board’s next promulgation should
read :—l. Do you consume animal food or vegetable ? 2.
State actual cost (1) of meat?—showing butcher’s receipt;
(2) of vegetables, for the year?—showing greengrocer’s re-
ceipt. 3. Do you pay for fuel, or is it possible in the
locality to pick up chips, bark, &c. ? 4. If you have a
family, state names and ages, and if the youngest (if in
infancy) is being reared naturally or artificially ? 5. Does
your mother-in-law live with you? 6. Do you smoke,
chew or drink ? 7. Have you a dress-suit, and are you an
Oddfellow? &c., &c. Also, if the teacher’s salary stand
at £l5O, and his expenses reach £149 19s. llfd., it is only
natural to assume that the Board, in the interest of the
taxpayers, and for the expansion of that surplus, will
dock the farthing and maintain its reputation for snavel.
“ The Coolgardie protest against filling up dis-
used workings with cyanide tailings has a good
foundation, and the cyanide question in general
has a variety of interesting aspects from a
pathological point of view,” writes a medical
man to The Bulletin. “ The action of the
cyanides on the human organism is by no
means a settled question, and among the
workers in big laboratories queer tales are
occasionally told. In 1890 the cyanide question
began to attract attention, and in a big
London institutean expert was set to work on the
entire group of 0 W compounds. The mercurous
end proved especially interesting and he put in
a couple of months with them. Finding his
health shaky, he took a holiday, and at the
end of three months died from rapid and
general softening of the brain. His locum tenens,
a powerful man and a noted athlete, pegged
away merrily at the work in the interval, and
on the death of his predecessor secured the
billet. Four months more broke him up, and
at the end of a year he died paralysed and in-
sane. Result of p.m., general degeneration of
the cerebral cortex with no adequate cause.
Meanwhile the laboratory-assistant had been
doing a little ‘on his own,’ and, feeling ill, re-
membered his former chief, and made his will.
He is an interesting case of paralytic dementia
now. The authorities at the laboratory shut
down on cyanides-of-mercury then and there.
These men had all the advantages of fume-
shoots, draught-cupboards, and other conveni-
ences of a big laboratory. All three were
practically experts. Coincidence will scarcely
explain it, and weirder stories are hinted at
among chemists. Will some worker step up
and give his experiences with cyanogen and the
derivatives thereof ? ”
“ Jack Shay ” re Sydney waitresses :
Waitresses’ hours in Sydney eating-houses are notori-
ously long-. Even in the high-p»iced and best-managed
places the day’s work averages 13 hours, for a wage of 7s.
to 10s. per week. But I know a place which puts in the
shade the worst sweating horrors ever heard of. The
last proprietor paid a woman (not a girl) 4s. per week of
seven days for 13 hours a day. The bed she slept in occu-
pied half of room 12 x 14ft., and in the other half slept
proprietor and wife, the only division being a chintz cur-
tain. The unfortunate slave was not allowed to touch
steaks, chops, or eggs. “ Eat the entrees,” was the order;
“ they’ll only go sour if they are left”; and the drudge
would have to fill up with stale stewed tripe, and such
delicacies. Present proprietor is a Dago of some sort, and
he expects two girls to sleep in the other half of his bed-
room. He pays them, respectively, ss. and Bs. per week
—not enough for lodgings elsewhere, so they’ve got to do
it or leave ; and he has no wife to tone down the situa-
tion. The premises are very old, filthy, and dilapidated.
Kitchen, a cellar 20 feet below street-level, and shop 10
feet below. Sacks nailed across area opening to keep out
street dust and filth. W.c. under dining-room windows,
and within 12 feet of kitchen. The property is part of a
very “ big ” estate, and the business of this dreadful hole
is entirely supported by working men, who so vigorously
stick up for their own rights. W.C.T.U. and Y. W.C.A.,
are you listening ! No ; you are too busy over the
Chow’s alleged “soul” to attend to the souls and
bodies of your wretched sisters at home.
“Porthos” on J. A. Philp’s defence of the
influx of Chinese to Australia on the ground
that, under arid conditions of interior, white
men can’t grow vegetables to commercial ad-
vantage :
The flaw in this argument is that if white men in these
arid districts can’t earn enough to pay as much for vege-
tables as would enable white men profitably to grow them,
then they’re not earning a living wage and the country
isn’t worth inhabiting. If the interior of Australia only
yields a profit equivalent to the difference between the
earnings of the few Chinese gardeners, and those needed
to support Europeans, I maintain that it would be
better to abandon it than make it excuse for introduction
of lower civilisation. As for Premier Seddon’s alle-
gation that the West Coast (M.L.) goldfields would be
deserted but for the Chinese—what of that? The pro-
vince would simply be purchasing purity of race and the
protection of the living-standard (which has been so hard
to attain) with the small profit made from miserably paid
Chinese swaggers.
Perhaps the real reason of the hideous muddle
into which Sydney municipal affairs have fallen
is to be found in the fact that the rates are paid
by the landlord and passed on—and not by the
tenant direct. The success of local self-
Government in cities like Glasgow and Bir-
mingham is due to the fact that taxation and
representation are closely allied —the man who
has the vote feels the pinch instead of having
to pay imperceptibly in increased rent. In
Ireland, when the tenant-farmers resolved to
no longer pay tithes to the parsons of the
minority, the Government of the day came to
the rescue and converted the exaction into a
land-charge payable by the landlord, who
passed it on to the tenant, as the “ earth
hunger ” of the distressful country allowed him
to do—in the form of increased rent. And,
though the Irish Church was disestablished
nearly 30 years ago, the Irish farmers are still
supporting the survivors of the old order.

“ Onlooker ” re the British navy :
Ed. Bulletin.—Let “Ex-Naval Officer” haul in the
slack of his ignorance and coil-down. The British
navy costs about £22,000,000 per annum, of which
the Australias pay only about £90,000 —[Wrong,
£126,000.—Ed. B.]—and, as the Australian squadron
alone costs about £140,000 a year, J. Bull practi-
cally makes us a present of £50,000 every 12 months,
and yet “ Ex-N.0.” isn’t satisfied. Also, his talk
about difficulty of re-manning British fleets “ after a
couple of . . . big actions” is mere “ guff.” No Power
could instantly replace such a wastage , with equally good
material. But John Bull has enough go in him yet to
give an enemy a thumping, and even then to beat him in
utilising reserves of men, money and material, that
although the Continental Powers have long been “will-
ing to wound,” they have also been “ afraid to
strike.” They all know to a pound the fighting
weight of J. Bull’s fleet, and are quite aware
that, ship for ship and man for man, the stout
gentleman who wears such big boots, and yet walks
so quietly, is still a lot ahead of them. Also, re the trials
of the Terrible, “ Ex-N.0.” is (literally) behind the Times,
according to which (Sept. 1-15) the ship was again most
thoroughly tested, and caine satisfactorily out of every
ordeal. But she and her sister-ship, the Powerful, though
they gave a good deal of trouble at first, now appear to
be absolutely reliable, and the huge coal-consumption
complained of was mainly owing to the poor quality of
stuff supplied during the Welsh strike. As to the old
ironclads mentioned, it has been wisely decided by the
Admiralty rather to push on with new modern battleships
of great speed and power than to waste time and money
on “lame ducks,” the partial re-arming of which has
been merely to give the crews the necessary training with
modern guns.

Hear Bulletin. —Mr. F. J. Gillen, S.M.,
who for 25 years has lived among the abori-
gines at Alice Springs (Central Australia),
urges that the Governments should com-
bine to set aside tracts of good country for the
natives of this continent after the example of
the Maori reservations and the U.S. Indian
territories. Just because they never had
bloody battles with the whites our own niggers
have been, and are, considered of no account,
with the result that every sq. mile of fertile
land, and much where the rainfall is precarious,
has been sold or leased by the Crown. The
consequence is that the blacks are forced into
arid deserts and rocky ranges where wallabies
or dingoes would starve except in specially
wet seasons. Then when they come down and
spear a few beasts or steal a handful of rations
punitive expeditions are organised. Don’t you
agree with Mr. Gillen ?—Yours, C. R.
W ILTON.
