The new Maggie Moore co., which opened
at Sydney Royal on Saturday, is the strongest which
that lady has gathered together for a good
many years past. Compared with the seedy aggre-
gation, of uncertain age, which followed Miss
Moore’s banner in earlier times, the improvement
is very noticeable. “ The Prodigal Father,” with
which the season started, is a feebly uproarious
comedy burlesque, vaguely reminiscent of “Walker,
London. ” The author got some very bright ideas,
and worked them out with a rather uncertain
hand and an unreliable inkpot. An elderly gentle-
man takes a craze for exploring and starts for
Africa with his valet, Smith ; but, on the way,
he gets mashed on a song-and-dance lady, and
wastes his substance in riotous bouquet at her
shrine, and follows her around till he is due to
return from Africa. Then, as he can’t well inform
his grown-up family about the song-and-dance lady,
he comes back with an unconvincing tale about
how he and his valet had pursued the blue-nosed
mangle, and the hypothesis, and the boa-conjecture
in the jungle. After that, only two things of any
importance happen. The song-and-jump lady
drops in, and proves to be the old man’s
daughter -in - law, and has to be boarded
and lodged as the price of her silence. An imagin-
ary black monarch called Kookaboo, who figured
largely in the narrative, also calls in, and the
arrival of a purely imaginary person is always
more or less embarrassing. The old man hasn’t
the faintest idea who this horrible black corrobora-
tor may be. The African insists on staying in the
house and endorsing the bogus narrative ; and as
the explorer has frequently explained that he and
Kookaboo were like brothers, he can’t repudiate
him and throw him out with his sable heels
uppermost. The legendary individual who thus
pervades the premises is a great load on the old
man’s nerves; though he proves in the end to be
merely his own future son-in-law, who has painted
himself black and is taking a rise out of the giddy
progenitor.
* * m *
Even with a rather good foundation the
“Prodigal Father” is a very moderate comedy.
There is a great lack of small incidents to fill in the
gaps. A great part of the time Roberts has to rush
violently up and down the room and explain that the
complications of his position are fast driving him
mad, else the play would stop altogether. Roberts’
explorer is a clever piece of work. At the same
time it leaves an impression that the shadowy old
man has not quite made up his mind what kind of
old man he intends to be. He isn’t a definite old
man, somehow. Harry Leston, who turns up un-
expectedly after a long absence from the stage to
represent a backblox pressman, is a very bright
item. Percy Ward, who has a special mission
to represent the small, scared curate, scores
heavily, mainly by hanging round and saying
little in a black coat; and Walter Rivers repre-
sents Smith, the explorer’s hired accomplice and
corroborator, who is one of the best bits of comedy
in the play. Miss Maggie Moore herself falls a
trifle fiat, and doesn’t either look or sing like the
kind of song-and-dance lady that an elderly ex-
plorer, who never explored anything, would lose
his heart to. The pretty and clever Marion Willis
is the best feminine element in the cast, and little
Ethel Bashford also deserves a kindly mention.
On the whole, the co. comes in first by several
lengths, while the play is a bad third.
** * *
“The Duchess of Coolgardie,” a mining
melodrama in five acts, was put forth as the second
novelty of the Dampier season at the Criterion on
Saturday night. As the name might suggest,
there is a good deal of nugget, and blackfellow,
and sand, and tin-dog, and red-ochre sunset in the
play, but the newest thing about the “ Duchess ”
is her title. The characters in the piece are the
good old stagers of melodrama—the villain and
the injured heroine, and the duped hero, and
the wrongly-attributed infant are all flourish-
ing in close proximity to the dry-blowers and
quite at home among the Six Families. The
Duchess herself (Sybil Gray)—the part in which
Hilda Spong paralysed some London critics in the
original Drury Lane production—is lady-help in
a Coolgardie pub, and having been seen by her
ex-young man, Lionel Leigh, alias Big Ben, with
a baby in her arms, is taken by him to be the
infant’s mother. He consequently hurls abuse at
her till towards the end of the fifth act, which
she naturally resents, and the baby remains
unaccounted for during quite a while. Sybil re-
proaches her Ben for being “cold and distant,”
as if he were in Klondyke instead of Coolgardie,
and hands the child over to Wallaroo, the faithful
blackboy, whose chief duties are dodging the
villain and holding the baby. Capt. Jack Want-
ley, a dead-broke army officer with a distorted
smile and an eye-glass, is the villain, and he heads a
search-party which goes into thedesertand finds and
jumps Big Ben and party’s claim. The audience
took great delight in this desert scene Where the
two parties alternately mine to slow music, and
try to keep from tripping over an obtrusive nugget
cropping up through the sand. Even here the
baby turns up, still in long clothes (it never
gets into short frocks, though it must be
quite an elderly infant by the end of the
piece) so does its real mother, Nellie Gray, sister
to Sybil, who has been betrayed by the villain,
and is eventually murdered by him (a regrettable
incident, as it removes a very handsome woman,
and the only French hat in the piece). An inno-
cent man is accused of the murder, but is acquitted
through the efforts of Ben, who, it appears, was a
barrister all along, and everything makes for an
effective curtain. “ The Duchess ”is realistically
staged, real sand lies inches deep on the boards,
and the Warden’s real horse ploughs convincingly
among real empty jam-tins. Also, D. Cope and
orchestra help along the'good effect between the
acts.
* • » »-
A. Dampier makes a success of the hero,
Big Ben ; he is one of those large-hearted, easy-
going, slow-spoken parts which suit A.D. down to
the ground, and they do have that tired feeling
very strongly in W. A. An Irishman is made very
funny by John Forde, especially in the court-
scene; also H. Smith, as Wallaroo, the lively
black; Johnson W eir, as the villain with the
glazed optic; and J. Bruce as Yorkshire Dick,
keep up their end of the log. Miss R. Barton, as
Nellie Gray, an original sinner who repents in a
new season’s hat, is a new-comer, and Rose
Dampier as her sister Sybil, the Duchess of Cool-
gardie, is depressed, but not more so than would
be natural to a victim of misplaced baby.
** * *
“ True as Steel,” the drama which, so far
as plot is concerned, is the “ Silver King’s ” little
brother Bill in a modified form, has been revived
after an absence of two years to fill in a week or
so at Sydney Lyceum. It is one of the biggest of
the machinery dramas; the huge steam hammer
which is meant to come down on the head of the
hero is a vast, persuasive implement; and as it
roars up and down with the flames of the big
furnaces behind and around, it makes a scene
which is reasonably like the orthodox hell. “True
as Steel ” is another of the plays in which the
person who was wrongly suspected comes back in
disguise, having accumulated much affluence in
foreign parts, to clear his character. He sets about
it by going to the hair-diminisher and getting a
wig and a big door-mat beard, and then he does
odd jobs on the false accuser’s premises. In other
words, he is Deaf Dicky over again, and he proves
his innocence mostly by slumbering in a barrow
beside the fire. What the steam-hammer doesn’t
accomplish in the way of removing the stain from
his character is mostly done by the big mill-
wheel. The Bulletin doesn’t remember
exactly, owing to pressure of business, how the
stain got on his character in the first place, but
there is reason to believe that it arrived there in
the ordinary way. The good Lyceum company
gives the drama as much chance as it deserves and
a little over ; considering that that cO. has to learn
a huge fresh tale of woe every week, the manner
in which it contrives to get the hang of its narra-
tive is simply wonderful. As a rule most of it
turns up on Saturday night with its new ten-act
wail in its mind all right, and if there is an
awkward break, which doesn’t often happen,
Diver forges a cheque to fill in the gap. A reason-
able smattering of people roll up regularly, and
the Lyceum keeps open month after month, like
the widow’s cruse of oil which played to very bad
business for three years and failed not.
** * *
As time goes on the millinery of “Under
the Red Robe ” appears to get a better hold on the
public. At least, that drama is doing reasonably
well—it isn’t an unprecedented blaze of glory, but
in a moderate sort of way it has got a hold—and
the millinery seems one probable reason for it.
The quaint ancestral home of Cocheforet is pro-
bably another reason, and the Sunrise among the
Pyrenees may be fairly set down as a third. And
Gil de Berault has had more time to make an im-
pression in the one scene where he is really excel-
lent—that in which he changes his mind to oblige
a lady, and doesn’t spill the vitals of the butterfly
captain on the lawn in front of her window. Con-
sidering what an unpleasant sight the captain’s
vitals would be on a lady’s lawn on a bright
summer morning, this is really the least that de
Berault could do, but he does it very effectively.
This scene, together with the ancestral hall, and
the Sunrise among the Pyrenees, and Hawtrey’s
lieutenant, and the pleasant fooling of Cecil Ward,
.and the capable and finished death’s-head of Cion
as represented by G. Majeroni, do their best to
compensate for the various evils of the play. Pos-
sibly the girly waist and the loud howl-
someness of de Berault in some jilaces where he
shouldn’t be howlsoine have grown less aggressive
during the week, but the mean prigging of Coche-
foret’s diamonds is an incurable item.
** * *
Dante, now in liis fifth week at Sydney
Palace, is going as strong as ever, and proposes re-
maining there another month at least. This is the
first time that any show has done five weeks of
what could reasonably be called prosperity at the
Palace —in fact, the writer can’t remember when
that house was an unadulterated success for even
one whole week before—which makes the perform-
ance all the more remarkable.
m* * *
The Tivoli and the Alhambra are both
about the same as last week. In view of the heat
and other things they may pass at that. Henry’s
old silver coin show dropped out of the Royal
Standard some time recently without The Bul-
letin noticing the exit, and Copper’s silver coin
show is there instead. Probably one silver coin
show is much the same as any other.
Cup-week in Melbourne seemed rather
more so than usual as regards show business, and
the only change of bill was at the Alexandra,
where “ The Kelly Gang ” is just now wagging its
gory tale in several lawless acts. The spectacle ot
Ned Kelly surrounded by dead policemen is always
favorably received, especially on Saturday nights,
but the present revival of that limelighted slaugh-
terer is unlikely to hold the floor for longer than
might have been expected. Perhaps the manage-
ment would have done better to produce a drama
of working-man interest, and bill it as “ Ihe
Grafter. ”
** * *
“ The Liars,” at Melb. Bijou, improves
on acquaintance because it is a brainy play, inter-
preted by artists who were mostly new and over-
anxious on the opening night. The dialogue has
taken a keener edge, and a number of little points
which didn’t get stuck into the audience at the
first attempt are now drawing blood. And whilst
the talk is gaining in effect the weakness of the
plot becomes less apparent, consequently the
question as to whether Mrs. Brough’s character is
quite reprehensible, or merely womanly, or both,
is not so important as it looked a week ago. And
if her loving husband feels satisfied, and the
African nigger-shooter can console himself with a
black princess during his travels, The Bulletin
will let this part of the matter drop. For the only
girl worth troubling’ about is one’s own particular
girl, as Brough wisely remarks, and if she de-
generates into a nuisance the whole fair sex doesn’t
amount to sixpen’orth of Dead Sea fruit. Which
remark also applies to man in general from lovely
woman’s point of view. A matinee performance
of “ The Liars ” is to happen on Thursday (10th.).
** m *
Audran’s music, and the Doll Tableaux,
together with numerous other features of “La
Poupee,” promise to go strong at the Princess’s
until further notice. “La Poupee” is a tuneful
little opera, anyhow. Moreover, the central joke
appears humorous to many people, and is so con-
stantly in evidence that if even the Melb. Scottish
Volunteers were to form themselves into a theatre
party at the extreme end of the building they
would probably laugh all together at the right
moments.
** * *
“Sporting Life” drew great masses of
populace to Melb. Royal every night last week,
and even the small sinner who lives riotously On
the proceeds of a cronk signature ranked as a
satisfactory item. A cheerful horse-drama, coming
at a time like that, was a sure thing for the man-
agement, nevertheless the objection to Walter
Baker’s unscientific encounter with Jim Crake
still holds good, and common sense calls upon
Walter to remove his shirt and braces ere he starts
to stoush the Bristol Chicken aforesaid. Further
remarks touching Bland Holt’s enterprise, and
casual references to the boomsome state of the
Opera House and other places, must be held over
on account of Albert Edward’s birthday, which
fell due a day earlier than was desirable.
At Poverty Point.
Scot Inglis, whose name in private life was Tom
Whalley, was originally in the counting-house of Connell,
Hogarth and Co., the Melb. merchants, and resigned a
good billet to go on the stage, so sure was he of his own
abilities. But others were confident as well. In 1891 the
writer heard him recite “ The Death of Arnold Winkel-
ried ” at a cricket-club smoke-night at Albert Park Coffee
Palace, and instantly concluded "that the youth would be
one of the leading actors of his day. About a year after
this Whalley became a prominent amateur ; and even-
tually he turned pro. At the outset he had
extreme hard luck. His first appearance was in
“ Skipped by the Light of the Moon ” at Melb. Opera
House under Johnny Gourlay, and, on the day of the
opening performance, Whalley’s father died, and he had
to make his maiden effort in the face of this bereave-
ment. Later on, he had some of the toughest of barn-
storming experiences, had to “ walk back ” on many occa-
sions both in M.L. and Australia. Then he gradually
forged ahead, and before he left had the satisfaction of
playing lead in the best houses in Australia.
“Haresfoot”: About Scot Inglis. I cannot, off-hand,
recall a Thespian suicide because of bad business, except
Dacre’s. Hope, or vanity, springs inextinguishable in the
theatric breast. Itis curious, by the way, to remember how
much Inglis was mixed up with Dacre. Off-hand,again, the
only suicide of any notable act ress I recall is that of Marie
St. Denis (Hoskins’ pupil), in Melb., for one can hardly
allow that Amy Roselle suicided in reality. At all events,
it is extremely doubtful. Had Dacre recovered, his trial
for murder would have been acute. Marie St. Denis
sujcided from disappointed love of a married man.
Walter Montgomery was unaccountably deceived by
Winnetta Montague. She was afterwards ill-treated in
America by an actor named Ward. A foolish entrepre-
neur thought she would draw in Australia, but caved
before the howl that arose.
“T.D.”: First saw Scot Inglis as one of the Howe-
Spong co. touring Maoriland at the beginning of ’94.
Inglis was very small potatoes in those days, but posi-
tively shone as a nonchalant detective in a iurid French
melodrama. To range from minor melodramatic detec-
tive to Wilfrid Denver and Cassius within four years is a
stride noteworthy in theatrical history. By the way, the
Howe-Spong co. was rather a henpecked concern. Fair
Hilda’s mamma superintended everything, from Hilda
herself to the treasury—and her diligent nightly method
of counting every head in the house was the admiration
of circle, pit, and gallery.
Tenor Newbury will probably close his Sydney “pops”
in time to make another assault upon Melb. pockets at
Xmas. After that he has an idea of again touring M.L.,
where people always roll up if they think a florin’s worth
will be given them for a shilling.
Smythe, the Much-Travelled, has been hurt by insinua-
tions touching poor old Poussard’s little boy. He writes
to Melb. ’Erald to explain his position, and takes the
chance of having a shot at a sacred sentiiyent. Thus the
cocky one:—“Meantime, and until I ascertained that
there was no other will, I sent the boy, who was not
in very good health, to a farm near the Murray, where he
enjoyed himself so much among the horses, cattle and
poultry that, if he could have stayed there about ten
days longer, I believe he would have quite forgotten both
father and foster-mother. So deep, so beautiful, so mys-
terious is filial love ! ”
Ed. Bulletin.— Re your affecting par. about Poussard’s
self-sacrificing housekeeper. Far from caring nothing for
the alleged debt due to her by the deceased maestro, the
lady fired in a formal claim immediately upon his death ;
and, with regard to her detention of his son, I am in a
position to say that she has been treated with the utmost
consideration by the child’s legal guardian, whose claims
she has persistently flouted. — Strad. [The poor woman
must have been most unworldly, else how could she have
allowed M. Poussard to fall into her debt for £270 wages
—at 20s. a week ? “Strad’s” statement in nowise de-
tracts from the sadness of her case.— Ed. B.]
Dear Bulletin.— Something wrong with King Hedley’s
identity. Trooper Hedley, captured by Moonlight and
co. before the Wantabadgery fight, was stationed for
several years afterwards at Mogil and Wee-waa. as
never on the boards, but reached the poor players para-
dise by a shorter route : married a brewer's daughter,
and runs the brewery at Albury.—H.B.
“M.J.F.” : Was recently seeing Maggie Moore in “ Hans
the Boatman ” at Charters Towers “ Royal,” and found
fully 25 per cent, of the audience smoking. Struck me
as rather “ pure,” considering the building is of wood.
Next me was a w r ell-known wealthy Chow, and with him
a popular barmaid. The Towers to-day with the white
man is not a bed of roses, and possibly the giddy girlie
was taking no risks. ,
A dramatic co. on tour, playing “The Kelly Gang,’
struck Mansfield (Vic.) last month, within a few days of
the 20th anniversary of the shooting of the police. Un-
fortunately, the widow and family of Sergt. Kennedy
reside within a stone-throw of the hall, and were much
distressed by the loud explosion of firearms which an-
nounced the “deed of vengeance.” Poor business
resulted. The Kennedy family being highly respected,
decent residents refrained from patronising the show.
Harry Jewett, who was a promising actor when he be-
longed to Geo. Rignold’s co., has taken a turn for the
worse since he went to America. The Christian Scien-
tists have captured Harry, and his present idea is to con-
vert the purfesh to religious principles. One time, he
says, he thought nothing of lighting his pipe with a leaf
from the “ Psalms,” but now he understands all the
mysteries of the Bible, and doesn’t smoke a pipe any
more. Neither does he drink. He spends most of his
time in lamenting his sinful past. Henry’s past was very
sinful indeed—he was a star footballer.
“D.”: A few months back, I would have been in-
clined to agree with “ H.M.” and dispute the assertion
that Signor Hazon can “conduct anything.” But, in
questioning whether he could adequately tackle Beetho-
ven or Wagner, that P.-Pointer forgets blazon’s produc-
tion of “ Faust,” in which the rugged Frenchman
Berlioz proved that not even Wagner or Beethoven had
such mastery over the orchestra. And, theatrical as the
Italian school of conducting may be, it is wonderfully
stimulating—performers know the differences involved in
baton-wielding. Hazon tickles, slashes, bangs, or lifts
music out of the instruments in front of him—and I never
see him doing that lifting beat without wishing to rise
among the audience and make myself hoarse with
acclamation.
“ Madame Bertha Rossow, a distinguished Australian
soprano,” writes Tiib Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“is billed for Cardiff Orchestral Concerts, and Charlotte
Hemming is doing well, though the effects of the China’s
attempt at overlanding have not yet left her.”
“ Melb.” : T’other night, at St. George’s Hall, the ever-
green quartette, “ Come Where My Love Lies Dreaming,”
knocked the audience as though it were a novelty. I re-
member it being given in this city 28 years ago by Tom
Rainford, Melvyn, C. Stewart, and Steele, with the so-
called Original Christy’s. “By the Sad Sea Waves ” is
another perennial melody of the same type.
“Paradis”: I never saw Hennings strike home as
thoroughly as with a picture in one of those panoramas
which used to be the delight of the Melb. Christmas pan-
tomimes. Old Melbourne and New Melbourne floated
across the stage. Where John knocked them was with
Fawkner landing from the Yarra, in the forest, on the
site of the future city.
“ B.E. ” : Tiie Bulletin is always delighted at, and in-
variably prints, a remark like the following: “It is
characteristic of the theatrical mind to read abuse into
every criticism that does not go the whole hogshead of
thick treacle,” with which “S.S.” enlightened my ignor-
ance in “P.P. ” 15/10/’9B. This is a well-worn jibe. The
theatrical mind does not as a rule read abuse, it only
reads ignorance. “ S.S.” surmises rightly. I have been
a poor player, and therefore probably know more about
the stage than he does. Every penny-a-liner thinks he
can write about matters histrionic, and does. That’s
what the average pro. objects to, and rightly. If a mum-
mer were to criticise, say, a story by “ 5.5.,” the latter
would, most likely, consider it an impertinence, and
advise his critic to stick to his last. And, as to values.
Even if granted that the actor’s art is of less value than
the writer’s (and that is a moot point), I am inclined to
think that the former’s is the more difficult. The writer
may put something of himself into his work with advan-
tage ; the actor, to be a good actor, must submerge his
identity. And to convey passions, emotions, character,
so as to convince, which is the easier medium, the ink-pot
or the make-up-box? Men were, probably, actors by
instinct before the first picture-writing was ever drawn
upon a flat stone. And even then there were, no doubt,
Scribes and Pharisees “ who sat afar off and spoke with-
out understanding.” [There are millions of actors-by-
instinct who don’t go upon the stage. The average
mummer is merely the offspring of a mummer, whilst
with new-chums it is mostly vanity and cheek that take
to the boards. How often do we find a small, fat man
aspiring to the part of Romeo ! The ordinary theatrical
mind does read abuse into every non-admiring criticism,
for “ unkind ” criticisms are bestowed only on poor per-
formers, and poor performers are full of foolishness and
conceit or they wouldn’t be in the purfesh. A really good
actor seldom gets rough notices from the Press. Some-
times an experienced mummer may be told that he falls
short of perfection, and then he howls “Abuse ! ”]
The death of Weston, the violinist, recalls that when
Cowen arrived in Melb. to conduct the orchestra at the
’BB Exhibition, he brought with him another first violin,
whom he promptly placed over Weston—previously en-
gaged by letter. At this, the local man kicked so vigor-
ously that Cowen was at length forced to back-down, and
create a first violin and a leading fiddler, or something to
that effect —much as the Firm satisfied both Bartho
and Dargo by terming one the premifere danseuse and the
other the prima ballerina.
In “ The Lights o’ London,” Rignold, as Harold Armi-
tage, has to say : “ I will go away from this place a
beggar.” On the last night of the piece in Adelaide,
where the season was very bad, he added : “ And I swear
I’ll never come here on a theatrical visit again.”
From Kansas Agitator, published in Garnett, Kansas,
U.S. A. :—A hermaphrodite gave a “ lecture ” (exhibition)
in Garnett, Monday night. The “ entertainment ” was to
men only. Just what benefit such an exhibition is to the
public we fail to understand. She, he, or it was dressed
in woman’s clot hes, which probably attracted more men
and boys than if she, he, or it had been dressed in coat,
pants, and vest. Had the “lecture” been given to
physicians only no fault could have been found with it,
but physicians are familiar with such “ freaks of nature,”
and they could have no interest, in freaks of that kind.
The fact is, this creature is merely travelling about the
country, giving exhibitions of her, his, or its self for the
money there is in it. The morbidly curious attend sim-
ply to satisfy their curiosity, and' no one is benefited.
Such creatures should be drummed out of every town.
“T.D.”: These phenomenal male alto and soprano
voices always remind me of the ancient order—when
Haydn, a chorister in a Vienna church, offended the
choirmaster by his revolt, and (luckily for himself) the
revolt of his parents also, against “the usual means then
taken to perpetuate a fine soprano in boys.”
Once when “ hypnotist ” Kennedy s6anced down South,
among the committee selected was a somewhat umbrageous
bookmaker. Mo, on being asked to examine subject in
alleged trance, said—“ He appears to me to be dead.”
Whereupon voice from gallery—“Go through him, old
man ! ”

The new battery of 40 stamps lately started at
the Galleymont, gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feedert
without shovelling, and the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissler,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*

The following statement shows how the revenue
of N.S.W., Queensland, Victoria, Tasmania and Westralia
has been improving or otherwise for that part of the
financial year ended 31st October, 1898, as compared with
the corresponding portion of last financial year to 31st
October, 1897. This represents four months of the finan-
cial year in N.S.W., Queensland, Victoria and Westralia,
and eight months in Tasmania. S.A. presents its returns
quarterly, so its figures are for the Julv-September quarter
of 1898, as compared with the corresponding quarter of
1897 :

N.S.W. revenue for October, was <£761,340 —
an increase of £57,055 as compared with October, 1897.
The revenue, up to 31st October (four months), is
£2,987,486; expenditure, £3,278,775 ; deficit, £291,289.
The month’s expenditure was £48,656 larger than that for
October last year, so it nearly overtook the increase of
revenue. And in the four months £627,413 of loan money's
have been expended, as against £486,436 in the same
period of ’97, and £397,727 in the same period of ’96. Ap-
parently the rule in N.S.W. is that when the revenue is
gooi) -the loan expenditure is increased, because the
couiKry can better afford to pay the interest; and when
the revenue is bad and the times are depressed it is also
increased in order to find work for the unemployed.
According to Premier Reid’s long-delayed
financial statement, N.S.W. finished last financial year
with a deficit of £17,484. This is the fourth successive
deficit (the existence of every' one of which was flatly
denied) in Premier Reid’s accounts, the total of the four
being £864,676. Next financial year, despite £176,000
new customs taxation, is expected to show a deficit of
£72,417, making five consecutive shortages, totalling
£937,093. N.S.AV. is having a real good time.
* * * *
\ ictoria’s revenue for October, 1898, was
£596,593, an increase of £97,364. This puts Victoria at
the top of the list with the biggest revenue increase up to
date. But it will probably fall into second place this
month. The revenue for November, 1897, was swollen
to stupendous dimensions by the receipt of £140,000 pro-
bate money'; and November, 1898, will probably drop
behind last y'ear’s record, instead of showing an increase.

Queensland’s revenue for October, 1898 (£317,623),
is £38,397 better than the amount received in October,
1897.

And AVestralia proposes to spend £1,379,876 loan money
this year—equal to £8 per head of the population, as
compared with about £ll per head spent for the year
ended June, 1838. Even in the maddest days of the Vic-
torian and N.S.AV. booms the expenditure of £4 per head
per annum of loans was considered a big thing. AVestra-
jia’s population has now shown a decrease for two months
in succession, and the AV.A. boom is about the deadest
boom any of the Seven Provinces ever saw.
* * * *
According to a balance-sheet of the City of
Sydney (published with great pride by the May'or to show
the solvency of his awful Corporation under its present
awful management), the city’s liabilities are £1,520,000.
And its assets, including cash, sinking funds, and every-
thing saleable and many things unsaleable (for example,
“Town Hall, £350,000,” and “ new markets, £574,000”)
are set down at £1,575,000. If the Sydney Corporation
isn’t utterly ashamed of this result it ought to be. It
means that practically every stick and stone the Corpora-
tion possesses is mortgaged to the last inch ; that it has
never paid for any one of its assets except with borrowed
money ; that it isn’t paying off a copper of its debts,
for the new debts rise up as the old ones are paid off;
and this despite the fact that the Sydney Corporation
managed long ago to pass some hundreds of thousands of
its debt on to the Government, and received much
Government assistance in other ways. If the present
Corporation is not convicted on its own balance-sheet, it
certainly ought to be.
• * * •
The first count in the Crown’s indictment
against the Q.N.B. ex-directors relates to that, famous
issue in 1888 of 29,540 new shares at a premium of £3 10s.
per share. It is alleged that 3155 of these shares wouldn’t
sell, so the bank bought them itself to keep up public
confidence, and to put a bogus value on the shares, and
that, as the price alleged to be paid for these shares was
put in as capital, and the premium alleged to be paid was
included in the reserve, both these items became bogus
and fraudulent. In 1890-’9l-’92 and ’93 it is alleged that
the bank kept buying its own shares in order to keep the
dropping market from even worse collapse. Q.N.B. shares
began to be rather feeble even in 1890, when they fell
from £l2 to below £lO, and after various fluctuations they
got down in ’92 as low as £5, so even in these far-back
times they badly wanted support. The rest of the Crown’s
indictment is mainly a monotonous tale about huge over-
drafts on which it is asserted the bank had prac-
tically no earthly possibility of getting either principal or
interest, but on which the interest was allegedly charged,
and reckoned as profit, and paid away in dividends. The
most astonishing part of the accusation is that the
management went on just the same after the 1893 suspen-
sion. It is alleged that even in some cases where it had
previously ceased to reckon interest on the bad debt as
profit, it actually started doing so again about ’95, and
that part of the alleged profits since the suspension were
made by reckoning in interest on old overdrafts practi-
cally abandoned as hopeless in previous years. If the
Crown can prove this, the Q.N.B. management W'as surely
the boss incapable of Australia. After ’93 there was a fair
chance to stop paying dividends and keep square—so
many other banks had stopped paying dividends that
there was no particular need to get deeper in the mud for
the sake of showing paper profits. And if the Q.N.B.
crowd didn't know enough to grab at the chance of
putting some of its old blunders straight, it was too stupid
to recognise an opportunity when it saw one.

That very dreadful proprietary concern, the
Provident and Industrial Insurance Co. of Maoriland,
issues its balance-sheet for the year ended 30th June,
1898. Said document sets forth that the revenue from
premiums, interest and transfer fees was £5673, and the
working expenses (including dividends and £l4Bdeprecia-
tion of furniture) were £7837—0r 90 per cent. These
were the actual working expenses of the year ; but in
addition £lOOO was written off previous expenses which
were not charged to the years they properl.y belonged to,
so the working ex.’s debited against this year’s income of
£8673 were £8837, or more than the whole receipts of the
business. And in addition claims were £2530. Leaving
out the £lOOO above-mentioned, the actual year’s receipts
and expenditure of the business were :
Receipts. Expenditure.
Premiums .. .. £8399 Claims .. £2530
Rents, Interest, &c. 274 Expenses .. 7837
Apparent Loss ... 1694
£10,367 £10,367
This is on the actual year’s working, but as the Provident
and Industrial professes to have also paid £lOOO towards
the held-over expenses of previous years, that makes
the apparent loss £2694. For the year ended June, 1897,
there was an apparent, loss—somewhere under £lOOO. The
P. and I. generally seems to have a loss.
Last year the co. included among its liabilities
"advance account £3320” (apparently money borrowed).
This year the advance account stands at £4577, so it looks
as if the institution was getting deeper in debt as it goes
along in order to keep afloat, though it pays dividends
and whoops greatly about its prosperity. An im-
portant item among its assets is £2563, money spent in
working expenses during previous years and not debited,
but put aside to be gradually written off. This isn’t an
asset at all, but a mere paper entry not worth the ink it
is printed with. The assurance fund professes to have
increased during the year by £546. The loss of
£2694 is turned into an alleged profit of £546 by
putting down the value of the co’s business pre-
mises (apparently leasehold) at £4508, where last year
they stood in the balance - sheet at £1268. There
appears among the alleged revenue the truly won-
derful item, “ Property—difference as per builders’ and
architect’s certificates attached, £3240,” which makes all
the difference. If that sum wasn’t added on, the Provi-
dent and Industrial’s balance-sheet would present an
appearance of utter hopelessness. The “certificates at-
tached ” show that the experts differed to the extent of
£260 about the value of the property, and the co. took the
larger valuation. Of course it is just possible that this
amazing item is all right, but even if it is, the co. can’t
add £3240 to the alleged value of its property every year,
and if it continues to spend £1694 more than its receipts,
while largely increasing its liabilities by accepting new
business, the result is certain 'collapse. The assurance
fund assumes to be £36oo—less than six months’ pre-
miums—but as £2563 of this is only represented by the
paper item of unpaid expenses, the actual situation,
assuming the increased valuation of property as absolutely
correct, is this :
Liabilities. Saleable Assets.
Capital paid .. £4,875 Deposit .. .. £5,000
Various liabilities 5,488 Property.. .. 4,508
Assurance fund.. 885 Agents’balances 119
Premiums due .. 700
Sundiy debts .. 301
Furniture, &c. .. 484
Cash .. .. 136
£11,248 £11,248
But for that famous increase in the alleged value of the
co.’s property there would be no assurance fund at all,
and about half the capital would have disappeared. As
it is, the actual assurance fund is only equal to a little
over one month’s income. And, as the “ deposit” is pre-
sumably' the £5OOO which has to be left in the hands of the
Government as security for the policy-holders, it really
looks as if all the liquid assets actually in hand to meet
claims (which amounted last year to £2530) are the above
£136 in cash. At the end of 1895 (no later figures are
available to The Bulletin) the Provident and Industrial
had £146,000 of insurance in force. How does the £BB5 of
actual assurance fund look against this? And why'doesn’t
the Seddon Government start a system of State audit?
Considering that its province owns what certainly looks
like the very worst life-assurance concern in the Seven
Provinces, the case calls for it.
* * * *
Another little proprietary concern, the Austra-
lian Metropolitan Life Assurance Co. of Brisbane, ended
its third year on 18th August, 1898. During the year it
collected £5509 in premiums, as compared with £4443 for
the previous year. The total year’s income (apart from
calls on shares) was £6393, and expenses swallowed up
£4423 of this, or fully 69 per cent. Last year expenses
ran to about 60 per cent., so things appear to be growing
worse as time goes on. The amount saved and added to
the funds was £1219, so the assurance fund, being £1903
last year, would naturally be £3122 this one. Instead, it
appears as £13,217, which glorious rise is made by
putting down the value of the company’s new building at
£lO,BBO more than was paid for it—at least, the report
says that the co. has bought an office building, and “made
a gain of £lO,BBO according to the valuation of that
asset,” though the sum doesn’t seem to fit in exactly.
The name of the sanguine individual who valued “ that
asset ” isn’t mentioned. But so far as can be
gathered from the balance-sheet, it certainly appears that
the Australian Metropolitan bought the equity of re-
demption of that building for £6OOO or so, and straight-
way' put it in the balance-sheet as worth £16,000. It will
be interesting to see, when the valuation of liabilities is
made (presumably there will be one some time soon),
whether the assurance fund would have had any margin
to the good if this streak of luck hadn’t come along.
This makes the third proprietary life assurance co. The
Bulletin knows of which has been somewhat helped (the
other two were helped when in a dreadfully tight place)
by the great kindness of the property in becoming worth
a lot more than was paid for it, or a lot more than the
previous valuation.
To all intents and purposes the big office-build-
ing is all the people assured in the Australian Metropolitan
have to show for their money. The assurance-fund, as
already mentioned, is set down at £13,217, and the office-
property (£24,000, less £BOOO mortgage) represents
£16,000, so the whole fund is in it.
Australasia of late years has begun to be over-
run with small proprietary' life assurance offices. They
supply no want, for there are more than sufficient good,
large, reputable societies already in existence for the
business to be done. Their chief apparent purpose is to
supply some people with good billets and to enable share-
holders to get 10 per cent, interest on their capital. The
assurers have no control over the management of the
funds ; the directors can do what they darned well please
with them, subject only to the control of the shareholders.
As a rule, the director’s and the management constitute
a majority of the shareholders, and in these cases there is
no control—the people who run the business meet to-
gether once a year to rigidly and impartially scrutinise
their own doings ; and to report, if the circumstances
warrant it, that their own conduct has been everything
that it ought to be. The Bulletin isn’t insuring its own
life in any small new proprietary office at present.
The County of Bourke Permanent Building and
Investment Society—one of the big land-jobbing institu-
tions of the Melbourne boom-time —has at last surmounted
its difficulties. When the great smash came it owed
about £140,000 to depositors, and its assets were about as
unreal isable as the average. Since then it has managed
to reduce its deposit liabilities to i 7663, so they are
practically wiped off. Out of the nominal capital of
£152,841, it is reckoned that, after allowing for all losses
and establishing a reserve of £10,500 in case of more
depreciation, £51,916 remains intact. Last year’s profits
were £3758, so the society can pay 31 per cent, dividends
on its reduced capital. Among the long list of Melbourne
land-jobbing institutions which more or less went under
in the boom-time this is the first to come definitely to
the surface, and after being more or less suspended for
seven years, the old business of lending money on desir-
able freehold securities starts again.
* * * *
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. reports that it
made a profit of £85,404 for the half-year. AlO percent,
dividend is paid, absorbing £85,100. The C.S.R. Co. has
£1,702,000 paid-up capital and various reserves amounting
to £419,000. Its profits hardly vary at all, and the 10 per
cent. div. has now been forthcoming regularly for eight
years in succession.
• * * *
The secrecy of the N.S.W. Treasurer’s Budget
proposals! The labored efforts to make it appear that
only the Treasurer and his Cabinet colleagues know the
new taxation to be disclosed in the Budget speech are
often theatrical humbug. The politicians doubtless mean
well, hut they don’t know. It was known in Pitt and
Hunter streets at 3 p.m. of the day on which Treasurer
Reid delivered his last Budget speech that tea was to go
under a 3d. a lb. tax, although it was after 6 p.m. before
the announcement was made in Parliament. Similar
things happen year after year.

Dear Bulletin.— Re a par in Bulletin of Oct.
28 from “ a White Cliffsman ” —I know the Barrier pretty
well, and the news that “enough copper has quite
recently been found about 100 miles N.E. of Broken Hill
to make the hair of any Mt. Lyell or Chillagoe man stand
on end,” made mine. I wired to one of the best of
authorities at Broken Hill and have this answer—“ Show
known as Grasmere, worked some time now held by
Adelaide speculators nothing sensational idle lately.”—
Sulphide.
* * * •
A cable-message reports the British B.H. Co.
as having a credit-balance of £10,328, though on the half-
year’s work there was a loss of £3553. Some> more figures
are wanted to explain things ; for instance, have the law-
costs of the half-year been included in the term’s expendi-
ture ? Anyhow, it has been a bad time for the British
lately. First, it began fooling round with the con-
centrating-mill, and somebody had to be called in to put
things straight again—all involving both loss of time and
waste of money. Then the stone has been terribly hard,
and continues that way ; in fact, another ore-breaker has
just been put in. Thirdly, the carbonates, which long
served the co. as an invaluable stand-by, have lately been
untouched —there isn’t much of them left. But of sul-
phides, there are big faces exposed in many parts, though
their hardness is leading to vigorous prospecting below
300 ft., in the hope of kindlier stuff coming in lower down.
It may; but it generally doesn’t.
• * ■* «
The B.H. companies were, a little while back,
asked by S.A. Railway Dept, to get as much ore as possi-
ble to the seaboard before November, in order to make
room for wheat traffic afterwards. And didn’t the co.’s
take the advice! Ore-export went up 40 per cent,
promptly. Every available wool-ship was sent to Port
Pirie for stiffening; Block 10 put over 2000 tons of concen-
trates into one boat. And the department, which saw
the wheat harvest fade under its nose, didn’t know that
its advice was so heavily loaded, and was sorry it spoke.
But it wasn’t consideration for the poor farmer that
moved the companies—it was the threatened increase in
freights.

The building of Mareeba-Chillagoe railway is
going apace :—From a recent report: “ Later researches
at Featherbed Creek tend to show that the cost of per-
manent line will be much cheaper than anticipated.”
Also, rumored in Melb. that a big find of copper, carrying
good gold and silver, has been made in one of the Pro-
prietary blocks. Be that as it may, the boom has faded,
and many of the boomsters who went to Chillagoe in
haste have returned at leisure. Some remain in the
North for the very best of reasons. By the way, some
prosecutions for misrepresentation, ringing the changes
on samples, etc., are talked of ; and generally things are
in a dubious way.
* * * *
Whispered in Melb. that a certain lurid mines-
ter is about to startle Australasia with a big diamond-
discovery, somewhere in the far wilds of Australia.
Lazarus, Bendigo, has a big reef at 3425 ft.; but,
at time of writing, it is too early to say what it’s made of,
except that it’s well-mineralised, and gold has been seen
in it. In most mines, 350 ft. is a fairly deep shaft. Here
there is a winze dropped nearly that deep from the 316gft.
level; and from that winze again long crosscuts have
been thrown out. Overcoats are not worn at the
ends of these crosscuts. And in the New Chum Consoli-
dated, a crosscut 80ft. long has been put out from a winze
500 ft. deep, sunk from the 3077 ft. level; good prospects
have heen got there, too. Also, overcoats are not worn
there either.

* * * *
The antics of a maiden with a bull ant on
her are nothing in comparison to those of the
boomster when he finds that the game is up. Months ago
when Lake Dora (Tas.) boom just started, fair warning
was here given, but to little effect. The Dora shows were
engineered by the same press-gang that was responsible
for the Barlen Hercules boom, and to all appearances it
got out of both specs, at a fair figure.
• * * •
Some weeks ago a number of papers published
statements to the effect that the Rothschilds had sent
one of their experts to examine the Mt. Lyell Co.’s pro-
perty. Later on these statements were contradicted,
but those in the know merely smiled an ugly smile. It is
well-known to anyone in the metal trade that the Roths-
childs are considerably interested in the copper ring, and
maybe they boss the whole show. Ain how, such firms
as Merton and Co., of the Metal Exchange, London, if
they are not exactly Rothschild branches, act with them
and for them ; maybe they are the Rothschilds only in
another coat. Now Merton and Co.’s experts were out
here and examined Mt. Lyell. They also —and, mind
you, not a paltry spy or two—carefully studied the West
Coast of Tasmania, with the result that big smelting
works are now being erected. These smelters will control
the output of ore on the West Coast in the near future.
What this means it is not difficult to guess.
At the half-yearly meeting of the Hercules Co.,
one of the really solid shows on the West Coast, the Aus-
tralian director of the Tas. Smelting Co. and expert agent
for Merton and Co. was present as a shareholder, and
strongly argued for the sale of the ore, presumably to his
co. It appears that the Tas. Smelting Co. was anxious
some time ago to secure the Hercules output for a num-
ber of years, but negotiations fell through. Now their
director is a shareholder. The co., or some of its agents,
will soon be interested in most of the going mining con-
cerns on the West Coast, and with unlimited capital at
its back can make its own terms.
The advance in the price of tin is answerable for
the move to resuscitate tin-mining in Tasmania; but the
prospect of hatching another tin boom is miserably re-
mote. The erstwhile alluvial stanniferous deposits of the
N.E. and E. coast of the island have been worked to the
bitter end. In the early tin-mining days the richest of
the ground was boxed ; then came the ground-sluicer,
and he was followed by the hydraulic. Now the casual
celestial can scarcely get enough to pay for rice. There
is no poor ground that the higher price of the metal will
make payable ; the persevering Ohow got all that, and no
new tin-bearing ground has been found for years. If Rex
Hill doesn’t redeem the Tas. tin-industry, the islanders
must go bald-headed for copper again if they want,
another boom.
Melb. Punch on N. Mt. Lyell : “ Taking steps
to ship about 70 tons a week to London. The profits on
this should realise at least £lO per ton.” Also, “ the
North Mt. Lyells are down 220 ft. in a rich ore body, and
are going down 110 ft. further. The estimate of the worth
of the ore in sight is two millions and a half sterling, and
we are inclined to think that this estimate is not so
much over the mark as is frequently the case with the
opinion and valuation of sanguine or interested mining
experts.” What do you think of that now? Two and a
half millions in sight, and shares at 455. ! 220 ft. in a rich
ore body, and going down 110 ft. further into it. Where
is the faith of the public in this great mine ? Or, more
important still, where is the public’s faith in Melb.
Punch ?
Things said of some Westralian mines by Lon-
don Economist’s special mining commissioner : —Lake
View —the leading mine of W.A. The largest output, the
most gold in sight, the most ore developed, best equipped
and managed mine on the field. In time its output and
gold in sight will have to give place to the Associated.
Nearly two millions in sight, of which over 1J millions will
be profit. A big development since this was written.
Great Boulder : A very fine mine but so mismanaged as to
do serious damage before long. Mistakes : —ln retaining
Zeb. Lane at 1000 guineas a year to live at Perth 350 miles
away, besides retaining John Taylor and Sons in London
to do the same work. In carrying out a policy, against
the strong representations of the manager, of picking the
mine to keep up a 2oz. average, and leaving behind large
masses of 7to lOdwt. ore. In delaying the erection of a
cyanide plant for tailings, and a roasting plant for
sulphides, and in arranging a contract for their treatment
by an unknown process at an unknown price. Value of
ore in sight and tailings, a million, of which £700,000
will be profit; the lode cut at 400 ft. gives high results.
Boulder Perseverance : Only important mine on the field to
which he was refused admittance. Told it was worked like
a rabbit-warren, which he could well believe after noticing
the surface equipment visible to any eye. Shafts dotted
here and there, no machinery, ore taken by carts and rail
to a custom-mill several miles away. Reputed to be
little or no development, and, with all its richness, is two
years behind any other good mine of the field. Investors
strongly advised to have nothing to do with it. Since
this was written, Nicholls has taken charge, and, from
what present writer knows of the man, this state of
things will change, or another change of management
will have to occur. Golden Horseshoe : Has a stretch of
reef 400 ft. long, which twists into its ground from the
Great Boulder and then twists back into that mine. At
the 100 level it is 6ft. clear of the boundary, and Bft. at
the 200, so the dip is in favor of its remaining in the
ground. May fault and go into the Boulder, which is the
only point against the mine. Average value for 7ft., 4oz.
Two other undeveloped reefs in the mine ; 60,000 tons in
sight, which should yield a profit of £360,000. Ivanhoe :
Ore in sight, 125,000 tons, which will yield a profit of
£500,000. Probable div. on £1,000,000 capital 12 per
cent. Mine opening up exceptionally well, and should
have a million in sight. Estimated that when the drives
are extended to the boundaries at the 300 ft., ten years’
work will be in sight. Ore to that depth nearly all free
milling. Hannan’s Brown Hill: A mile distant from all
the other rich mines, and the ore of a distinctly different
character. Evidently a purely local deposit of schist and
apparently does not continue to carry payable gold below
about 250 ft. (How very quiet this has been kept—and
how cursed was the writer for saying so a year ago !)
Nearly all the payable ore in the mine exposed, but the
value is remarkable. Estimated tonnage, 160,000, worth
a million, which should give a profit of £6 a share. New
plant will probably show a profit of 200 per cent, but in-
vestors must not be led away by these high results.

Lock washday
Shortly after his arrival in S.A., Gov.
Buxton was good enough to say to an Adelaide
audience: “What surprised and pleased Lady
Victoriah and myself on landing on your hospit-
able shoahs was to see that your little children
were the same as those whom we have seen with
so much delight in the deah mothah country ”
What did he expect ? Piccanninies ? Also, Lady
Victoria Buxton, just before her departure from
Adelaide for England, was discussing with a
lady the acquirements of Edmund Barton. “I
met him in Sydney,” she said, “and I found him
to be so cultured and so well read that it is hard
to believe that he has never been out of Australia! ”
* * * *
A deputation has waited upon Sunday
Brunker to ask that female attendants be ap-
pointed to look after lady-drunks at the watch-
houses. The request is reasonable ; it has been
made before, and should have been complied with
long ago. Apropos of drunks, there is another
and long-neglected field for the exercise of philan-
thropy. Anyone who gets locked-up on Saturday
night has, failing bail, to stay in quod till Mon-
day. A society which would devote itself to the
good work of visiting the city lock-ups, armed
with long cold whiskies-and-soda for the benefit
of those stranded in limbo with swollen head,
would do—if for once in a way this paper may
venture to speak on behalf of Heaven—a lot more
towards saving their souls than by going to church
THE DANGERS OF THE GRAMMAR-LESSON.
LADY TEACHER (describing the various kinds of sentences): “For
instance, if I said ‘My hair is golden, ’ what would that be?”
LITTLE MARY JONES: “That would be a lie, ’cos it’s red.”
and putting 3d. in the plate. The Bulletin
understands that there is a band of well-meaning
women who do visit the lock-ups early on the Sab-
bath bearing cups of tea for the ladies who find
themselves literally in a tight place. But tea—tea
without a stick in it!
* * * *
One hot day, while the M'Sharry case was
running its course, a solitary spectator found his
way into the gallery of the Court-house. At the
time of his arrival there was a bit of a break in the
evidence. Bruce Smith was searching amongst
his papers for a document with a lot of figures on
it. R. B. O’Connor was reclining at the table
with his chin on his hand, waiting for the docu-
ment to turn up. The arbitrator was —well, a bit
drowsy ; the sleep of the instructing attorneys was
so calm that spiders were spinning webs round
them. Seeing such a pacific scene beneath him
the gallery occupant put on his hat, pulled out his
pipe, and lit up. In about half-an-hour the usher
came along and told him to put his pipe out in
court. “ Oh,” said the visitor, “I didn’t know it
was a court! ’’—and retired to Hyde Park again.

Things have been lively recently in the
R.C. minister - manufacturing industry, Card.
Moran having turned out, at St. Mary’s, Sydney,
the other Sunday, two priests, four deacons, and
three sub-deacons, while next day no less than
two dozen young men from St. Patrick’s College,
Manly, were admitted to “minor orders.” The
“ould faith ” appears to be going very strong in
Australia.
• * * *
In the last 30 years £160,000 has been
collected for St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney.
♦ * * *
A study in ecclesiasticism :
Recently did two long railway trips from Melbourne.
Up, we had a parson of some sort in the same carriage.
He was a thorn in all sides and a weariness of the spirit,
objected to cards, abhorred the smell of whisky, howled
about draughts, and wanted two seats and a whole rack
to his own cheek. When not objecting, he was preaching
dolorously at the chilled female opposite about medicines,
the management of children, and his own ailments.
Coming down, a R.C. priest got in at a wayside station.
Usual type, fat, red, and amiable ; had no objection to
anything, took a hand at euchre, pooled his own whisky,
handed round cigars, and talked like a man and a brother.
His reverence may not have been a saint, but he gave us
a new respect for heaven as possibly a habitable place
after all. Also, was in a city baker's shop this morning.
The girl behind the counter said : “I hate to see that
parson come into the place ’’(referring to one who had
just gone out) ; “he is such a cold, nasty man. He came
here the other day with a poor fellow, for whom he bought
a 3d. loaf. Before the beggar got the loaf the parson said
to me, ‘ Give me a knife.’ I handed him one. and he took
it and cut the loaf into four pieces. ‘ Now, my man,’ he
said, ‘ you won’t be able to go and exchange the loaf for a
pot of beer.’ It was charitable, of course, of him to buy
the poor man a loaf, but how disgusted I did feel at the
way in which he did it! Though lam not a Catholic, I
must say that you could never imagine any priest doing
such a cruel thing.”
Nor does The Bulletin think that many parsons
would have done such a cruel thing—and it is
quite ready to believe that loaf-cutting shepherd
meant well all the time. But the fact remains
that much of the R.C. Church’s power is due to
the priest being a man of this world as well as of
the next.

In Bananaland railway-carriages (second-
class) only certain of the conveyances are provided
with cushioned seats.. Writer was travelling
second-class some time ago and naturally wanted
to get into a cushioned carriage. Was stopped,
however, by a uniformed inmate of the carriage’
who informed him—“ This compartment is occu-
pied by a prisoner, sir.” This scribe, not having
robbed a church, a hen-roost, or a Chinaman,
stepped mournfully into a carriage without
cushions.

“ Bargo ” : North Sydney Suspension
Bridge (toll 3d. return) has some peculiar uses
Recently, saw nine of a- “ push ” following a loving
couple evidently with intent to “ deal it out ” to
the successful swain, when one of the party ejacu-
lated, “ Gor bly me—he’s takin’ ’er across the
bridge.” A hurried reckoning-up of funds panned-
out only s|d., and by that time the pursued was
disappearing with his prize amongst the ferns on
the opposite bank.
In a Wellington (Maoriland) hotel,
the ether day, a pakeha cast admiring
glances at a comely Maori dame. The
lady resented it and asked him, “ Do you
wink at Premier’s wife ?” “No.” “Do
you wink at Premier’s daughters?”
“No.” “Then why you wink at
me ? ” —at the same time giving
him a tremendous slap in the
face. The logic was unan-
swerable, and the wret-
ched pakeha subsided.
White-vested Zox,
as chairman of the Vic.
Board of Enquiry into
the Habitual Drunkard
question, wants to
know whether the
Board is to regard
“drunks ” from a cur-
able or incurable point
of view. Why not re-
solve itself into a Board
of Instruction re the
science of habitually
liquoring without “giv-
ing way to intemper-
ance”? Drunkards, in
a great measure, are
merely unintelligent
boozers, needing to be
taught how to carry
their liquor “ proper-
ly.” Disraeli, who
started his political life
towards the close of
theheavy-drinkingera,
consulted an old hand
as to how he was to
avoid getting beastly
intoxicated every time
he dined at a noble
lord’s table. The advice
was—mix your drinks as
little as possible, and don’t
talk. The “ habitual,” nowa-
days, mostly comes to grief through his craving for
conversation when whisky’s about. A still tongue
makes a strong head.
The sum of the Chinese-vegetable con-
troversy appears to The Bulletin to be this :
Vegetables are dearer in Australia than in Eng-
land, yet white men leave their cultivation to the
Chinkies because “it is impossible to compete
with Chinamen.” This seems contradictory. The
explanation lies in the fact that while it might be
possible to make gardening pay small white man’s
wages at current prices of vegetables, no sooner is
an attempt made to compete directly with John
than he cuts prices down to white man’s ruin-
figure and then, when competition ceases, puts
them up again and people resume wondering why
the whites leave vegetable-growing to the Chows.
In free-labor competition the Lord is on the side
of the man with the fewest wants !
* * * *
As this paper said the other week, the
average white working man won’t grow his own
bit of vegetables when he can. Yet Ah Sue, the
Chinese fossicker whose head was battered in
near Bendigo lately, though he didn’t possess any
garden-space, managed to grow, in pots and tins,
enough onions, thyme, and other things of that
sort for his own use.
* • * »
In a Northern town, a man by mistake got
into the wrong ’bus. Discovering his error, he
immediately sprang out and got into another.
The conductor of first ’bus followed, demanded
payment, and finally got it. Chuckling hugely,
he was stepping out, when conductor No. 2 stopped
his brother in the trade and demanded payment
also. And he got it—to the joy of all beholders.
* * * *
Boy jumped on to the ’bus, caught driver’s
whip round his bulgy part, and he dropped off
with a howl. Driver laughed. Boy ran up be-
hind, got on step again, and had five minutes
ride. Then dropped off yelling “ Whip behind ! ”
and round came the whip, swish across face of
stout matron, whose genial phiz was projecting
from the window of the door. At first driver
thought the devil had got into his ’bus. Then an
umbrella-point darted through the cash-window
and nearly through him. Driver pulled up, and,
moment he stopped, the door was nearly wrenched
off, and 16st. of infuriated “shemale” whirled
round and, with skeleton of umbrella, smacked
him across face, jabbed him in mouth, rammed
him in throat, and poked him in ribs. Then she
started on horses and sent them at a wild gallop
down a street crowded with children pouring out
of school. When the remains of the driver did
manage to pull up a policeman swooped down on
him and took his name for furious driving.
♦ * * *
“ J.F.D. ” : Was riding recently on top of
Sydney ’bus, and wife, who was inside, counted 23
women and children there. Top also crowded,
and horses ready to drop. Yet driver stopped at
every “ hail ” and invited passengers with
“ plenty of room inside.” Told me it was worth
his job if he neglected to pull-up when hailed by
prospective fare.

At Bundaberg (Q.), lately, an individual
against whom evidence was very damning was
promptly acquitted. Judge advised him to remain
in the town—other places mightn’t agree with
him. At Charters Towers, a prisoner accused of
stealing bacon, and absolved of the theft by 12
intelligent Bananalanders, was advised by Judge
Chubb : “ Next time you want any bacon, I would
advise you to apply to some of the present jury.”

There is a Q.N. Bank curse looming.
Drury is dead, so is Palmer. Byrnes instigated
the present cases, and died ; and it is on the cards
that one of those very deeply interested will not
bother about anything much longer. Also, it
flattened out various (hitherto) unblemished poli-
ticians and sowed lasting seeds of dissension in
the Labor ranks. M‘llwraith’s health and humili-
ation are also in the reckoning, also the quarrel
with G-riffith over the Acting-Governorship.

Daily paper item of last Monday week :
During a thanksgiving service at Albury, Canon
Spencer said that the opportune downpour of rain was
evidence of the efficacy of prayer.
The rain came three days before the prayer
started. But, as this wicked paper prophesied, if
the rain came afterwards—no matter how long
afterwards—it would be set down to the praying,
and if it came before it would be set down to the
fact that there was going to be prayer.

MARRIAGE MORN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Fades the moonlight on the sea,
And the dawn is coming in—
What will this day bring for me,
This of all days, Evelyn ?
Ah, to-day our hands we plight;
Life or death is in the vow ;
All that earth knows of delight
Or of grief is round me now—
While the dawn-light limns the shore,
And thou in thy lonely sleep
Dream’st thy maiden dreams before
Hymen’s mystery shall steep
Thy heart’s fancies in my own,
And the pulse of passion stir
With the ecstasy that’s known
Only to Love’s worshipper.
R.C.

Astonishing, yet Expected!
Recent analyses of Sydney Lager Bier and of all other beers on Sydney market
show wonderful figures in favor of Sydney Lager, and it is thus scientifically
demonstrated that Sydney Lager deserves to be the national drink of New South
Wales. Supposing that a man drinks three pints of liquid per day ; then he will,
in a life of 70 years, on this average drink some 10,000 gallons. Suppose that
instead of Sydney Lager a man drank 10,000 gallons of deleterious highly-alcoholic
liquor. It wouldn’t matter if he could replace his liver, kidneys, and stomach
with a fresh set every twelve months—but then a man isn’t a machine of that sort.
If one of his inner and delicate mechanisms go wrong, he breaks down all over.
10,000 gallons ! —enough to float a ship ! Isn’t the thought enough to make you
solemnly kneel down and swear to be faithful to Sydney Lager for the rest of
your lifetime?

One family gaily took off its mourning
t’other day, while another as sadly put it on.
There were two t’othersiders named Stout in the
crowded Westralian hospital, and somehow their
identity became mixed. Presently, when one of
them died, the wrong family sent over the funeral
expenses, and went into mourning ; while the
poor beggar who was being mourned-for wondered
during his convalescence why his friends on the
other side had so utterly forsaken him as not to
write a line. It took several weeks to straighten
out the painful little tangle.

A case of incest was heard in Melb.
Supreme Court, the other day, the offenders being
brother and sister. The boy was 15 years of age,
the girl 14. The latter is enceinte, and the
parents informed the authorities of the facts, which
led to the prosecution. Justice Hodges said that
the lad, who pleaded guilty, had been an unwit-
ting offender, and, further, that he had committed
the crime against his will, and, therefore, he was
let off cheap.
Purves, Q.C., ably defended the boy afore-
said. The lad (whose parents themselves had
informed the police of the hideous affair) pleaded
guilty, but escaped with a nominal sentence. Next
day the eminent counsel received a letter, marked
“ private and confidential,” from a thing calling
itself a man, asking him to let the writer
know the address of the unhappy parents, as
he desired to write to them and offer to marry the
girl straightaway, conditionally on the father pay-

ing him a substantial sum for so doing ! ! The
writer explained that he had money when he came
to Australia some years ago, but he had lost it, and
had to do something to live. He was, therefore,
willing to overlook the past record of the little
girl, and take her off her parents’ hands and make
her his wife, if they made it worth his while!
Verily, it takes quite a lot of people to make a
world.

Caldwell, S. Australian M.P., who has
been drying his bones in the Vic. mallee country,
tells of the woes of the minister of religion down
there. “I took tea with the poor fellow last Sun-
day afternoon, after he had held forth to a bush
congregation with a fly in his throat, prior to his
starting for his third (or fourth) service, at a place
15 miles away. He told me that four of his
churches yielded an income of only £2 during six
months.” Wonder he didn’t swallow the fly.
Verdict of Westralian
coroner’s jury: “We find that
deceased was killed accident-
ally, but desire to add as a
rider that, in our opinion,
steps should be taken to mini-
mise such fatalities, which we
regard as a menace to life and
limb.”

A recent Melbourne Age obituary—the

CAREY.—In sad and loving remembrance of Michael

Esq., of Cahir Prison, county Tipperary, Ireland, and

John Kirby, Dublin and Broadstone Railway Depart-

Cremar, Brunswick, Melbourne ; and cousin of the late
Daniel O’Sullivan, Esq., Commissioner of National Edu-
cation, Kingstown, county Dublin, Ireland ; and of the
late Miss Ellen O’Sullivan, R. Catholic teacher, Kangaroo
Flat, Bendigo, aged 19 years 11 months 3 weeks and 3
days.
Ex-Attorney General Homburg (S.A.) as-
serts that there will be no true Law Reform until
power is given to restrict the fees of counsel, who,
he asserts, are responsible for all the delays and
most of the expense of legal actions. He wants
the Judge to be given greater power, and he asks
that barristers should all be made to disgorge
when their fees are exorbitant. “I guess you
won’t go as far as I’m prepared to in this direc-
tion,” answered Kingston, who has the Law
Reform Bill in hand.
* * * *
The recent tragic sequel to the celebrated
Lang worthy affair marks a curious possibility in
revulsion of feeling. Langworthy, who, the other
day, suicided out of grief, it is supposed, at the
sudden death of his wife, was the immensely rich
man who, about 10 years ago, after going through
an invalid marriage-ceremony with the lady, cast
her off with every indication of repugnance, and
was compelled, after protracted litigation, to pay
her £20,000 damages for breach of promise. The
complete reconciliation which seems to have fol-
lowed suggests that, although a more extended
acquaintance often serves merely to accentuate the
incompatibilities of a couple, it might sometimes
work in the reverse direction, so that those who
come to snarl might remain to worship.

yers still above ground.

What is the value of a woman compared
with (say) a tin of baking-powder? A Karori
(M.L.) butcher was convicted lately of having
stripped his wife naked and then scraped her body
with his nails for an hour or two —-except when he
stopped to flog her and other variations. The
medical evidence corroborated the woman’s story,
and described her as one horrid mass of wounds
and raw flesh ; and the butcher-fiend simplified
matters by pleading guilty. As a further aggra-
vation, it was attested that the wife had been
compelled to leave her husband 17 times by reason
of his cruelty. Then the Court was very cross
indeed, and sent the butcher-person to gaol
for six months. He didn’t get committed for
trial, and receive seven years from a calmly judi-
cial judge ; he got exactly six months. Yet if he
had stolen a leg of mutton, and it was proved to
the court’s satisfaction that he had stolen 17 pre-
vious legs of mutton, he probably would have got
seven years without the slightest difficulty. There
is a strange streak of brutish insensibility to
suffering in the law of every English-speaking
country and in every English-speaking com-
munity—unless the suffering happens in China or
Cuba.
* « * *
So great is the exodus from the West that
for months past it has been next to impossible to
get a passage from Adelaide to Melbourne or
Sydney by steamer, all the boats being crowded
with people returning from Westralia. The s.s.
co.’s offices are besieged with holders of return-
tickets desirous of getting away from the Holy
City, but they frequently have to take train back
and fight out the matter of monetary recompense
with the busy companies afterwards.
“ C.F.” : Be Japs drinking (8., 15/10/’9B).
Have had many years’ experience of Japs, in their
own country and elsewhere. In Japan,
the men drink “saki,” and else-
where very few drink to excess, while
the women hardly drink at all.
Have never seen a Japanese woman
touch anything stronger than tea,
though I have seen men try hard to
persuade them. The Jap Tottie is all
over the Far East, and, despite Japan-
ese legislation and repression outside,
she continues to spread. Her vogue
I attribute chiefly to her abstention
from grog, and consequent quietness
and respectability—if Tottiedom can
be respectable.

Rough on the two lads who recently stow-
awayed in order to see the Melb. Cup, to be
hauled out and made to work, and, after all,
handed over to the police. To sling slush and toy
with the tar-bucket is the usual prerogative of
the unauthorised traveller whom the captain
means to shoot with a parting kick on to the wharf
to go about his business. But if this isn’t the
skipper’s intention, he has no right to anticipate
the legal penalty. When you lock Burglar Bill
in the pantry and rush off for a policeman, you
don’t expect 8.8. to while away the time shelling
peas or polishing the silver.

A Kalgoorlie (W.A.) man who was out on
bail, and wanted to drop the painful subject,
nearly got away by train disguised as a woman.
Another passenger was gradually fascinated by
the spread he made with his big feet and by his
short hair ; being of an interfering turn lie called
an official’s attention to him, and “ Martha Reid ”
was dragged out by the skirt at Coolgardie
station. But if he had had sense enough to pin
on a tail of artificial hair and to keep his feet
under the seat all might have been well. It is a
curious illustration of the obtuseness of people in
general that though a man, as a rule, sees a
woman oftener than he does any other strange or
foreign object, in 99 cases out of 100 he has never
noticed how she is constructed, and couldn’t make
a reasonable copy of her to save his life. No
woman with big feet ever spreads them out at an
obtuse angle in a railway-carriage.

Concerning a much-canvassed recent N.S. W.
public service appointment:—
Editor Bulletin. —The invidious appointment of Mr.
Hayes-Williams to the position of N.S.W. Registrar
General is but another instance of the absurdity of sup-
posing that the immaculate and unbiassed Public Service
Board dispenses its patronage altogether minus external
influence. As I am neither a solicitor nor a public ser-
vant, any remarks I rise to make hereon are not made in
envy. The P.S.B. was unable —and cheerfully acknow-
ledged its inability—to make a proper selection itself, and
had to delegate its “ power of appointment ” to a com-
mittee culled from the Devil’s own. Or, as the P.S.B.
puts it, “so that the appointment might meet with
general approval, the advice of the leading solicitors was
sought.” And so the P.S.B. is going to say who are
“ the leading solicitors ” of Sydney! Like its gall ! It
was admitted by Premier Reid that one of these “ leading
solicitors” was brother-in-law to the double-barrelled-
named appointee, but he declined to disclose the rest of
this P.S.B. brand of lawyer, possibly for fear of any of
them turning out to be uncles or brothers of the fortunate
Mr. Hayes-Williams. In fact, to pick out this handful of
lawyers, who might or might not all have been related,
and call them “ the leading solicitors” of Sydney, allow
them to appoint a Registrar-General and then call it the
profession’s selection, is simply egregious cheek. It is,
also, a barefaced performance placing an outsider (whose
admission as a solicitor dates but a few years back) over
the heads of capable men who have been many years in
the department; men who were very likely expert at
their work long before the man appointed to rule their
dept, had begun his legal career. And as to Mr. Hayes-
Williams being the best conveyancer in Sydney (that’s
what is practically alleged) several very widely-known
practitioners whom I have heard express themselves on
that point had hardly heard of the new Registrar-
General at all. In re'the repugnance of other men in the
department to the billet, such an allegation is, of course,
twaddle on the very face of it. If any employer should
ever wish to gild me to the extent of £lOOO a year I
shan’t be over-particular what my predecessor left the
position for. I shall be as ready for it as a stranger—my
oath ! Anyhow, the next time the P.S.B. deems it ex-
pedient to appoint a select-committee to find a man
for an important public office, I suggest that in common
decency the committee’s relatives be barred.
S.C.C.

“ Miss Jennie Armitage, of Franklin Park,
near New Brunswick, saw a tramp, to whom she
had given food, coolly mount her bicycle and ride
off. She ran to the stable. The only animal there
was a steer. She leaped on his back and belabored
him with a stick until he broke into a lumbering
gallop after the thief. The tramp abandoned the
wheel and took to the woods. Miss Armitage
recovered her property.” —New York Journal.
That this story is perfectly feasible is shown by
Frank Mahony, in his series of sketches, “Mr.
Bowser’s Buliock Comet,” in The Bulletin’s
forthcoming Xmas number, which glittering pub-
lication comes out Dec. 8, and is the best and
cheapest typically-Australian reminder for trans-
mission to absent friends. Posted from Bulletin
office to any English or foreign address for Bd.
stamps. • ,
“BATS!”
In days of old ’twas “ All my eye ! ”
Or “Fudge ! ” when flippant talker
Commented on a seeming lie ;
Anon the term was “ Walker ! ”
They served a-while, and passed away ;
Their salted sense lost savor ;
Men wanted something new to say—
And “ Rats!” came into favor.
Best term of all: it breathes disgust,
Contempt, or kindly scorning,
Or nothing more than cold distrust —•
’Tis e’en a note of warning.
A mighty force in modern slang,
“ Rats ! ” crushes the romancer
Whose blatant screed or vain harangue
Deserves no other answer.
Requiescat in pace.
Little we thought when on that horse he rode by
That in a few hours on Matlock’s bleak road our darling
would die.
In the snow and the cold his poor mangled body would
lay,
While his dear life and his life’s blood was ebbing away.
Sad was that day when our darling took up the reins,
And rode on that horse that with his life’s blood is stained.
We will go to his grave now as his dear spirit has fled,
And amongst the green sods he is lain with the dead.
And the heart that once loved us is mouldering to clay,
And the worms of the graveyard are gnawing it away.
We will go to your grave, darling, and offer a prayer
For the soul of that poor body that lay mouldering there;
And we will think of your mild looks and kind words not
a few
The last time we parted were spoken by you.
—lnserted by his loving father and mother, sisters and
brothers, M.C., K.C., M.C., N.C., K.C., L.C., A.C., J.C.,
G. Carey, Wood’s Point.
