A WOMAN'S LETTER.
t-, ~ Sydney, November 8, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
Sydney has continued to sleep o’ nights all this
week, and, as far as that portion of society goes
that gets its tea-drinking and
pr f", fc ’ a B Teat P eac e reigns. In the mearf-
t me, while the society cats and kittens are taking
their last delirious laps from the Carnival Cup, all
sorts of little people from nowhere at all have been
junketing round and having no end of a good time
in their own particular way. I went to Manly on
moonlight trip the other night—by the way,
on t you think it very kind of the ferry people to
a vertise these trips as moonlight excursions and
choose nights when Diana isn’t looking ?
It was a hot, hushed kind of night, no stars, no
m °?”> ° n y. 6 hghts of one’s own jolly steamer,
u 6 P assin " green and ruby gleams from work-
aday boats pretending to be out on their own little
restive. The people began to gather long before
the boat started, and only the indifference begotten
P* u ch broiling and a final bake reconciled a
timid being to sail with such a freight. It isn’t a
nice thing to be maudlin, I feel sure, but at the
risk of it I confess that the sight of that swarm of
men, women, and “ worrits, ” listening entranced
to a cracked-voice warbling, “Told in the twilight,
oh my darling,” and turning their washed-out
laces to the breeze, touched a chord somewhere,
horribl 611 e J a ded piano missed one
We all sat up on the top of the boat very close
together, and the music came and went at inter-
vals, and the sacred, solemn silence which that
cr ?wded boat-load of babies and sweethearts and
middle-aged people with the gilt worn off their
sun, bestowed on the fitful concert, puts every
other audience I’ve ever known to shame. The
concert wasn’t altogether free, either—l suppose
you know it s fin de siecle to make a fuss over free
omy—for everybody round me put pennies
a s d i. i s , lnto the cracked voice’s hat with un-
affected cheerfulness. After the collection I
noticed the song was “Non e Ver,” and indeed
the voluntary penny and the sixpenny return-fare
the total outlay for the joyful evening. Down
at Manly a big brass band was making music, and
it you didn t want the worry of losing a few flounces
on the plank you could sit still at the boat’s head
and listen to operatic favorites from the shore.
INot the sort of favorites that set going such
agitating dialogues as, “What’s that? Know it
so well “Sodo I, but—oh, what is it ? ” “ It’s,
its oh, but I do know it so well.” These airs
were as unmistakeable as “Home, Sweet Home,”
and suited everybody. Altogether, nothing nicer
could be imagined, and I heard one seamy little
woman telling another “How she never slep all
the summer for want of hair,” and can believe that
many of the people like hair and the briny and the
beautiful, although there’s a Prejudice against
such a belief.
Winch recalls a touching little ad. I saw in one
or the dailies : “ Wanted a room ; must be within
sight of a green tree. ”
Coogee also is entering into the fun of things,
and the Railway Commissioners are letting people
ride out there at the usual rate when the moon is
U P- No. I hat doesn’t sound right, and generous,
and benevolent, and all that. I mean, they have
announced their intention of getting the masses
out on the cliffs, and have arranged with an un-
usually intelligent band to make music beside the
breakers ; and, although many people think that
the return-fare, which is now Bd., might be 6d., it
isn’t an uncommon style of thought, and the Com-
missioners have not been smitten by it so far.
Crowds of people find their way out, however, and
even sealed and silly things like oysters might
notice the Australian’s general feeling for anything
like music.
Here I’d like to air a thought that is probably as
much anyone else’s as my own. When you look
round at the dome of the world in the dark, or
the half-dark, with _ trees in shadow, and stars
showing pale festhetie lights, and perhaps the sea
m the foreground, or perhaps a park in the city, or
even a suburban grass-plot where all the washing
can t hang out at once, you can realise the sort of
setting Nature meant for music. Yet to hear good
music we all sit together on Austrian chairs with
backs like officers from the same country, a gas
glare in the eyes, fans fidgetting till the hot air is
hotter by several degrees, hundreds of people
jammed up against you, and a horrible, barbarous
sound of clapping smashing to pieces every beau-
tiful musical thought. This seems to be the best
civilisation can do for people who love music. Yet
m this city of summer nights it would be the
easiest thing in the world to have one’s music in
the open air. Even if there’s a wind, what
matter ? Wagner and wind are a more natural
union than Wagner and fans and chocolate creams
and gas-glare and stiff chairs. And who wouldn’t
like to hear the Appassionata or the Waldstein in
the dusk with the trees moaning quietly in the
background ?
Things were worse than ever at the last arehi-
episcopal garden-party. Despite the oppressive
heat the worn-out curates of the Primate’s flock
and their weary wives were not permitted to take
refuge in the cool verandahs before the vice-regal
party arrived. Moreover, when the sultriness gave
place to a smart shower of rain, and another more
determined rush was made for cover, all but about
30 of the Very Elect were debarred from going in-
side for fear of crowding the passage for Lady
Hampden and co. As the distinguished visitors
did not arrive until 4.30 and the curates
turned up at 3 o’clock, the latter have come to the
conclusion that rather than go through another
hour and a half of such discomfort they would —
will any layman please oblige with a fitting con-
clusion ?
Rc Mr. Mitchell’s splendid and much-talked-of
bequest to N.S. Wales, there are stories told of
great people who sought to quiz his library, and
were much surprised to receive a polite refusal.
Among others. Robert Louis Stevenson travelled
out on his card one day, and sent in a message
that R.L.S. had come to see this great collection.
But he went away without seeing it, in spite of
the R.L.S. The only privileged beings are
genuine students in search of information, and for
them Mr. Mitchell, who is acquainted with the in-
side as well as the outside of his books, goes to no
end of trouble. Apropos, a certain Sydney
woman-writer drew her first pen-money from this
generous source. She was a school-girl in those
days, and edited a little journal written on fool-
scap and passed round her class for a penny a
read. After the paper ceased to be the complete
edition of these slips was lent to a friend who
showed them to Mr. Mitchell, and one day the
school-girl was knocked over by the news that a
book-fancier would pay her so many guineas if she
would part with her scrip.
All these years Mr. Mitchell has been N.S.W.
Public Library’s biggest rival. The Library’s
finances being limited exceedingly, he could
always buy rare books over its head, and unkempt
people with literary curios would flounce away
from the head librarian’s room with the threat,
“We’ll take it to Mr. Mitchell!” This is all
changed now, as the Library and Mr. M. are
practically working for the same end.
But why, oh why, doesn’t somebody arise and
play arson with that scandalous book-shed yclept
the Public Lending Library ! Even the remotest
little galvanised country town wouldn’t stand such
a building as this. The new3paper-room is rotting
to pieces, live-stock gambol about the woodwork
of the stairs and counters, and the sickly, debili-
tating atmosphere is enough to make even the
long-haired poets, who found exaltation in diseases,
squirm on their shelves.
Of course the city is full of Cup yarns this week,
and I’ve already heard of three drapery shops
whose milliners have won enormous fortunes.
The story starts in Pitt-street Hordern’s, where a
poor little workgirl received a hoaxing telegram to
tell her she had won the big sweep. She fainted
when she got the wire, and one doesn’t like to
dwell on her coming back to consciousness and
poverty.
The decadent days of the Salon rise again at the
memory of that stately lady, the late Madame
Juliette Henri. Even unimaginative people
thought she was like a page out of a Court romance
wherein fine manners are as ordinary as fine
clothes. Last week the members of the Cercle
Frangaise who have rung the changes from Avez-
vous-vu to De Musset under Madame Henri’s
guidance, put up a monument in Waverley ceme-
tery, to keep green a memory that will not soon
fade away from this city. Mr. B. R. Wise spoke
eloquently at the grave, and many people found
their way out to the ceremony.
A C. of E. cleric seems to have sethis colleagues
by the ears by performing the marriage ceremony
at a recent fashionable wedding elsewhere than in
the sanctuary. Also, it is averred that the rev.
gentleman contravened the rubric by omitting
certain telling portions of the service.
The frocking of the d.c. that welcomed Maggie
Moore back to the Theatre Royal on Saturday night
was a sort of larky gambol with fashion’s cast-off
odds and ends. F’rinstance, I counted no less than
five pairs of shoulder-straps supporting run-away
dresses with bits of red velvet and green satin in
memory of the year before last. The stage frocks
didn’t rise above pink nun’s veiling, and blue
brocade and wide lace put on thick. Re which,
wide lace that isn’t a skirt wide is as dead
as ? n .ly a frippery can be. To keep these little
oddities in countenance, Maggie Moore sported a
sea-green gown under a white lace cover. I didn’t
think this was exactly improved by the lines of
black velvet that emphasised the undulating
country underneath, but nobody cared, and if
Maggie had been as slim as a hickory-tree she
couldn’t have had a hotter or more flowery wel-
come.
Pattie Browne finished up her season at the same
theatre the night before, with that sickly young
person, Sweet Nancy. A lovely blue silk tea-
fown with cobweb yoke was as fresh as ever, but
didn’t notice that the sun-bonnet suited Pattie
any better than of yore. Inez Bensusan was a
chic study, in cream and cherry, as the naughty
lady whose husband “ was away.” A plumy black
hat shadowed her delectable dress of yellow silk
with touches of cherry velvet at the neck and
waist. Miss Juno put a new heliotrope vest into
her dowdy black, and May Brown’s ten-year-old
legs utterly repudiated the idea that this infantile-
looking little person is Mrs. Somebody or other in
real life.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Brilliant Cup costumes are not monopolised
by the sex. A couple of Cingalese amused race-
goers on Saturday morning by parading Bourke-
street in loose white cotton continuations, sur-
mounted by flowing white skirts down to the
knees; above they wore little mauve jackets some-
what like the ex-fashionable bolero, and white
caps, gold-laced; the whole set off by blue sashes
tied round the waist with long streamers flowing
in the breeze. If the men dress that way in their
country what gaudy pictures the women must be.
“Old race-goers may say what they like, but I
don’t believe there was ever a dressier Cup than
that of 1898. Clothes enthusiasts mentally fell
down and worshipped one delectable toilette after
another till they became blase and found fault
with the omnipresence of Honiton lace applique.
The modes of the moment, what with trimmed
skirts, cobwebby embellishments and ornate hats,
are best described by such adjectives as ‘elegant,’
‘elaborate,’ ‘expensive.’ Last year ‘dainty,’
‘ fresh ’ and ‘ fluffy, ’ were strong-enough terms for
the fussy muslins and chiffon sashes of that period.
“It’s not the fault of the vice-regal fashion-
leaders that we’re all mad on net butterflies and
lace-y lovers’ knots, though come to think of it,
the latter sentimental device in steel and jet
sequins did sprinkle the bodice of Lady Brassey’s
Cup gown—a plainish princess robe of black moir4
antique. Other adornments were creased fichu
handkerchief of creamy old lace, pleated chemi-
sette of pink chiffon, and one of her ladyship’s
little wretches of pink toques. So far, female
amateur detectives have not solved the mystery of
Her Ex.’s unfailing supply of rose-colored mil-
linery. Does she buy the pink ’uns wholesale?
Or did ‘ Himself ’ advance money to a blue-blooded
modiste, who during the pink boom invested not
wisely but too well m the epidemic color, with the
result that the unsold surplus fell into the hands of
the mortgagee ?
“Anyhow, the sample unearthed for Grafter’s Cup
had rosebuds and forget-me-nots sprouting out of
a torn-down little grass plot. At the same
function Governors Brassey and Hampden affected
shepherd’s-plaid pants and sad check ties. If the
tartan mania continues to influence them, they’ll
be converted to kilts before they’re done. Button-
hole bouquets are not epidemic at present, but the
few specimens visible in the vice-regal pen and
elsewhere were either cornflowers or else small red
blossoms. Miss Daisy Madden’s short sunburnt
fiance paraded a posy of the latter description.
Scarlet is emblematic of Love’s passion, so says
that theatrical star who puts on a paler gown in
each scene of her stormy stage-married life to help
her to express the gradual cooling-down from the
ardor of Love’s young dream to the frigidity of
threadbare conjugal affection. Miss Madden her-
self wore her bridesmaid’s frock of maize silk and
chiffon, dozens of tiny lisse flounces running down
hill from back to front under the ‘ apron ’ skirt,
while masses of similar fairy frills trimmed bodice-
yoke and sleeve-tops. Pretty sister Ruby’s pea-
green gown of identical design seemed over-gay in
hue for the wearer’s complexion that stifling day.
‘ ‘ Most of the vice-regal group lost their ‘ little
bit’ on Massinissa. In consequence, his Westra-
lian Ex. bowed his hoary locks on his breast till
constituents or vice-subjects, or whatever you call
them, over here for the races were prevented from
admiring their Pro-consul’s Paisley-mixture tie.
The shawl-pattern cravat seems the newest thing
in male chiffons. Gov. Smith’s sample was greeny,
but the prettiest are dominated by a powder-pink
background. Re speculation: Aide Neville, at
the last moment, escorted a squad of senior lady
visitors out of the V.R. pen to back, for the final
time, their respective fancies for the Cup. After
the event, the party returned with limp upper-
lips, poorer by a joint-stock £3 loss for their belated
burst of gambling-fever. Their cicerone, marching
manfully ahead, broke the news to the serious
gubernatorial fogies, awaiting the return of their
women-folk. His tidings met with no response
beyond a chorus of unmirthful snorts, led off by—
can’t you guess ?
“Lady Lamington, apparently, didn’t madly
enjoy the races. Still blue-eyed and slender as
of yore, Bananaland heat has wilted out most of
her color and ‘go,’ and, like the majority of
Northerners, her coiffure falls short of elaborate
Victorian ideals. Her frock of black gauze over
white silk was over-run as to the bodice with
ruched ivory lisse and yellowish lace. She further
affected the omnipresent necklet of large creamy
pearls, and a button-hole spray of lily of the
valley, while her black-and-white toque had a
crown of recumbent ostrich plumes.
“Oh these men ! But they’ve got their own
troubles, if that’s any consolation ! If the Banana-
land Governor takes after his pa it must be per-
fectly horrid for him to live in a climate where
washing-garments never go into winter quarters,
and home wouldn’t be half so homely without a
laden clothes-line blocking up the back-yard.
Lamington, senior, in his day and generation, had
such an objection to sight, sound, scent or even
suggestion of laundry operations that he erected a
central depot in his Scotch village where, on cer-
tain days, his tenants were compelled to assemble
themselves together and do what had to be done
with soap-suds in decent retirement.
“ The prize Cup toilette, so men think, was
achieved by a tall blonde Sydney belle. Its rich
pink silk skirt, strewn with filmy devices of creamy
lace, widened into a deep graduated flounce of
darker pink, lavishly embellished with raised pink
filoselle embroidery and lace applique. A long
frilled black lisse sash, hat-plumes varying from
pale pink to decided rose, and a ruffled chiffon
parasol of blushful hue, completed an impressive
costume.
“ Lady Hampden’s black lisse veiled white silk,
and Sydney people seemed pleased (so they ought)
with her long ear-rings and becoming bonnet of
white wings, black bows and realistic rose-buds.
Daughter Dorothy’s green and white muslin blend
calls for no remark except that Its wearer had
a cheerful time in the society of that jovial
Paddy, Captain Haigh of the Inniskillen dragoons,
at present Westralian attache. This same broth
of a bhoy put a pinch of spectacular spice into the
otherwise tame-cat vice-regal tableau. A-lump of
a lad to begin with, other characteristics are a fat,
flushed face, curly hair, impudent nose, and white
hat cocked askew. Besides looking like ‘ Paddy
Whack who comes from Tipperary O,’ Gov.
Smith’s military minion has annexed something of
the old gold-fields swagger, the ‘Roaring Camp’
air, the ‘ Man that shot Sandy M‘Ghee ’ cut. He’s
a doughty polo-champion, too, having helped to
win the last two cups for the Inniskillings at Hur-
lingham and the Open Cup in Ireland, and would
have shown the Camperdown players goodness-
knows-what at Moonee Valley t’other day, if only
he’d brought over his own ponies from Westhralia,
begorra. Local equine pigmies, it seems, can’t
go fast enough with his weight up, and
small blame to them. Outsiders surmise that,
at the outset, attache Haigh must have
been imported expressly to liven up the dull-
ness of t’otherside vice - regal entertainments.
All Derby Day he was helping his Chief’s
disabled daughter up and down the steps at
Flemington. Strange to say. Miss Kenned, y
daughter of a former Westralian Governor, was
also incurably lamed, through stepping on
a scythe in the garden at Govt. House, Perth.
Miss Smith, for the benefit of those who haven’t
seen her, is a nice-looking girl enough with a very
prominent nose. On Cup Day she seemed cool in
yellow and white, while her frugally-minded
mother brought along a cloak to preserve her own
plumage from dust in the vice-regal coop. At the
Derby, it seems, the unsuspecting dowager annexed
goodness knows how much smirch in that whited
sepulchre.
“Beforehand, a huge boom in chiffon sunshades
was foretold for Cup-time, and shop-windows were
full of fluffy specimens that close inside-out
for the preservation of their elaborate linings.
However, when the time came nearly all the
“ naicest ” carried dainty silk ordinary-shaped
parasols of every imaginable shade, pure white and
resurrected magenta being as popular as any.
“Mrs. G-eo. Chirnside showed up tall, fair, and
graceful as ever in white silk banded with black,
black sash knotted carelessly at the side, and red
straw hat turned sheer up in front with trimmings
of cerise and pink. Her parasol was red, by the
way, and, as a rule, filtered a becoming flush
through.
“ Several fine women of the ‘ big ’ Vic. sporting
families were mounted in all sorts of high places,
as usual, inspecting the field. After all, what’s
the use of wearing Parisian shoe-leather and
cobwebby hose, not to speak of sensational under-
skirts, if you’re frightened to do a bit of climbing
when so inclined ? At balls and sich the Naicest
never hesitate to catch up their draperies knee-
high, to put it mildly, in the storm and stress of
the ‘gee-gee ’ cotillion figure, say, or those out-of-
date kitchen lancers.
“Mrs. Everard Browne in an exquisite blend of
pink silk, ivory lisse and fine black Tuscan hat
and feathers was in rare glee, like all her clique, over
Domino’s steeplechase win. Nothing could have
been daintier than Miss Mary Clarke’s pale blue
silk with white fixings and turquoise toque, its
three upstanding ruches edged with forget-me-nots.
“For love or money, I can’t find out the name
of the wearer of the most beforehand gown on the
grounds. She deserves credit, whoever she is, for
pioneering coming fashions instead of hanging
back with the ruck of dumb-driven women cattle.
Bodice and skirt of white silk, brocaded with
pink. Over these a sort of polonaise, consisting of
three-tiered overskirt and zouave of corn-flower
blue.
“Chinamen, as usual, treated their charmers to
the Grand Stand, and got the cold shoulder as
soon as the ladies found themselves safely landed.
Colored gentlemen too, came with their Dinahs,
but met with more gratitude. Woolly heads are
popular with the fair sex, somehow.
“ One poor fellow (white) was palpably thread-
bare, and wore the mingiest little fur cap all
through the heat. His companion’s gold grena-
dine over purple silk and jewelled belt were quite
barbaric in splendor. A frightfully fat woman
snorted and puffed like a steam-engine in scarlet
glace silk and hat heaped up and brimming over
with poppies.
“At present fashionables wear tiny gold crosses
jingling on their neck-chains in company with
single pearls and scraps of pink coral. At her
mother’s concert t’other night Mrs. Gilbert Wilson
had a large, plain gold cross brooch-wise on the
left side of her corsage. It looks as if the Sign of
the Cross were once more to be in the running as
an ornament.
“ I only counted two pairs of long ear-pendants
on Cup Day, but Lady Smith (W.A.) sported
those funny little gold sleepers, formerly relegated
to small girls.
“ Talking of hot-looking clothes, a pair of in-
fatuated sisters paraded through all the swelter
and glare in thick grey-tweed tailor suits, scarlet
vests and parasols. Going home their poor faces
looked warm enough to frizzle eggs on.
“ Mr. and Mrs. Sidney Webb left Melbourne on
Wednesday after seeing the Melb. Cup. It was
only the third racecourse Mr. W. had been on in his
life, so he was a good subject to be impressed by
Elemington. They investigated the working of
the crack racecourse much as though it had been a
factory. Probably Byron Moore has never had
new-chum visitors who asked so many questions
about it. But the all-absorbing point of interest
for the Webbs in Victoria has not been the big
racecourse, nor the mighty minds of the politicians
they have met, nor the lavishness of the hospi-
tality, but—the wages board established under the
Peacock Factory Act. They investigated the
working of all the boards thoroughly, and in what-
ever they write about Australia will almost cer-
tainly say that this experiment is the most in-
teresting and important they have seen, and one
in which English trades can be advised to follow
in Victorian footsteps.
“Mr. and Mrs. Webb commingled with all
sorts and conditions of men in Melbourne—
with Trades Unionists and Employers’ Unionists,
May ors, politicians, workpeople, &e. ; and, in fact,
with anybody from whom they could get informa-
They were the guests of Mayor M ‘Eacharn,
at his Balaclava mansion, and chummed with
Fitz Gibbon and other magnates. The few—out-
side official persons—who met the joint authoress
of ‘ Industrial Democracy ’ were charmed with her.
‘ Brilliant and delightful ’ is Alfred Deakin’s de-
scription.
Yours affectionately,

No. 2.—The Postman.
A rural postman writes : “I desire to add this
unsolicited testimony to the sterling qualities of
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa. lam a rural postman, and
exposed to all kinds of weather. I have used Dr.
Tibbies’ Vi-Coeoa daily this last eleven months,
and I believe that I can do my work with less
fatigue since I have used it. I can honestly re-
commend to my fellow-postmen Dr. Tibbies’” Vi-
Cocoa for its sustaining qualities.”
Of late years many new manufactures, rejoicing
in a multiplicity of names, have been brought
before the public notice, but there are none which
have bounded into favor so readily as has Dr.
Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa. Throughout the country its
sales have increased with great rapidity, and an
article which only a few years back was unheard
of has now become one of the most popular
beverages of the people.
Merit, and merit alone, is what is claimed for
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, and the Proprietors are
prepared to send to any person who names The
Bulletin (a post-card will do) a dainty sample-tin
of Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa free and post-paid. There
is no magic in all this. It is a plain, honest,
straightforward offer. It is done to introduce the
merits of Vi-Cocoa into every home. Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, as a concentrated form of nourishment
and vitality, is invaluable ; nay, more than this,
for to all who wish to face the strife and battle of
life with greater endurance and more sustained
exertion, it is absolutely indispensable.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
Chemists, Croeers, and Stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-st., Sydney.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
What an eloquent man was Judge Win-
deyer ! People used literally to hang on his words.
Tommy Dodd, of Active Service Brigade
fame, is now secretary of that most respectable
institution, Sydney School of Arts debating club.
At Perth (W.A.), a few days ago, a man
got three months for forging a cheque to buy a
pistol to shoot himself with. He didn’t shoot
himself when he got the pistol—hence the trouble.
The Australian-born nephews of one of
England’s most distinguished statesmen are seldom
out of N.S.W. gaols. Their chief occupations,
when at liberty, are avoiding the police and not
avoiding stock stealing.
John Forrest, Westralia’s doughty Pre-
mier, will, on account of being in the Govern-
ment before the advent of responsible rule (i.e.,
as a quasi-imperial official), be entitled, if he
lapse from paid politics, to a life-pension of <£soo
per year.
Police-Magistrate Ranking, to be trans-
ferred from Rockhampton to Brisbane as second
P.M., is brother of Montgomery Ranking, who
some 20 years ago figured largely in London as a.
promising minor poet. P.M. Ranking was identi-
fied with the Barcoo in its very early days.
London Times, in a long review of Louis
Becke’s “ Rodman the Boat-Steerer ”: “ All the
scenes are fresh with real experience, and four or
five of them nearly reach the very high standard
which the greatest writers of short stories have
raised.” Which goes to confirm The Bulletin’s
earliest judgment concerning Becke.
The grossest libel yet committed on John
Norton was that uttered by the Mayor of Sydney
at Melbourne
He had to acknowledge that the Sydney Council had
not been of late so harmonious a body as one would wish
to preside over, but he attributed the squabble to one
gentleman, who, however, had now quietened down.
Think of it! John Norton quietened down !
“G. 8.” : Incorrect that the late P. A. C.
O’Earrell, crank solicitor (Melb.), once owned
much land in and around the city. It was
O’Farrell’s father who was rich in land, and who
devised same to the Church. Another Crotty
biz. Whicli made the sons madder than Nature
had designed them, and fashioned them into
Ishmaels.
CONTRADICTORY.—A STREET SKETCH.
Tas. Premier Braddon gets no salary. It
is customary in Tasmania for a man to take the
Premiership without pay, and not go back to his
constituents for re-election. That is what Braddon
did because of the shakiness of his seat at West
Devon. But the House sometimes gives him a
couple of hundreds for “ travelling expenses,” as he
lives up North. The other day, when the Esti-
mates were before the House, an attempt was
made to raise certain civil service screws. “ Why,”
said the Premier, “ some of these men have more
salary than Ministers of the Crown. This man has
-£6OO a-year more than I get! ”
The “eminent N.S.W. Labor-legislator
and journalist,” who lately lost his Parliamentary
billet and pickings, and thereupon took the job of
running a “ solidarity ” sheet out back, has begun
his quarrels with the local Labor panjandrums.
I he most aggressive and long the most prominent
of the latter is charmingly rocky in his spelling,
and abhors punctuation-marks, and uses capital
letters when the spirit moves, which is generally
at the wrong time. And the quarrel is so bad that
when the Aggressive Panjandrum wrote a snorting
letter of remonstrance, the Eminent Journalist
printed it, mis-spelling, unexpected capitals and
all. Characteristic.
“Kati-K. ” : Ever been
to a meeting of Swedenborgians
or theosophists, Single-taxers
or vegetarians, anarchists or
Social Purists? That’s the
place to learn that there’s some
truth in phrenology. Not a
normal head-outline in the lot.
Of course their eccentricity
may be a sign of superiority,
as is the abnormal character of
the leader in a mob of sheep.
An ordinary jumbuck would no
more think of taking the lead
than a normal man would step
out of the mud-puddles made
by his ancestors’ boots.
When the 2nd battalion
of the 14th Regt.went to Ho-
bart, under command of Col.
Dwyer, Mrs. Pysh called upon
Mrs. Dwyer and sent in her
card. The Colonel’s wife looked
hard at it. “ O’Pish ! ” she
said; “sure, Colonel, there’s
no name—av the quality, any-
way—like that in Oireland ! ”
Well, well,” said the Colonel,
“ this is the anti-podes, me
dear, and they tell me there’s
mighty quare fishes this side av
the worrld.” But when the
visitor explained that the name
was Fysh, and that the “O ”
indicated her husband’s Chris-
tian name, the brow of the
Colonel’s wife cleared some-
what. “There’s a mighty dif-
ference, me dear,” said she,
“ betwixt O’Pish and Pysh, an’
I’m glad to know ye. Is it
sherry or port, or maybe ”
and she winked. Mrs. Pysh
declined refreshment. When
she left, the Colonel’s wife re-
marked, “Well, now, that’s the
firrst fish I ever encountered
that wasn’t thirsty. But, sure,
e s a fish out of wather, poor
crayture. Them rubbishy toi-
tles, Colonel, they do be de-
moralisin’.”
Count Strezlecki, disco-
verer of Gippsland, was a the-
saurus of grim stories. One :
A paralytic millionaire had to
look on while his nurse
gathered all his valuables into

A certain N.S.W. school inspector was
about to lecture, in a South-Coast town, on “Pope
and His Contemporaries,” and sent on to the local
paper his ad., which produced a par reminding
the intending lecturer that he would have to be
mighty careful what he said about “His Holi-
ness,” as “the audience would consist largely of
the O’Dwyers, Flannigans, and Dooleys.”
The patent conferring Q.C. on B. R. Wise
runs: “We, confiding in your knowledge, experi-
ence, prudence, ability, and integrity, nominate,
constitute, and appoint you to be,” &c. To think
of Reid and Jack Want conspiring to stand
sponsors for Wise’s “experience and prudence,”
after a six months’ bitter denunciation of him as a
gaseous impracticable, without settled convictions
or coherent ideas ! The politician makes a toy of
Truth ; but the politician plus lawyer makes a
drab of her.
AtMelb. Benevolent Asylum there died, the
other day, Agnes Smith, allegedly aged 116 years.
Came out to Hobart Town in 1810—the year Gov.
Collins died. Remembered Davey, Snodgrass,
Foveaux, Paterson, Sorell, Arthur. Came over
to Port Philip in ’36, and had lived in the country
ever since. Once well-off, she had for many years
been a poorhouse inmate. A bright old dame
whose darling reminiscence was that she had
opened the first Sunday-school at Brunswick, near
Melbourne. Start her on that subject and you
were in for a penalty.
Premier Kingston has not yet received a
penny for his legal services in the Harrold Bros,
insolvency-case, now before the Adelaide Court for
18 months, and concerning which he has had
several disputes with the presiding Commissioner.
There is probably not another S.A. barrister who
would do the same thing, but Kingston’s pride as
well as his heart is in the work, and he has pub-
licly announced his intention of seeing the case
through. Yet Harrold is an ex-president of the
National Ass. and was a political opponent of
Kingston when in Parliament.
“Melb.”: P.A.C. O’Farrell undoubtedly
bolted 30 years ago. Yet he returned, and no-
body dared to wag tongue against him—any more.
Curious to note such departures and sudden pops-
up-again. The usual cause of the return is the
death of the one implacable and relentless foe.
X., the broker and ballet-philanthropist, only
went off without notice on a pleasure-excursion,
and didn’t he just put the whipcord into the
Collins-street cacklers when he turned up one
fine morning ! Z., the city-councillor and build-
ing-society man, has never come back, but will
dawn rubicund some day and shake hands with
the Exchange—and himself.
NASTY JAR.
BULLETIN MAN (out for a happy day on the harbor): “I say,
what a colossal liar that man De Rougemont is! ”
BULLETIN SLATEE (with unction) : “ Yes, by thunder, you ought
to have him on your paper!”—(And then the band
played.)
There was published some months ago, in
Queensland, a political brochure, containing an
account of Queensland Parliament. Portraits of
tioo legislators only were given, viz.—Byrnes and
Crombie. The prescience of that pamphleteer !
He seems to have seen Azrael marking out the
pair m the distance.
The phrase, “ The Guard dies, but never
surrenders,” attributed to Gen. Cambronne, was
really the invention of one Rougcmont, a tapageur
of the cafes in Waterloo days. So that the Rouge-
mont of Australia is only a second-hand liar after
all. But he may have adopted the name of his
prototype in an impulse of cynical humor.
Wragge on prayer-for-rain :
But since we cannot in meteorology give mate in three
moves to the Master Weather Man, we look on with
resignation, and endeavor to learn yet more at His
hands, instead of indulging in puerile supplication, which
could be of no more avail than if we asked Him to make
the earth rotate the other way.
The time is clearly at hand when the clergy will
seek to Put Down Wragge aforesaid.
J. H. Finlayson leaves England for Ade-
laide Nov. 8, but it is not yet certain that he will
resume the editorship of the Register on his
arrival, as he is in very bad health. Alex. Suther-
land, of Melbourne, takes his place as Register
London correspondent, but the appointment is
only temporary, and there is talk of acting-editor
Sowden being sent to fill the position.
The most conspicuous and influential
bunch of men in the public life of Queensland
seven or eight years ago were Griffith, MTI-
- Palmer, Morehead, Lilley, Drury, Hart,
Webster and Attorney-General Byrnes. They
were all colored by the Q.N. Bank. Now Byrnes,
Lilley, Palmer and Drury are dead ; MTlwraith
is at the other end of the world; Hart, Webster
and Morehead are on trial, and Griffith sits in
judgment on them.
Deeming’s house at Southern Cross, W.A.,
is now occupied by three miners. The murderer’s
name is carved over the mantel - shelf. After
Deeming’s arrest, the 20 barrels of cement found
on the premises were handed over to the local
council and used upon the Reserve cricket-pitch.
The “Cross” is a very slow place, and the local
miner never omits to point out the house and the
cricket-pitch, and tell the tale hanging thereto.
From Maoriland come accounts of Grein-
Munchausen-Rougemont, representing him as hav-
ing figured in Wellington and Nelson as a dabbler
in wonderful healing nostrums and a mystery-
monger generally. Amongst other things, it was
found that his infallible consumption-cure con-
sisted of bullock-hoof filings. But he seemed to
have been considered, on the whole, as more of a
crackbrained but clever visionary than of a design-
ing rogue.
Yic. Govt, has in four months sliced
.£llB,OOO off dead men’s estates in probate duty.
Contributors: J. L. Currie (squatter), .£47,000 ;
John Matthew Smith (lawyer), £25,000; James
Crotty (mining speculator) £ll,OOO ; Geo. Robert-
son (bookseller), £lO,OOO. The late T. C. Payne’s
£91,000, paid during last financial year, still
remains the biggest probate duty cheque on record
at the Vic. office. Next month (Nov. or Dec.)
about £27,000 will be collected from the estate of
the late Jas. Graham (merchant and ex-M.L.C.),
and about £4OOO from that of William Miller
(squatter). Altogether by next June the probate
receipts for the year promise to exceed £200,000.
“ P.A.” : Mark Twain thinks Thackeray a
bore, but people should not be too ready to
accept Mark’s opinions as gospel. He is a
humorous man, of the slaughter-house kind, to
whom the lower orders owe a great debt. His
pork-sausage and ham-and-egg banquets tickle the
vulgar palate, but when he calls the omelettes,
truffles, and turtle-soup of his superiors saw-
dust and slush, it is time to take his criticisms
with salt. There are no words in Mark’s exten-
sive vocabulary sufficiently strong to express, to
his satisfaction, his contempt and loathing of
Bret Harte. Twain’s coarse, practical mind can-
not appreciate writers so essentially refined and
gentlemanly : his favorite is the “ bluggy ”
Dickens, whose melo-dramatic slabs of pathos,
plastered with a shovel on impossible situations,
are about as convincing as the cracked paint on
the face of the 50-year-old ballet. No one, except
a man with toothache, is sure of anything. We
all like what suits us, and the fellow who differs
from us is always more or less of a fool.
“ Warrington ” : Re “ Inna’s ” comment
on Mark Twain’s depreciation of Thackeray, will
you let me suggest that no worshipper of Mark
Twain can be expected to take much interest in
Thackeray’s characters. By the way, “ Inna ”
knows very little about W.M.T., or he would not
identify him with “ Vanity Fair ” —a compara-
tively amateurish work. “ Pendennis ” and
“ The Newcomes ” are novels which might have
interested even the author of “ Innocents Abroad ”
(a fairly amusing book for boys), if he were not
incapable of admiring anybody save himself. Do
you remember that Twain snorted at Bret Harte ?
Can you mention the name of any popular institu-
tion that Twain hasn't snorted at ?
The newly-appointed Mayor of a big Vic.
country town was recently gazetted as a Jaypee
in compliment to his position, and, by way of
pleasing th’ould man, the town clerk took in two
documents for him to initial in his new capacity.
“Just initial them there,” he said. “Nishul
’em ? ” cried the perplexed beak. “Fwhat’s that?”
The town clerk stood perplexed for a moment,
then he drew his thumb across the top of the
paper and said, “Just write two P’s there.” His
Worship seeming to comprehend, the official left
the room, and later in the day collected the docu-
ments, to find that “ Tupees” had been laboriously
scrawled across half the entire paper.
S.A.’s Lieut.-Governor, Chancellor of the
University and Chief Justice, Way, D.C.L.,Q.C.,
is probably the only man in the world occupying a
position of that importance who belongs to the
Bible Christian sect. W. S. Lilly, in the Nine-
teenth Century, gives a capital sketch of the
average service at the average little “Ebenezer”
chapel of the denomination ; and adds that he
can’t understand an educated man, or one of
refinement, belonging to the cult. But, anyhow,
it was the “ chapel ” of Way’s father —Way, sen.,
was the pioneer parson of the small flock in S.A.—
and the little man has shown grit in hanging to it
despite the remarkable pulpit and prayer-meetin’
antics of its host of unpaid and illiterate local
preachers and prayer-leaders. It is Way’s influ-
ence as much as anything—more than anything—
which puts the sect among the first half-dozen in
point of adherents in S.A., while in the other
Australias it is either almost unknown fas in
N.S.W.) or very weak (as in Vic.).
Gilbert Smith, pressman, came down.from
Coolgardie to Perth very sick. Lodged at a quiet
pub. kept by a decent Irish woman. One evening
had a fainting spell. Mrs. Rafferty, knowing
nothing of newspaper men, thought her lodger
was dying and sent for a priest to pass him along.
When Smith recovered from the swoon he beheld
the father “ performing.” “ Why, what’s this ?” he
cried. “Whist,” whispered the landlady, “my
poor man, you’re gettin’ the last rites.” “Rites!”
ejaculated the scribe, sitting up—“l’m not a
Catholic ! ” “ Not a Catholic ! ” exclaimed the
priest, corking up the holy water. “ Not a
Catholic ! ” screamed the landlady, bursting into
tears. “Don’t cry, Mrs. Rafferty,” said Smith.
“Mistakes will happen. It was very kind of you;
but, anyhow, let us have drinks- I’m not the first
to impose upon the church. What’s yours,
father? ” And so genially did he conciliate the
good father that the latter went home reconciled
to the fact that a pagan had almost cheated him out
of the sacrament.

The lately-deceased Henry Barkly was
made the victim of a legislative experiment, while
Governor of Victoria. Hotham’s salary was
.£15,000, and Barkly started at the same figure—
.£lo,ooo as salary and £5OOO as allowances. But
Victoria was gathering sense, and one day a Bill
was hurried through Parliament fixing the Gov.’s
salary at £7OOO, and wholly wiping out allowances.
The Bill, too, was made to apply at once to
Barkly. This Barkly pronounced a breach of
faith, and, as Parliament was obdurate, he for-
warded his resignation to England. But the
royal assent was refused to the Bill, and Barkly
was urged to hold on and fight his Ministers into
“reason.” The outcome was that Parliament
agreed to make the salary £lO,OOO, but to pay
nothing as allowances. Barkly acquiesced, with-
drew his resignation, invited his Ministers to
dinner, and, soon after, the whole of Parliament
to a Govt. House ball.

Mt. Gambier, one of the few picturesque
places in South Australia, has given over talking
about its fathomless lake and its Gordon monu-
ment, and its conversation now runs to cow. The
local agricultural society offered a prize, of course
for the best milker, and a steward was told off
to see each competing quadruped drained. A
respectable oitizen, who hasn’t so far been accused
pi mixing spirit with his milk, put in the astonish-
mg report that a solitary beast had yielded no less
than 4A,lb. u ''* k » showing, according to the recog-
msed tester 13,3 per cent, of butter fat-that is,
.J T lb butter for one milking ! The other stewards
aie holding over the animal—the cow, not the
steward—for further consideration, and meantime
there is talk of floating her into a limited liability
~ **7,200 shares, to be registered as the Old
Bnndle Butter Factory.

“ Boleg “ Rick ”is pretty right about
the diversity of tastes in cows, though the subur-
ban animal is not the type. Funny, for all that
that no cow however hungry, will touch another’s
leavings, while a horse will unhesitatingly grab
all he can reach. Another thing—when a cow
dies, and has paid off the last dog and crow rich
grass springs up to mark the sacred spot. Yet
when cattle come along, perhaps starving (mostly
( 10watkl y s )i they disdain the grass, but chew
the bones by the hour with great relish. Respect
tor the dead, or just pure cussedness? Ever
notice, by the way, resemblance of vaccine obse-
quies to Hibernian wake? Crowd round dear
departed, one starts bellowing, rest take it up •
then they wade in and fight without any regard to
age, sex, or statement of claim. Writer and co.
have more than once brought two bulls together
in the presence of a lately-defunct. Are always
assured of our money’s worth of “ scrap.” Death
makes all (live) bulls equal.

* * * *
Many years ago, a big tree at Omeo bore
the inscription “ Horatio Nelson Rowley died of
snake-bite. Horatio was out prospecting, lost
bis horse and “ tucker,” and had to swim a flooded
river with all his clothes strapped on his head,
■bn r &ute, a floating log came in contact with the
duds, ’ and carried them clean away, so that,
when he landed, he was in a terrible fix. There
was a house in the distance, which, after dark, he
approached, only to be dog-chased for his life.
1 a , cou P le days he hallooed himself hoarse,,
but the occupants of the “humpy” couldn't or
wouldn t hear. Every night for a week he made
to that house, with the result that the dogs got a
bit of him each time. At last, in despair of ever
getting assistance, and being too weak and foot-
sore to travel, he made up his mind to die. Then
it struck him that his friends wouldn’t like it to
get about that he had suicided ; hence the inscrip-
tion executed with a sharp stone. Afterwards,
somebody came along and found him unconscious,
and he lived to be a great joke in that part of the
country.

“ Noirmont ” : Was once way-back with a
rheumatic old boy, who had been advised to try
iguana oil. We went to neighboring aboriginal
camp, and asked one of the men if he could catch
an iguana. “ Yohi! ” he said, “mine catch it
that t teer fl u ' c k.” He got a rod about 12ft. long,
and fixed on one end a noose of stout string.
I hen, having spotted a “goanna,” he walked
slowly towards it, with rod at arm’s length. The
iguana, fixing its gaze on the rod, remained per-
still, and allowed the noose to be passed
over its head. The rest was easy. Have since
done same thing myself.
* * • *
, “ 0.P.” : Just finished investigating the
’roo-tail question and beg to say Yes-No 1 Tail
occasionally touches ground by accident, but ’roo’s
construction shows that tail was intended to
counterbalance the leaning fore-quarters and keep
the animal’s equilibrium, by swinging up and
down in concord with his motions. Thus, when
he lands from a jump, the tail swings down and
strikes a balance with the fore-quarters and enables
him to get off again with the return swing.
Jumping with weights is an adaptation of this
principle. As a bit of smart locomotive mechanism
the ’roo takes a lot of wiping out.

“M‘6.” : Recently saw a Murray cod in
Melbourne which was a splendid sample of self-
reliance and business ability. The fish, caught at
Eofiuca, had taken the bait while it held in its
mouth, half-swallowed, the latter end of a carpet-
snake. The catcher said the snake was still
wriggling when he landed its devourer.

Yang Anyut ” : Re superfluous limbs (8.,
15/10/98). Had a tame chi-chak lizard, which
used to come on the table for crumbs, and a friend
ot mine, not knowing its status, banged table to
scare it away, and as usual with terrified lizards,
it dropped tail on table. Tor days it kept away,
but finally returned ; and a few weeks later, not
one, but two sprouts appeared, developing in the
end into two full-sized tails. Eventually cat ate
the curio. Have seen a five-legged hen (in Shang-
hai museum), a three-legged duck, in Sandakan,
and a four-antlered deer, in Malacca.

Glass bottles lying amongst dry grass, with
a hot sun shining on them, frequently cause bush-
fires. More than one instance is known also of
fire originating through a spark struck from a
stone by a horse s hoof. A Flinders River man
was credited, years ago, with lighting four fires in
a mile in this way. Sceptics ridiculed the idea,
saying he dropped matches ; but his mates swore
they saw the sparks fly from the hoofs of his gal-
loping horse. e

“ Baldy ” Davis, M.P. for Bourke, some
years ago bought a Queensland station from a
certain distinguished poetess. It doesn’t seem in
accordance with the eternal fitness of things that
a poetess should be a boss cocky in private life,
but that s the way The Bulletin heard the story,
Wii' if Bo ' 11 ® t 0 tell the y arn . i n its own way.
AA ’ 77 - met Baldy in Sydney lately,
and confided to him that there were on the Q.
property spacious limestone caves containing
almost inexhaustible deposits of a substance which
she had always believed to be asphaltum. Davis
had a small sample sent to Sydney, and John
Haynes, who knows everything and whose ap-
proaching marriage made him cheerfully optimis-
tic, said that of course it was asphaltum—any
tool could see that at a glance. The delighted
Davis set about forming a syndicate to participate
in the untold riches of the great discovery. Bar-
ton said he would take a share, and Waddell and
other Parliamentarians did likewise, and then
somebody sent a sample to the Govt, analyst, and
he pricked the bubble. It was merely marsupial
excreta.

“Pmpi”: The largest deposit of kauri
gum ever found ? One big find was made 25 years
ago on the Collarbone Spur, Thames, M.L. There
was a great moss-grown boulder a few feet off the
track, on which countless prospectors had rested.
One, more curious than his predecessors, chipped
away the moss with his tomahawk, and found the
boulder was a solid lump of gum worth over £6O.

“ Trebless ” : Have often eaten large white
tree-grub of Western N.S.W. Difficulty is to get
delicacy out of hole without smashing it. You
spike the grub with pointed twig, take him to fire,
and toast him. I have seen a white man eat
them uncooked and alive.

“Kink”: “S.H.” asks how the young
scrub turkey gets out of his incubator. Let
S.H.” dig up a chick, put him inside his shirt to
carry him home, and lie’ll know all about it. If
there isn’t a railway tunnel scratched right
through him it will he very adjacent. Eggs are
laid anywhere about the heap of dirt, sticks and
leaves. One heap is used by all the female tur-
keys within cooee.

Let there be no mincing the matter. There
may have been a mam as well as a bull in the
Suffield case, but that man was by no means
necessarily the accused Suffield. The Bulletin
asks the public to read the evidence as disclosed
in the pamphlet published by this office, and see
whether a plausible alternative theory does not
stick out half-a-mile. The Suffield pamphlet will
be mailed from Bulletin office to any address for
7d. stamps.
At Flagstaff Garden (Syd.) recently, four
Chinkies were disputing with two small Austra-
liennes possession of a clover-patch. One little
Chow: “lour father don’t pay for the bluggy
park. What you father do?” Young Australia
(proudly): “ Our father’s a capting-—capting of a
big ferry steamer.” Small Chow, triumphantly :
“ Yah ! He don’t pay. My father hawker ; he
pay license. You clea out! ” And he calmly
took possession.

The N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
W £ a lrr ha ® lon ff troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
ts heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
mumcate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though _ the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.*

“0.P.” : Very many shearers look upon
the parson’s advent as a regular God-send
in the wrong sense, and regard his ministra-
tions purely in the light of an entertainment. A
parson struck our shed this year, backed up to the
shearers’-hut fireplace, and opened up. “ The
Nap. push ” kept on playing, and the rest of ns
settled ourselves along the table and tried to work
up a fitting frame of mind. Got on all right until
someone outside struck up “ Annie Rooney” on a
concertina, and evoked a torrent of blasphemous
expostulation. The parson stuck like a man,
though, and saw the thing through. Then he
suggested a little hymn, and started us on “ Work
for the night is coining.” It was a ridiculous
spectacle. The poor cleric, with cracked voice
and earnest face, bawling his level best, and a
ghastly chorus, squeaky and howling, from 90 odd
throats following about half a note behind and a
note or two on both sides. Then the parson tried
another hymn, but the crowd vehemently de-
manded “ Work for it’s gettin’dark” again, and
he gave it them over and over again, smiling
serenely all the time, and woefully unconscious of
any joke till he was fairly pumped out. Then he
began to spout, but, before he had finished, there
wasn’t a soul in the hut. He had even choked off
the gamblers.
“N.A.T. ” : Has any readei
ever come across persons immune
from snake-poisoning ? Have heard
of several such cases, but have no
personal knowledge of any. There’s
an old chap at Mathinna (Tas.) who,
it is alleged, laughs snake-bite to
scorn because he possesses infallible
antidote. At all events, he handles
all varieties of the reptiles quite
fearlessly, and submits to be bitten
hy any casual snake that a sceptic
may bring along. Generally, also,
carries a loose one or two in his
pocket, and occasionally makes a
stir by suddenly yanking a “ coiler ”
out amongst a crowd. Doesn’t
apparently yearn to make capital
out of alleged antidote, but pro-
mises to confide secret to a resident
when he’s going to peg-out.

A Unique Australian Weapon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Compabativelt little is known of that extra-
ordinary Australian aboriginal implement, the
werpoo, a piece of emu or kangaroo leg-bone,
worked down to a long, thin, sharp instrument,
of which the thickest part to be used is not more
than about Jin. wide and l-16in. thick, the whole
The illustration
shows the two kinds
of werpoo - hollow
and solid.
being from 10 to 15 inches
long. It is usually carried in
a wrapping of emu-feathers
and fastened in a waist-belt
of 50 to 100 strands of hu-
man hair—the kallote. The
instrument is contrived with
devilish ingenuity to take
human life in a manner that
leaves no direct trace of in-
jury. The murtpoona (mur-
derer) having craftily stalked
his man, the victim is so
seized that he cannot call
out, and thrown on his back.
The operator’s companion
then holds up the legs and
the killer inserts the instru-
ment just underneath the
skin and steadily forces it in
a. parallel direction for some
little distance, after which
the point is adroitly inclined
towards the heart or lungs
and pressed until the desired
vital part is pierced. The
implement is then carefully
withdrawn and the crescent-
shaped puncture caused by
the hollowed-out bone is
kept so tightly closed by
thumb-pressure that it soon
becomes imperceptible. I
have known a clever surgeon
examine a man killed in this
way without success until,
when informed of the use of
the werpoo, he discovered,
after diligent search, a small
puncture, which he probed
without finding any sign of
injury more than skin-deep.
Yet when he opened the
body he found the heart had
been pierced by some sharp
and slender instrument, the
position of the wound, how-
ever, not corresponding with
the external mark. Further
examination, nevertheless,
... showed that the outward
almost - invisible puncture was the entrance
point of the werpoo, which had been so cleverly
manipulated afterwards that, if one person pre-
sent had not known of the instrument, murder
would never have been suspected. A family I
knew well lost a. member in this way, the victim
being under the influence of liquor when seized by
some natives who had marked him out to be
“ werpoo d.’’ The instrument was inserted
at the base of the neck and then forced
in an irregular line down to the heart. The
reason given by the blacks for the use of the
werpoo is their belief that when a man is killed in
this particular fashion “him never comeback.”
“That one pieanninny hole too small”; “that
one not get out ” ; the idea being that from such
a small wound so neatly closed up the spirit of the
man could not come back to earth to frighten
them. “Suppose blackfellow kill whitefellow,” a
black explained to me, “ make big hole ; then
that one soon come out along a hole, and when
blackfellow sleep that one sit on him belly and
baal let him up. Blackfellow call ’em all about
blackfellow, but no one hear him—that one sit too
ebby. After long time him go way. My word,
blackfellow big one tired and big one prightened.”
HARRY STOCKDALE.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ The Parliament for God ” is the war-cry of
the S.A. Christian Endeavor people, who are already
making a noise in opposition to the totalisator. The
agitation—which is in view of the '99 Assembly elections
—apparently raises the question of whether the Deity
favors the tote or the man with the bag. The latter, in
S.A. at least, has dropped the bag, pawned his diamond
ring, and ekes out a precarious existence as an illicit tote-
runner in the sewer down the alley, which seems to sug-
gest that Providence rather favors the totalisator at pre-
sent, anyway. The parsons rely on the woman’s vote
to give them a majority on the subject. Three mem-
bers of the Ministry—Kingston and the two O’Lough-
lins—favor the totalisator, but Messrs. Holder, Jenkins,
and Butler are against it, and so last year the Govt, made
the motion for its abolition an open question.
* * * *
Bookmaker v. Tote. Broken Hill being in
N.S.W., the machine is illegal there, yet the S.A. races
(on which the tote is legal) are the Barrier’s most import-
ant sporting fixtures (except, of course, the two Cups).
Local bookmakers do biz. with punters at “ tote odds ; ”
but—with a £lO limit. So, even if Rocket, a rank out-
sider, wins Adelaide Cup and pays £439165. the B.H. book
only pays over a “tenner.” So strongly has this fact
been borne home on bettors that they are unanimous in
support of the machine, and if ever a vote is taken B.H.
will yell a thundering “ Aye.”
* * • *
N.S.W. Leg. Council, being desirous of finding
out all about the tote, appointed a select committee to sit
on it. The L.O. has done so in more way's than one, with
the help of the churohes. Evidence was invited on the
subject and a lot of condemnatory parsons and other
laborers in the vineyard came along and damned the
machine. That the godly men knew' nothing about it is
only a circumstance. The A.J.O. secretary also testified ;
what he said is of little import, for he cannot have had
any personal knowledge of the people’s machine-made
bookmaker, and, anyhow, the A.J.C. cares precious little
for anyone whose bets are necessarily limited to half-a-
sov. The formation of the select committee w'as also
peculiarly adapted to the dishing of the tote, for there
does not seem any particular reason for such men as Hon.
Bill Long yearning after anything likely to be popular
with public punters ; and as for some other members
of the committee, to them betting in all shapes is ana-
thema maranatha. Moreover, it is stated no evidence
was taken on the bed-rock of figure facts, i.e., the com-
parison between what the public gets in the W'ay of prices
from Mo and what the tote gives. Seeing that all
such evidence must be hugely in favor of the tote, it was
just what the select committee probably didn’t want to
hear or let anybody else hear. As for the great help the
tote has been to the hospitals of Queensland —such an in-
teresting item was not even mentioned, more by reason
that said committee does not seem to have been aware that
the tote went to Queensland in 1884 and has stayed there
ever since. And so the select committee has closed its
labors : and to the ordinary person not of Pecksniff-tram-
melled intellect it just appears that the select committee,
beyond listening to the cackling of a lot of male old
women, did nothing in particular and did it very well.
* « • *
It was at Hampden races, Maoriland, in the
flush times of the early Sixties. For the chief event,
the stakes to be paid on the course, five came out. It
was at the close of the day'’ssport, when the “ three-star”
had put in its work. To maintain the “ uprightness ”
essential to the judge, that official, a miner, was securely'
bound to the winning-post with a rope, otherwise he
would have fallen down on the globe. An excellent race
resulted in so close a finish as to cause a rush for the
judge’s verdict. Thus awakened suddenly from a doze as
the last of the beaten horses passed the post, the official
promptly pointed a paralytic finger at the final animal
and said “’e’s won.” And the money was paid over
accordingly.
* * • *
Though many are in gaol, and a still larger
number are discredited, Melbourne has never been pro-
perly able to shake off the Ohadbandic influence of her
boomsters, and consequently the most ridiculous and ex-
traordinary prosecutions for gambling-offences are weekly'
cropping up. A few days ago, the licensee of aßichmond
hotel was fined for running a shilling sweep, though it is
well-known that she has had the same sweep going for the
past 15 years, and has every year devoted the whole of
the profits to charity. For her trouble, the woman is
now made to pay £lO and has her hotel branded as a
common gaming-house, while the ultimate result will be
merely that humanity will suffer a bit more in future It
is not the detectives who are to blame for these prosecu-
tions. They are themselves watched like criminals bv
busybodies of the W.C.T.U. and kindred institutions.

Judge Boucaut (S. A.) in giving judgment in a
tote-shop appeal, the other day, said as .or himself he
had no knowledge whatever of betting, and had never
been interested in a bet or a lottery. Another Adelaide
man, Dr. T. K. Hamilton, the eminent eye-specialist,
lately confessed that he had never plaj r ed a game of
cards, had never been in a theatre, and had seen but one
horse-race in all his life.
* * * •
“F. 0.”: Re Jordan, draughts champion. The
game is, no doubt, puerile and slow, but Jordan is none
the less a master. He has three times—in Scotland—
played 22 opponents, himself blindfold, on one occasion
winning seven games and drawing the rest. He is also a
chessplayer of no mean ability, and, so far, has not found
a man in Melbourne who can even draw with him at the
royal game—though that may not be saying much. Is
a quiet, unassuming young fellow of 26, a hatter by trade.
The trouble in his case is that he came out here without
a manager, and he has had to practically “ run ” himself
and doesn’t yet know the ropes.
* * » •
Amusing to note how the Sydney dailies go on
printing cabled accounts of pugilistic struggles in distant
climes, although they absolutely refuse to mention local
fights in any way. Having paid for the fistic items from
oversea the papers seem to oonsider it their duty to print
them and a lot of other useless matter, to the exclusion
of good local news. The first daily that breaks away
from the cable amalgam will have a glorious opportunity
to show up its rivals in the European news department.
One day last week The Bulletin counted six items in the
Sydney dailies which would scarcely have been published
if received from local sources.
• * * *
It was after his second drawn battle with Iron-
bark Burge that a well-known clever boxer swore a deep
and awful oath that he would never go into the ring with
him again. But bad times and much persuasion at last
prevailed, and they met yet again. The clever one made
it hot from the start, with the usual result: he knocked
out —himself. “How are you getting on with him,
Jack ? ” asked a friend of the soienced man when he came
baok to his corner. “ How am I getting on with him 1"
he said, in a voice full of savage self-contempt. “ Here I
am again «o the ring with this : and I haven’t got an
axe.”

Cobbett, heckled by a barrister as to a
date said —“ It was the day when you told me
Lord Sidmouth trotted out his daughters so that
one of them might fall in love with you.”
Mr. W. J. Lyne lately entertained N.S. W.
Assembly with an anecdote about a jury, of which
he was one. • Eleven of the good men and true
had agreed, but the odd juryman held out, and
there was a chance of a lock”up when one of the
11 doffed his coat and prepared to “convince”
the odd man, who soon became “ even ” with the
rest. Mr. Lyne does not say which of the two he
was—the “one” or the “ Peter Jackson.”
Melbourne’s champion drownist was found
in the Yarra, the other day. Yarra-bank anarchists
and demolishers were just beginning to demolish
on that Sunday morning when a man was noticed
struggling in the water. Every cop. who is put
“on juty ” in that neighborhood can swim, his
chief business being to interfere with the liberty
of the subject who wants to drown himself, so
Policeman M'Guinness speedily extracted the
man. He said he fell in by accident, so he was
dried and sent home. Pour hours later Policeman
Grogan dragged the same man out of the river at
the same place, and, as he seemed to be meeting
with too many accidents, he was locked up. Which
spoiled a very interesting attempt at putting up a
record.
The Boston Shower.
The greatest luxury one can wish for after a day’s
work on the farm or among stock is a good bath.
Unfortunately, in few rural dwelllings is the water
laid on so that on returning from the fields at
night the wished-for bath may be indulged in.
The Boston Shower has been invented to meet
this want, and it has many and great recom-
mendations. It is ready at a moment’s notice;
easily put in operation ; can be used with either
cold or hot water, and, above all, it is extremely
cheap. The Boston Shower (of which Messrs. M.
Moss & Co., the old established merchants of
Sydney and Melbourne, are the proprietors) may
be set up by anyone, and any corner in a shed
will serve for the purpose if there is no bathroom
on the place. All that is wanted is a bucket of
cold or warm water, and the bath is ready. The
block showing this patent shower illustrates how
it is set up. The patent consists in a most in-
genious pumping arrangement, enclosed in a metal
disc about 4ft. 6in. from the ground. A short
handle projects from this disc, and by working
this backwards and forwards a continuous shower
is produced. The action of the pump is remark-
ably easy, and requires scarcely an effort to force
the water into the shower ; at the same time it is
so powerful that it will throw water 150ft. This
means to comfort and health is within the reach
of the smallest selector in the country. The price
of the “Boston Shower” is only 37s. (id., and
when packed weighs 451bs. Messrs. M. Moss and
Co. will, on application, be pleased to forward
further particulars. .


HOT WEATHER DRINKS.
Man’s thirst in days of heat and toil
Is boundless as the sea;
But once the BILLY ’gins to boil,
The fragrant BILLY TEA
Cures cark and care, and stills turmoil,
And satisfied is he.
Then, if on Social pleasures bent,
When friend would meet with friend,
You seek for means of quiet content
A pleasant hour to spend,
Of all the gifts that gods have sent
None beats “ White Heather ” Blend.

SYDNEY and BRISBANE;
Agents for “ White Heather ” Whisky,
Oranuma and Fodah Porridge Meals
Kuppers Elberfeld Lager.
The Mining Broker’s “ Tip. 1
Mark Twain is credited in saying “ Take all the
advice you are offered and then do as you please.”
This terse axiom generally applies, and the public
have found this specially applicable to tips from
mining brokers. There are, however, exceptions,
and a member of the well-known mining brokers
of Davis and Co., 407 Collins-street, Melbourne—
Mr. T. Andrews—recommends something from
which he received satisfactory results, in which he
still has confidence, and from which thousands of
Australians have benefited. We quote his letter
for the benefit of our general readers. “ I can
strongly recommend Warner’s Safe Care. I have
been a great sufferer from liver complaint, and
after taking a few bottles the change for the better
was wonderful.”
