POLITICAL
POINTS
The Queensland Ministry has lately won
a couple of bye-elections—purely through the
plural-vote.
Writes F. Kidd, an erstwhile New Aus-
tralia enthusiast, from Paraguay : “ My opinion
about things in general in this world has greatly
changed. We are queer people; not two agree on
anything.”
Outside of ministers, Higgins is probably
the best-reported man in Vic. Assembly, and
should either of the weak men of the Govt. —Duffy
or Williams—go, he will, despite his atrabilious
temperament, have a good show for the vacancy.
Although Perth Morning Herald pro-
fesses to be against the Forrest Govt., it is locally
supposed to do and say very much what the Pre-
mier wants. Brother Alick is chairman of direc-
tors and biggest shareholder, and—“ blood is
thicker than water.”
Murderer Butler’s extradition from the U.
States figures on theN.S. W. Estimates of Expendi-
ture at £4418. The French Estimates disclose an
expenditure of £7OOO for a late extradition, and
the British Estimates £BOOO for an extradition—
both from the U. States.
The Forrest crowd is still determined to
bring all Westralian trade through Perth. Ukased
that in future the gold-escort from Lawler’s will
go via Menzies, thence, of course, to the capital.
Formerly it went via Cue to Geraldton, a much
shorter distance and better travelling.
J. W. Taverner, Vic. Minister of Bacon
and Butter, will, according to opinion in his
Kerang constituency, go to England shortly to
represent his province. Already two candidates
are loudly demanding the seat that isn’t yet va-
cant —one of them in the interests of the Mallee
and Mildura.
Four candidates rushed for the lately-
suicided Larnach’s seat at Tuapeka (M.L.),
and out of 2496 votes polled, the Tory,
Rawlins, came on top with 1012. If Maoriland
had the second ballot, probably the Govt, would
have retained the seat, but the 1284 Liberal votes
were split among three candidates, while the Tory
vote went solid. This reduces Seddon’s majority
to about nine, and three of these are none too
reliable.
The most combative man in Q. Parlia-
ment is Chas. M’Donald, member for Flinders.
Whoever may fail in pluck among the Northern
Democrats, McDonald never does ; and he ham-
mered away at the Q.N.B. business when hardly
another Labor-member dared open his mouth.
M‘Donald has also made a specialty of constitu-
tional procedure and Parliamentary practice, and
recently bowled ove* Premier Dickson twice in
succession.
Perth (W.A.) is making a row over its
Water Supply Board, and complains, among other
things, that the conduct of a member, who is
charged with selling an old pump of his own,
worth £250, to the Board for £750, wasn’t all that
it should be. Also, there are objections about an
item of £671, which is said to represent a wharf-
man’s wages for four months. But things like
these will happen. Every boom is decorated with
such episodes.
Sydney D.T. Is getting ready to oppose
the amended Federal Constitution at the next
Referendum campaign. Commenting on the
Reid Budget (which imposes new Customs taxa-
tion) the D.T. remarked : “ The people will now
have a taste of what they may expect under
Federation.” S.M. Herald is now mildly apolo-
getic of Reidiosyncrasies. The D.T. is already
barking, and may soon bite the hand it has been
shaking for four years.
Thanks mainly to Vosper, M.L.A., the
Bill brought in by John Forrest’s little b’rer, Alex.,
and Sept. Burt, to amend the W.A. Immigration
Restriction Act by doing away with the tonnage
regulations, was shelved for six months. Alex.
Forrest protested that the N.W. station holders
would be ruined if the Bill was not passed, alleging
that it was impossible to get white labor up there.
Whereupon Vosper produced a letter from a
couple of shearers alleging that they had been
refused work on one of brother Alex.’s stations
and immediately afterwards Chows were put on.
Which blew out the Bill’s chance.
Democracy goes ahead in Tasmania like a
house on fire. During latest session the Upper
House (elected by about 7000 owners of property)
managed to snavel for its constituents some
£30,000 worth of relief from taxation. The new
centralisation-of-police law, by charging to general
revenue the amount formerly raised by a 9d.
police rate on real estate, saves the landlords
£16,000 a year. The elimination of all but a
dividend tax from the Ministry’s income-tax pro-
posals saves Fatman £BOOO formerly levied on
wealth. University increases and other strictly
upper-circle perks make up the balance of the
£30,000 above-mentioned. The position now, as
officially stated, is that only 15 per cent, of Tas.
revenue will be derived from direct taxation, and
the other 85 per cent, from indirect taxation.
Necessaries of life are dearer than in any of
the other provinces ; the spending power of
the people is less than in any other province ;
and Fysh’s poetical “ wave of wonderful pros-
perity” threatens to swamp the somnolent fly-
speck at any moment.
‘K.K.” : The European’s
dislike to the Chinaman is not s
matter of taste, but of healthj
racial instinct. It is the sam«
survival of an out-of-date instinct
as is evidenced in a horse’s instinc-
tive dread at the smell of timers.
The smell of Chinaman differs
from that of European, and
awakens that sense of distrust in-
herited from pre-historic experi-
ences of the ancestor to whom a
man with a different smell meant
also a man with a club and a wish
to use it on him. In the case of
the Chinkies, this out-of-date
instinctive dislike has lasted long
enough to be useful again as a pro-
tection against a race that is
more dangerous to civilisation
than a savage with a club is to a
fellow savage.
One of the bitterest fights
made by the Tory party in M.L.
House of Reps, in the matter of the
Old Age Pensions Bill, was with regard to
the name of the measure. The Dreary party
fought for all it was worth to have the measure
described as a Charitable Bill, and to call the pen-
sions “relief,” and to have it understood that the
people concerned were not pensioners, but pau-
pers. Which is a beautiful characteristic of that
kind of party. The pensions would cost just the
same, but it is the joy of being able to howl
“ pauper!” after the aged recipient that is dear
to the soul of the Man of Fat.
The secrecy of the ballot. A Parliamen-
tary candidate, who stood for Stanley (S.A.), re-
ceived but very few votes, with one plumper
among them. When returning thanks, he offered
to give £lO to the sensible man who had recorded
that plumper if he would reveal himself. After a
little delay, an elector rose and claimed the money.
“ You are a liar,”said the candidate—“l plumped
for myself ! ”
Last year King O’Malley’s Bill, giving
a widow an absolute claim on enough of the old
man’s estate to provide her with a sufficient main-
tenance, was rejected in S.A. Assembly by a
majority of three. This year a still larger idea,
giving both the widow and children a claim on the
estate for maintenance (in case the house-father
has bequeathed his pile to the church or to a
Flossie, and left his family unprovided for) was
endorsed by a majority of 26. Even this slow
world moves a little now and then.
The annual holiday which Premier Reid
promised so cordially just before the last N.S.W.
election (the police vote being strong in his
electorate) appears to have many flies on it, after
all. Sunday Brunker explains that the bobby
must apply for his annual 14 days leave and give
“ some reason why his request should be granted.”
If the tired and heavy Robert is to have his holi-
day as a matter of right like any other public ser-
vant, why should he have to “ give reasons?”
Maloney, backed by Trenwith, Kirton,
Hancock and the Democratic party generally,
raised the Old Age Pension question last week in
Vic. Assembly. Premier Turner got angry after
a while, and cast the stale fish of contumely at
them. His case is a pretty hard one and is some-
thing like this : “ I believe in Old Age Pensions,
but they would cost £250,000 a year and I have to
find the money. The Council wouldn’t pass a Tax
Bill for such a purpose, and it wouldn’t pass a
Pension Bill either. I don't know how to shift
the Council, and the people who tell me I should
do it never show me how it is to be done.” After
all, talking about Democratic programmes in Vic.
Assembly is about as barren as sowing beans on a
pyramid. There is only one reform worth con-
sidering in Victoria : the firing-out of the Council
which makes all reform impossible.
A BACK-HAIR VIEW.
THE ELDER SISTER : “ You may think I don’t know anything, but I
could give you a few wrinkles."
THE YOUNGER : “ I daresay—and not miss them either!”
Poor old Duncan Gillies is a sorry figure
in the Vic. Assembly to-day. All his former
vigor and force have departed, leaving him merely
a driveller in a lost cause living on his old-time
reputation. To every suggestion he disagrees with
Gillies now raps out the old phrase, “Playing to
the gallery,” with sickening reiteration. That one
sentence is apparently the best thing in the way
of epigram that the late Agent-General has at his
command. In fact, Gillies is not only the “late
Agent-General ” ; to all intents and purposes he is
“ the late Gillies.”
A LARGE ORDER .
CONFIDENT OWNER: “ Now, Tom, you have your
instructions. If you’re tackled by anything
this side of the distance-post, you must sit
down on the horse and fairly LIFT him home.
We’re all on him.”
The charge made by George Hutchison,
M.H.R., that one of Premier Seddon’s deceased
relatives had been a dishonest person when he
was alive, and other things of the kind, moved
Seddon to call for a formal enquiry, and Hutchi-
son was invited to present his evidence and
blacken the deceased’s character in proper legal
form. But though the court of enquiry sits in a
patient sort of way, Hutchison never turns up.
Nor does he seem inclined to present his evidence
to the House itself. Even his own party doesn’t
drink with Hutchison now —at least not very
often.
Judging from all the hysterical gun-cackle
from Maoriland, it would really seem that M.L.
contemplates declaring war against Prance
The leave of all members of the Permanent Artillery
has been stopped. All the fortifications are now' manned,
and steps have been taken to provision the men for an
extended period. Ammunition has been overhauled, and
Martini Henry rifles are being issued to the new corps in
Wellington in place of Sniders. As regards the volunteers,
transport and victualling arrangements have been made.
. . . Precautionary measures have been adopted by the
defence authorities in all the chief ports of the province.
The fires are being kept banked on two steamers ready in
case of emergency to lay a mine-field at the entrance of
Wellington harbor.
The Kingston (S.A.)
Govt, doesn’t favor the
suggestion of Caldwell,
M.P., that public accounts
should be periodically in-
vestigated by a committee
of finance. Why the ave-
rage Treasurer opposes
enquiry into his adminis-
tration is, perhaps, not
such a mystery after all,
considering the methods
prevailing. A good Trea-
surer is worth a lot of
votes to his party, and
often a good Treasurer is
simply a Treasurer who
hasn’t been too closely in-
spected.
Conciliation in indus-
trial disputes is of com-
paratively little use unless
the arbitrator is allowed
to conciliate with a club.
In Maoriland the State
provides a Conciliation
Court to adjudicate on in-
dustrial disputes, but,
though its decisions are
almost always accepted, it
happens now and then
that the losing party re-
fuses to acknowledge the
award. So a brief and
logical Bill now before
Parliament provides for
the fining of that insurrec-
tionary loser anything up
to £5OO. Where it is the
trades-union which kicks,
that body’s funds may be
seized to make good the
fine, and, if they are in-
sufficient, a levy, of as
much as <£lo a member
may be made to fill the
gap. Which is a level-
headed idea. Conciliation in industrial disputes
is a great idea, and has saved Maoriland many
thousands in strike losses. But it must be accom-
panied with power to flatten out the party who
won’t conciliate.
At N.S.W. general election, the con-
stituencies were implored by the Ereetrade press
not to return Protectionist candidates, as such a
course would inevitably lead to their “tinkering
with the tariff,” if they achieved office. The
reptile press, however, forgot that Premier Reid
could be his own tinker at the very shortest notice.
■ N.S.W. Premier Reid says he won t take
the Agent-Generalship—“ even to get away from
Crick.”
Besides Fane De Salis, W. B. Dailey, and
John Haynes, the late Henry Parkes
duced a Bill into N.S.W. Parliament for the
abolition of capital punishment. The second
reading of the Bill was moved 25th Jan.,
1861, and was defeated by a large majority.
And yet, though Parkes advocated the
abolition of hanging, he never went out of
his way to save a condamne. He even
hanged the wretched Hewart, to say
nothing of several persons whose offences
would have been punished in England
with a few years’ gaol.
As Q. elections draw near
politicians begin to crawl before
“the R.C. vote.” During a recent
debate in Q. Assembly, Labor-man
Kidston’s was the only voice raised
against voting money to ex-Premier
Byrnes’ relatives. As Kidston
pointed out. Byrnes has really left
no record of service except what
was handsomely paid for. On the
other hand, Lilley who gave
about 40 years to public duty
(also well paid for)—was allowed
to pass out of sight in a private
hearse without any official re-
cognition whatever. And Lilley
was a greater man than Byrnes
ever could have been. —but, then,
he was a democrat hated by the
boodlers.
Maoriland again leads the
way among the Australias by
abolishing the plural vote at
municipal elections. Under
the new Act every owner
of an estate worth £25 in

Tasmania’s Upper House presented eight
alleged reasons for objecting to the renewal of the
income-tax. Two of them read as follows :
Because for revenue purposes the income-tax is un-
necessary, as the statement made in the Council by the

Because it has been promised that during next session

Which is all so like the average dunderheaded
House of Property. The Tas. Council holds that
because the customs duties are being reduced,
with great loss to the revenue, the income-tax
should also be reduced, at further loss. It must
be a comforting thing to be Treasurer to a crowd
like that.

The bright side of things. Rejoice that
you are young ; for, though you have an intellect,
it has not yet reached alarming dimensions.
Rejoice that you are middle-aged ; for middle-age
is the time for philosophical folly—which is the
biggest kind of folly—and there are few forms of
folly better than that. Rejoice that you are old ;
for old age is the time for dropping that load of
ignorance which we call knowledge. Rejoice that
you are dead ; for you are, doubtless, sorry to be
dead —and sorrow chasteneth.
“Father Steven, R.C. chaplain to the
German garrison of Marienwerde, has been dis-
missed from his post by order of the Minister of
War for having omitted to deliver the customary
laudatory oration upon the occasion of Emperor
William’s birthday. When remonstrated with by
the general commanding the garrison, he excused
himself on the ground that he was too hoarse to
deli ver a sermon on the day in question. This
explanation has not been accepted, however, and
he has been placed under arrest on a charge of
Use majesti.’’—- Ex.

EVERY CYCLIST
Should make a point of examining the Tyres when
purchasing a machine to see if they bear our Regis-
tered Trade Mark. This precaution is advisable, as
a few unscrupulous agents pass off on their un-
suspecting customers poor imitations of
as the genuine article, with the result that instead
of obtaining a pair of the finest Tyres made
they have only a poor substitute.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“ Bargo Re daily paper advts. of situation 9
wanted and employes wanted which give the addres
as (say) “ 8.X.”, “Alpha,” or “A.8.C.” to such-and-such
a P.O. An advt. lately appeared in a daily paper—clerk
wanted. Address—(say)—Alpha, st. P.O. I know
the man who got the job, and this is how he did it. He
waited till the last mail of the day had arrived at
the P. 0., then called and collected some 80 letters ad-
dressed to “Alpha.” These he carried off, but first
posted his own application for the position. The genuine
advertiser called and got only three replies, and as my
friend, the desperate smarty, had the strongest appli-
cation he got the billet; by collecting the 80 replies
the night before he had reduced the chances against him
from 82 to 1 down to 3to 1. He afterwards told me that
some of the applicants whose replies he had collected
could have run “rings around him” as regards testi-
monials, “ but I had to get that job—spent my last four-
pence the day I got it.” Another case : Know a woman
who changes servants weekly. Never patronises a
registry-office or advertises for a new one—simply
collects the replies of some person’s ad. for “ Young girl
to do housework, etc.” In same way servants short of the
necessary shilling swoop down on another “ slavey’s ”
“ situation wanted ” ad. and collect replies. Also know
one party who has been consistently working another
man’s “ Nervous Debility advt.” for the last three months.
Tells me a gold-mine is not a patch on it.
* • * *
A well-known Sydney solicitor fell in rather
neatly the other day. A debtor in an insolvent estate who
intended to leave the province on business was brought
before the Registrar and asked to pay a comparatively
small amount he owed in the estate. The solicitor, who
appeared for the debtor, spoke highly of the honorable
character of his client, whom he had known for many
years. His client, he said, couldn’t pay then, but he
would send the money from the province he was going to.
The assignee, however, was not to be put off ; he wanted
a guarantee that the money would be paid, and he rather
originally suggested that as the solicitor had such a very
high opinion of his client, he (the solicitor) should
guarantee the amount. The lawyer man was taken aback
at the novel suggestion, and stammered that it wasn’t
fair to him ; besides, he added, the debtor might die next
day and he (the solicitor) might have to pay. In that
case, blandly remarked the official assignee, the guarantor
would not be asked to pay. * So the Registrar made an
entry to that effect mentioning that if the debtor died
within a certain time, the solicitor would not be asked to
pay. The record is perhaps unique.
* * * *
A Melbourne paper remarks that Major Howie,
the city’s richest absentee, receives £40,000 a year in
ground rents from property which he never set eyes upon.
Premier Turner might seriously consider the fact that a
5 per cent, absentee tax would bang the Major to the
extent of £2OOO a year, and he and the other absentees
would probably crush for such a decent sum that in five
years the Mildura railway could be built with the money.
* » » *
A harassed Sydney publican has threatened his
boss brewer with an appeal to law to have a thumbscrew-
ing agreement abolished on the ground that the agree-
ment establishes a “ tied house.” Chief Justice Prender-
gast, of Maoriland, some time ago decided that such
houses are illegal there, “ as the tie operates in restraint
of trade,” but, so far, N.S.W. courts have been dumb on
the subject.
* * * *
In a northern border town recently the store-
keeper wanted a job of bricklaying done and called in a
local bricklayer who agreed to do it. Storekeeper subse-
quently wanted him to take out the price of the contract
m store goods. Bricklayer refused, and would not go on
with contract except on a cash basis. Storekeeper offered
him £3 to relinquish contract; bricklayer agreed and gave
an unstamped receipt for amount. Storekeeper laid in-
formation against brick gentleman for giving unstamped
receipt, and got him fined £2 and costs.
B.H. Water Supply shares are being unloaded
in England (a register was lately opened in London) as
fast as safety will allow. Water shares have always been
in high favor ’tother side ; and it is quite possible that
investors there have not all quite realised yet what it
means to hand over the entire works in 21 jears
without compensation. It is this English unloading
which makes the co. unwilling to allow' concessions that,
though they would pay In the long run, would reduce the
proportion of profit to turnover for the present.
• * m m
“ S.S.” : The coupon system is quite a boom in
Melbourne suburbs, and all business people are forced to
adopt it. It means the introduction of another middle-
man, and as profits in groceries, butchering, &c., are cut
very fine as it is, the customers who think they are
pocketing a bonus are paying to fatten an additional
superfluity. Here and there, though, one hears employes
complaining that wages are being cut to provide the
requisite.
• * » *
A nice point, of much interest to sailors,
awaits N.S.W. Supreme Court decision. A British-
registered steamer shipped her crew in England,
and ultimately arrived in Sydney. Here she was char-
tered for the interprovineiai trade, and was put on the
N.S.W. register. Some of the crew then refused to go to
work. They were prosecuted under the N.S.W. Act for
disobedience to lawful orders, convicted, and ordered to
forfeit 10 days’ pay. The ground of appeal is that by put-
ting the ship on the Australian register the men became
liable to the above penalty, while by the English Act,
under which they shipped, they would be liable to a for-
feiture of only two days’ pay. The question involved has
far-reaching consequences, for the coastal wages are
almost—in some cases quite—double those paid for deep-
sea voyages. If the contention of the owners is correct,
then it will be impossible to maintain the standard of
wages on Australian coasting ships. It will only be neces-
sary for “ colonial ” traders to charter an English ship, or
for English owner to send out a ship, with a crew engaged
for deep-sea voyages, and run her on the coastal trade
here. The inevitable result will be to lower the standard
of Australian wages to that paid for deep-sea cruises—in
other words, to reduce them almost 50 per cent.
* * * *
An ex-employe of Melb. Tram Co. applied to a
line manager for a billet whilst the Royal Commission was
sitting, and was told to apply at the close of the commis-
sion, “ when there would be plenty of good billets about.”
And certainly the number of dismissals since made justi-
fies the prophecy.
* * * ■
Cabled that Edison has taken out five patents
in connection with the working of Maoriland ironsand.
The whole of the M.L. west coast is strewn with deposits
of black titanic ironsand, but it occurs with particular
richness along the stretch of beach between Manakau
Heads—a few miles from Auckland city—and the Sugar
Loaves at Taranaki. Fortunes have been expended in
trying to payably work these deposits, the difficulty being
in the fluxing ; but iron produced in small quantities has
been pronounced by experts to be better than anything
else known for manufacture into steel. A clever Pitts-
burg Yankee was imported years ago, and apparently dis-
covered the secret, as he produced ingots from the furnace ;
but just when the shareholders were congratulating
themselves on their good fortune the expert, in a fit of
ungovernable rage, shot an acquaintance over a game of
cards, and got ten years.
* * * *
The passing of Married Woman’s Property Acts
in various American States has given rise to quite a
series of conflicting decisions as to whether the new state
of the law permits a woman to enter into an ordinary
business partnership with her husband. The Supreme
Court of Maine has very recently decided that husband
and wife cannot become business partners.
* • *
Tas. Great Western Gridiron railway-grab gets
afloat but slowty in London. Indisposition of John Bullion
to rush the good thing is being quoted as proof positive
that the concessions granted by the Government are not
such a good thing after all. The Bulletin smiles. There
is no mistake about the value of the grab, Mt. Lyell
deteriora ion notwithstanding ; flotation depends more
on the size of the cut demanded by the promoters. And
the Baker-Rymill crowd was never abnormally modest in
that connection.
* • * *
S. A. Treas. Holder’s best hope now is in ex-
cessive mortality among the ancient gilded ones of his
province. His budget was built on a bountiful harvest :
but the earlier rains were followed by a dry Sep-
tember and October, and furious hot winds have
shrivelled the grain beyond all hope of repair. In many
districts farmers who reckoned less than two months back
upon a gross yield of, say, £6OO, will be lucky now with
£2OO. Tens of thousands of acres that were to have been
left for wheat are being cut for hay ; and already hay and
chaff are up to starvation point in the market.

Woolloston-house, Gastlemaine,
November 30th, 1897.
My dear Mr. Malyon,—
I have used .vour ointment. Ligmolliene with distinct
success in my own case, but with greater as an emollient
unguent for the face of a young lady suffering from
“Acne,” very common during puberty, and you should
push it in that direction. Kindly send me another box
of it.—Yours truly, GEO. D. WOOLEY, M.D.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record pro-
gress with regard to my old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro-
fessor M'Call Anderson and other eminent derma-
tologists, with only partial and temporary relief.
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
your ointment Ligmolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly say my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain vours sin-
cerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.RiC.S., L.

Owing to the Prince of Wales’ birthday
interfering with mail arrangements, The Bulletin
is forced to go to press this week a day earlier and
thus omit much current matter. Why the people
of Australia should profess to keep up the P. of
W.’s birthday, of which the people of England
take no particular notice, has always been a
mystery to this paper. However, any excuse for
a holiday. Albert Edward’s natal day is probably
as good a day to go fishing on as any other in the
almanac.

WE WANT YOU TO LEARN ALL ABOUT
“ RED BIRD SPECIAL,” “ RED BIRD No. I,”
and “BLACK BIRD” Cycles.
We want you to make comparisons, so you will be able to tell the good from the
fair, and the fair from the bad.
There are many reasons why “BED BIRD” Cycles are the best. Here is one
point of their Superiority :
Other makers of high-priced Bicycles use 25 per cent, carbon tubing in frame con-
struction. We don’t. We use nickel steel. Nickel steel tubing is twice as expensive,
and more costly to work, but makes the strongest bicycle-frame. 25-per-cent. Carbon
tube loses over one-half its strength when brazed. Nickel steel loses not 3 per cent, of
its strength when brazed ; therefore, “ BED BIRD ” frames are stronger, more rigid,
and faster than all others.
All Mechanics who know anything about cycle construction acknowledge “BED
BIRDS ” to be the best, unless they receive a commission to sell other wheels.
Drop us a post-card, and we will send you a Catalogue of good solid sense, from any
of our Agencies.

Melb. Cup result was fairly “ up to form,” and
quite in accordance with the doctrine that it is folly to
back common stuff for the big race at Flemington except
on a very wet day. Grafter was only beaten by a head
last year ; moreover he had run second for Melb. Stakes,
the one good test race of the season. Eight times within
the last 14 years the first or second horse in Melb. Stakes
has been first or second in Cup. What better tip can
punters ask for? Cocos had won the Derby, and Wait-a-
Bit, despite his previous cowardice, was a reasonable
public hope. The Ballarat horse has now been placed
among the first four in a Newmarket Handicap, two Aus-
tralian Cups, a Caulfield Cup and a Melbourne Cup.
* * m *
Mr. Forrester made no mystery of his belief that
The Grafter could beat Bobadil for the Flying Stakes last
Thursday. “ Bill ” backed the big horse heavily, advised
enquirers to back it (J. C. Williamson, for instance, took
four “ponies” to one on the owner’s tip), and wore a
beaming face before the race was run. W. R. Wilson, on
the other hand, looked nervous and seemed to be trying
to bluff himself into the idea that Bobbie couldn’t lose.
* ♦ * *
When will the public learn that Caulfield Cup
“ form ” is a delusion and a snare as regards the chances
in the longer race at Flemington? Massinissa was an
absolute failure, like Parthenopasus, Trenchant, Paris,
Sainfoin, Tim Swiveller, Gnaroo, Chicago, and other
veterans in the days of yore. The Grafter has twice run
nowhere at Caulfield previous to finishing second and
first for Melb. Cup. Gaulus, for that matter, was also
among the “nowheres” at Caulfield, in ’97. This
year we had Massinissa and The Chief in front of
Wait-a-Bit and Grafter, but when they met 17 days later
in Melb. Cup, on same terms as before, the extra half-
mile and the broader course gave a very different result.
Given a burst of speed and a bit of luck, anything may
shine in a Caulfield Cup, whereas the hero of the national
event must be a stout horse. If not the actual best at the
weights he, or she, is always one of the best.
The Provost, unbacked, finished a long way
behind the leaders in a hurdle race at Caulfield. Yet on
the strength of that very unsuccessful performance, this
scribe suggested The Provost as a likely winner at
Flemington, and the Westralian horse took the 3 miles
race on Cup Day.
The “ objection ” scandal is clear proof of some-
thing wrong in Australian horse-racing. Pat, the winner
of the G.N. Hurdle Race, last July, was objected to on
the “ interference ’’ ticket. So with The Grafter for the
Cup. And in each case backers of the winner were re-
minded that the result of Tim Swiveller’s Caulfield Cup
had been upset on a second appeal.
Hear the moan of the jock who rode the first
favorite (Clarion) in Melb. Cup : “I was knocked back at
the first turn, and almost ran into the fence. Later I
made up some ground, but as soon as I was getting once
more into a good position I was bumped and knocked
about, and, entering the straight, my horse galloped on
the heels of another and made me think we would have
another Caulfield Cup smash. At that point there was
an opening, and I thought I had a chance, but the bump-
ing beat me, and that’s all I know about the race.” What
a pleasant yarn for the punters who took 5 to 1 about
Clarion winning on his supposed merits 1
* * * »
Ikey Mo was outrageously insulting in many
cases on Cup-day. When things are for the moment
going wrong with that “ plucky,” “ honorable,” and “ in-
variably courteous” parasite, he is apt to become very
foul-mouthed. A good many punters, who were told to
wait anxiously for their Grafter money until after the
last race, wouid have been justified in pulling the aggres-
sive beaks of their respective Ikeys. “O, for the Tote 1 ”
was a general cry.
* » * •
Consoling for the idiots who backed losers on
the strength of alleged nocturnal visions. Said Alf.
Joseph to an Arous admirer : “ One of the reasons that
made Clarion such a hot favorite for the Melb. Cup was
that a certain lady [Wanted, her name and lateaddress.—
Ed. B.] dreamt that Clarion won the Cup, and that she
herself was dead before the race was run. As a matter of
fact, she died in the meantime, and many people who
knew of her dream backed Clarion, but the Carbine
horse, as you know, did not win. Talking of dreams, I
may say that at least half of the horses in the race have
been dreamt of as the winners. Eleusinian, Trapper and
Battalion were all dreamt of, and backed with me to win.
One gentleman lost £BO with me over Battalion in this
way.” The Buddetin would like to know how many of
these weird “tips” emanated from gentry whose busi-
ness it was to lay agin’ ’em. Shakespeare’s Richard
the Third was a “ metallician ” at heart when he re-
marked in his opening soliloquy : “ Plots have I laid, in-
ductions dangerous, by drunken prophecies, libels and
dreams.” It is usual for the stage Richard to elevate his
eyebrows and simulate suppressed laughter when he
comes to “dreams.”
* a a a
Outside of legends from the St. Albans’ stable,
l here isn’t the least evidence of Manfred being more than a
second-class animal. He doesn’t look like a flier, and he
never offers a suggestion of his alleged fleetness when he
carries the Wilson colors in public. Still they go on
believing in the brute. The stable-boys, to a nipper, had
their money on Manfred again this year, and hoped that
the illogical device of a strange jockey on his back would
tempt the son of Robinson Crusoe to “ gallop.” The
strange jockey was Dicky Walker, who appeared to be
taking Manfred quite seriously. All the way down to the
starting-post he administered hard smacks to the horse’s
neck, but this particular form of encouragement had no
effect. Perhaps it would have been better to stroke his
hindquarters with a hot poker.
* • * •
Suppose a man went to Oxenham, 18 months
ago, and asked, “ What odds against this : An owner
getting first and second, in Melb. Cup ’97, with two sons
of the same sire, and the second of these being first in the
Cup of ’98?” H.O. would say, “It is all i have to a
pound.” Nothing happens but the impossible.
* * » *
Cup-day was, more than ever, the day at Flem-
ington this year. Newspapers try to perpetuate the
fiction that the Oaks has particular attractions for ladies,
but, as a matter of fact, the Thursday meeting has lost
much of its sassiety importance. Given equal weather
conditions and a reasonable amount of success for favor-
ites in the Carnival’s early stages, the gate-money result
is—Cup-day, 1; Steeplechase, 2 ; Derby, 3 ; and Oaks, 4.

Cocos had bad luck in his alleged “fluky”
Derby race, for something galloped against one of his
heels and cut him considerably. In the Cup race he sus-
tained further damage.
* * * a
Alec Taylor reckoned that Wait-a-Bit couldn’t
be beaten for the Cup. When the Malua horse had lost
the first “game” race of his life by half a neck, and the
protest had been dismissed, old-man Taylor was quite
pathetically crushed.
• * • *
Melb. Argus sent a special reporter to the Vic-
torian Club (Melb.) on “ Cup Eve,” and that awe-struck
myrmidon grave the world a beautiful word-picture en-
titled “ Calling the Card.” His treatment of the majestic
figures in the foreground was soft and sympathetic :
“ These men never talk in units. Only some of them
talk in hundreds. The leviathans, between the puffs of
their cigars, sit back, and in low, unpretentious voices
drop thousands from their lips like ripe plums in a wes-
terly wind. What grand heads these men must have for
figures, thinks the novice. It is a wonderful gift, for they
never make mistakes.” Ye gpds !
Clarion, Manfred, Dreamland and many others
who performed such wonderful watch-breaking' (and
heart-breaking) gallops in their Cup “preparations,”
finished in the actual race down the course—a too common
occurrence with animals that have been “ thoroughly
wound up.” Dreamland in particular is a most un-
fortunate gee, as once before the little heart he possesses
was galloped out of him a few days prior to the Cup.
* * • *
Noticeable the “ soft spot ” in the sons and
daughters of the mighty Malua. For instance, the four
that were hacked for last Melb. Cup at one time or other
—Wait-a-Bit, Aroha, Marusa, and Malto—a nice faint-
hearted quartette to make a recovery on. Wait-a-Bit’s
soft spot, however, seems to be hardening.
» • » *
At the complimentary gobble in honor of Sep.
Miller, chairman of the Vic. Racing Club, the humorless
Brassey informed them that 40 years ago he won a steeple-
chase. Even Byron Moore smiled, and irreverent waiters
whispered behind their hands.

different starters for Melb. Cup, including Wait-a-bit,
Merloolas, Massinissa, The Chief, and others, but, alas !
nary a Grafter. Hard luck !

* * * *
“F.C.”: The Flat, after Melb. Cup race, af-
forded an interesting lesson on the tote. From the “ hill ”
could be seen small, excited knots of bettors gesticulating
violently, then a break-away and a vision of an individual
turned upside-down in a hurry, with a vigorous tossing-
about of arms and legs ; then a couple of white helmets,
lockup and peace —till next time. One small welsher,
when last seen, was high in air on the shoulders of a big
brewer’s-drayman, disappearing in the bushes near the
Saltwater River ; and presently the drayman returned—
alone ! Writer was led to notice these little amenities as
comment on stability of the tote by the fact that he had
backed Amberite, as per Bulletin advice, and so didn’t
have to look for his bookmaker.
* * • •
“K.”: Just to show what a howling farce the
police raids on Wren’s (Oollingwood) tote-shop are. On
the word of a punter (a gent, compared with whom Judas
Iscariot was probably a perfect nobleman) we were passed
through the tea-shop into the tote-room. It was a busy
day, and from behind iron bars five men in masks and
striped gowns marked the sheets ; everything was fair
and square, and when you won someone from round the
corner (of a pigeon-hole) passed out the stuff. But what
was to prevent me (a stranger and perhaps a d.) from
bailing-up the lot and calling in the police ? Nothing.
* * * *
The imported sire Gozo easily tops the list of
winning sires in ’Stralia this season and there is every
probability that he will continue to hold the pride of
place till the season closes. Trenton comes second, with
Lochiel third ; but the St. Simon horse, Bill of Portland,
may supplant one of these latter ere the curtain falls.
* * * *
The big crowd that attended at Flemington on
Saturday had great weather to assist it in “suffering
a recovery.” Bobadil’s failures were not sufficient to run
the punters off, and he started at 10 to 9 on for the
Spring Stakes. At the distance, the favorite was
in the lead, but under the whip. Cocos then took
command and appeared to be winning without an
effort, but Lee-Metford put in his claim and after
a slashing set-to won by half a neck. A protest
was entered by Cocos against Lee-Metford for boring, and,
being upheld, the race was awarded to Cocos. For the
Flemington Stakes, Graceful, stable-companion of Bobadil,
was made favorite at sto 4, but she finished nearer last
than first, and the winner turned up in Condiment, an
Bto 1 chance. Another 8 to 1 chance in Catspaw won
the V.R.C. Handicap ; and Pirate, at 5 to 1, carried off
the Steeplechase from Tremolo, the fencing being good
and falls few. The public could, of course, stand nothing
but The Grafter for the C. B. Fisher Plate, which he won
comfortably from Battalion ; and then Eleusive sent a
good many away happy by starting a 3 to 1 favorite for
the Final Handicap, and winning easily from Kallara.
m * * •
Sydney galloway Battler, who was one of those
to try his luck among the horses without success, has re-
turned to pony ranks, and made the fact known by win-
ning at Kensington at his first two tries. Pony-racing is
an interesting study. Some of those who went up with
Battler and are now scoring amongst “ horses ” whenever
asked, were not reckoned to be within pounds of him
amongst the ponies.
Why cannot Australian turf authorities agree
upon some uniform plan with regard to nomenclature ?
At present there is a Coreas in Queensland likely to win
Q.T.C. Derby, whilst in M.L. a horse of a similar name is
a good favorite for N.Z. Cup. At 'Ome they solve the
difficulty by affixing the numerals, 1., 11., etc.

* * • *
The other Sunday, in an Adelaide suburban
synagogue, the preacher declared that he would rather
live on a Tottie’s earnings than on the proceeds of
gambling—or words to that effect; the precise language
is probably too blue for this unrighteous print.
* » * *
A unique cricket record was registered in Melb.
t’other day. Hawksburn set Carlton the tidy score of
393. Carlton responded with no wickets for 101 on first
day. The match, according to V.C.A. rules, had to be
finished during the week, play starling at 5 p.m. and
stumps being drawn at 6 p.m.—a manifest disadvantage
to batsmen. Hetherington got run out for 108 on Mon-
day night, and the respective scores on each night were 1
for 179, 1 for 258, 1 for 324, and 1 for 396. Carlton thus
won by nine wickets. T. Warne 140 not out, Brooks
141 not out, three century scores. The Carltons have
shifted from their old ground on Sydney Road, where
they have been playing for nearly half-a-eentury, to an
oval in Princess Park, and the match was played on abso-
lutely new turf.
* * * *
The match, First N.S.W. Eleven versus next
Fifteen, began on Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday,
and brought to the front a couple of good young ’uns in
Duff and Deane, who quite collared Howell’s bowling,
scoring 141 not out and 80 respectively. Duff plays with
and is a consistent scorer for N. Sydney, whilst Deane
throws in his lot with Burwood. Deane, by the way, is a
younger brother of Mr. Syd. Deane’s. He used to play at
Lawson, on the Blue Mountains ; and, in one season there,
scored no less than nine centuries.
Broken Hill’s most sensational cricketer this
season was lately for a year chairman of the S.A. Baptist
Union —the nearest approach the hard-shells have to a
bishop. Then his throat gave out, and he left the
pulpit and went into the tobacco business.
» * » »
George Giffen, it is chronicled by an admirer,
“ walks 16 miles daily before breakfast with a view’ to
striking cricket form.” But George has a long way to go
to wipe out his evil reputation of last year.
» # * m
Old Jesse Hide, who a score of years and more
ago taught S.A. cricketers and appeared in early inter-
provincials, playing this season tor Cornwall County,
made 91 (of a total of 405) and then was caught. Also he
took 4 wickets for 31 and 3 for 74.
* * * ♦
S.A. team picked to play Victoria in Adelaide
this Saturday (12th) contains ten of last year’s men ; a
new man, Peters, who is a fair club bat, making up the
eleven. Lyons has been shaping poorly, and is included
on his reputation, but he is very partial to Vic. bowling.
The S.A. team chooses its captain by ballot this year for
obvious reasons. Giffen is a wretched skipper, but no
one has ever had the pluck to nominate a rival. A ballot
will probably result in the selection of Darling. On paper
the match looks a good thing for S.A.
* * * *
George Giffen made 58 for W. Adelaide against
Sturt the other day on a very bad wicket. His batting
was in his old form and delighted his friends, but the ex-
champion has apparently lost his ancient bowling powers.
The English crowd who derided Stoddart’s team
last year— after it had been decisively licked by Aus-
tralia—as not being representative, should be silenced by
the licking Stoddart and co., without Ranji, gave Eng-
land’s best at Hastings, and the additional fact that
Hearne, who was a painfully plain and ordinary bowler in
Australia, is top of the English averages this season. In
Australia, our best team is superior to England at pre-
sent ; in England, where conditions are different, the boot
is, perhaps, on the other foot.
* * * *
Gympie has its Sabbath football and other
sports, too; and to cleanse the district of that impurity
the local parsons lately promoted a movement to secure
Saturday half-holidays. But the miners, who would have
had to work an extra half-hour on other days, reckon
that eight hours underground is quite enough, and stand
off. So the movement, which was smiled upon by some
of the managers, is at present devoting itself to the con-
version of the workmen —with poor results, so far.

sculler who won the single amateur scuds at Henley last
vear. and who would have been a certainty for this year

• • » *
Billy Edwards, the Melbourne boxer, fought
Yankee Frank Craig, the “ Coffee Cooler,” for 1 200 a-side
in South Africa, recently. Craig, who, a bit back, «as
boomed as a tip-topper at his weight, showed that he has
not utterly gone off by outclassing the hard-hitting Aus-
tralian, and winning in eight rounds. Australia, vvnicn a
few years ago could show a champion in every class, nas
now but one to its name, Bob Fitzsimmons, and it re-
mains to be seen if he retains his form in anything but
newspaper fighting.
* » * *
Australian greyhounds taken to America by Mr.
R. de B. Lopez, of Sydney, have done well. A daughter of
two Australian Waterloo Cup winners has been carrying
off some stakes in England ; and Mr. Sam Bladon is now
on the road ’Onie with Bogan, the last Vic. Waterloo up
winner, and the youngster, Bald Coot, with an eye to the
next English Waterloo Cup. Tarana[s Pride also went
with Mr. Bladon, who is a regular Bill Kelso at his own
game, and if the puppies show their Australian form, the
Times may make the discovery that Australian dogs, like
its horses, are little, if any', behind the British article.
Few men rode right through the great Warr-
nambool road race on the one machine, as the western
roads by no means upheld their reputation. Voisey, of
Castlemaine, the fourth man, was an exception, for he
rode a 221 b. “Swift” machine right through without
mishap.
* » * •
There was some tall riding at the recent Ade-
laide cycle meeting. “Swift” Walne, as usual, did some-
thing phenomenal on his favorite track. On this occasion
he rode half-a-mile in 59sec. from scratch, threading his
way through his field with consummate skill, and winning
not only the heat, but the applause of the multitude. He
failed to get through in the final, being beaten by “ Bob ”
Lewis, another Swift rider.

* * m *
It was at Twowharves post-office that two cane-
cutters got into an argument about their respective
knowledge of Jack M‘Lean —for a space champion sculler
of the world. Said one : “ Why', blowme, I went to
school with him for years. Know him well! I should
think 1 did.” “ Bah ! " from the other. “ What of
that? Me’d him worked tagetha fa months. Best axe-
man I ever see. When he stawted choppin, ya couldd’t
see him fa chips, he was that quick. Gev me the biggest
hidin’l ever got, too.” “ Oh, that was easy enough to
do!” “Was it? You couldn’t do it, anyhow!”
“Could.” “ Well, put up ya hands’n’we’ll see.” And
at it they wont. About round 10 M‘Lean himself hap-
pened along on horseback, and was asked who the com-
batants were. “ Blessed if I know,” he said—“ never
saw either of ’em before in my r life ! ”•—and rode on. Left
’em fighting.
AT THE ROOMS.
Observe yon person, fat of jowl,
Who royally surveys the scene—
He does not shriek, or roar, or howl,
He simply sits—and looks serene.
That gentle, foggy-spoken man,
Is Joseph, the Levi-a-than.
Didst see Jack Cohen book a bet ?
A silently impressive sight!
Great Oxenham’s stern face is set
In form majestical to-night,
But Westbrook condescends to smile
In such an unpretentious style.
The “ Count ’’ (Lord knows his other name)
Wears ne’er a laurel on his brow,
Despite the splendor of his fame,
He lays the odds to lose a thou.,
As though he were (which isn’t true)
A mortal, just the same as you.
Gaze on them all, but shade your eyes
A moment from the dazzling glare,
For diamonds of largest size
Are mounted in the rings they wear.
The smoke-clouds curling through their lips
Melt into air, like punters’ tips.
And "twig" the Argus on his knees,
A-jotting down a rapid note
Of all the wondrous things he sees,
He cries, “ Confusion to the Tote 1 ”
Then reverently bends him low
To lick the boot of Ikey Mo.

Edmund Barton had the ill-luck to fall and
break his arm the other night; and as Barton
is a heavyweight, the break was a bad one. lhe
friends of the Federal leader will be glad to hear
that it is mending as quickly as can be expected,
though Mr. Barton is not yet able to leave his bed.
Mrs Maclurcan’s cookery book is having
an excellent sale, the first edition being exhausted.
The book which can be bought from all the local
stationers, only costs 3/6, and as it is suited to
Queensland and contains over 750 recipes, the
value given far exceeds the sum necessary for its
purchase.*
Be statement in issue of sth inst. that
Saul Samuel 50 years ago cowhided a Sydney
citizen on Homebush racecourse, 1 he Bulletin is
informed by an eyewitness that there was no cow-
hiding in the matter, the facts being that when
Mr. Samuel ordered certain non-subscribers off the
course, the person in question, seeing that all non-
subscribers were not so served, refused to go and,
in resisting the police, committed a technical
assault, the charge for which was dismissed on the
ground that the police had exceeded their duty in
the matter.
Recent analyses of Sydney Lager Bier and of
all other beers on Sydney market show wonderful figures
in favor of Sydney Lasrer, and it is thus scientifically
demonstrated that Sydney Lager deserves to be the
national drink of New South Wales. Supposing that a
man drinks three pints of liquid per day ; then he will
in a life of 71) years on this average drink some 10,000
gallons. Suppose that instead of Sydney Lager, that a
man drank 10,000 gallons of deleterious highly-alcoholic
liquor. It wouldn’t matter if he could replace his liver,
kidneys and stomach with a fresh set every 12 months—
but tlien a man isn’t a machine of that sort. It' one of
his inner and delicate mechanisms go wrong, he breaks
down all over. 10,000 gallons !—enough to float a ship !
Isn't the thought enough to make you solemnly kneel
down and swear to be faithful to Sydney Lager for the
rest of your lifetime !
Apropos the lately-reported incident in a
Bendigo mine, when a descending cage loaded
with mitiers crashed into bearers across the shaft
at 2455 ft., it transpires that the platman (the per-
son in charge of the landing), who ought to have
withdrawn the bars, was a youth of 19, just put
on at the job. A prosecution followed—fined,
40s. ; fine to go to the three injured miners. And
here is another of the excitements of Bendigo
deep-mining. In the Shamrock, the other day,
two men wore being hauled up from the 2800 ft.
level. The cage had gone only 50ft. or so when
there was a terrific crash on top of it. Yet,
though the engine was strained on its bed and the
engine-house shook and swayed, the steel-rope
held. Presently the driver slowly lowered to
2800 ft., and then tried to communicate with the
men ; but the signal-wire would not work. Half-
an-hour after, while he was still trying to signal, the
two men walked up to the engine-house ; they had
crept into a neighboring mine and been hauled up
that way. Then they went down to search their
own shaft—and found that a couple of hundred-
weight of sandstone and quartz had fallen away
from 1320 ft., and had hounded down 1410 ft.
before it had met the up-coming cage. The two
men weren’t even scratched.

“ Melb.” : Miss Patterson, aged 61, died
suddenly at Hawksburn railway-station this week.
She had stitched into her clothing a memo, to
the effect that, in such an event, she was to be
taken to her address, which was given, and not to
the morgue. Unfortunately, she had been lying
a good while there before the scrap was found.
Lisson, condemned for killing his sister-in-
law at Sydney—he also tried to kill a man whom
he regarded as a business enemy, and his own two
children —has had his sentence commuted. The
official medical authorities consider prisoner to be
now sane, but there is little doubt that he was
irresponsible when he ran amuck. Anyhow, few
people nowadays consider that anyone should be
hanged except for deliberate murder done for the
sake of gain.

when applied to a man is a high academic distinc-

“ T.J.S.” : No reason for “ Disgusted ” to
be so at the spinelessness of N.S. W. civil service ;
it is simply “the survival of the fittest” over
again. The process of weeding-out persons of
individuality, or who dared hold an opinion at
variance with that of their red-tape superiors, or
who declined to recommend unwarranted public
expenditure to suit their friends, or who bucked
if undeservedly sat on, whenever the livers of those
superiors prompted them to sit, has been going on
for years. Writer was once in a district where a
grant of some .£4OO had been made before he took
charge. M.P. wrote Minister to know why this
grant was unexpended, and writer was told to
report. lie reported that the expenditure was
quite unwarranted, and would only serve two
people. Up came an official from Sydney, in-
spected, informed the scribe that he agreed with
him, but, to block agitation which might interfere
with the departmental sleep, it would be better to
expend, say, <£so to £lOO, and let balance of grant
lapse. Also, in future it would be well if he bore
in mind that “a public officer’s first duty is to
draw his salary as quietly as possible.”

Dearß. —Dead, Richard Brotchie, long-time
printer of Melb. Leader and Age. Remember
Brotchie as a young man, fat and comely, overseer
on Melb. Morning Herald. Liked by all hands.
A quiet, gentle, humorous sort, whose life seemed
a perpetual chuckle. He never altered much.
Always good-tempered, with a kindly word for
all who deserved it, and silence for the unspeak-
able. Yet he suffered, most of his life, from rheu-
matic gout. Had a theory as to its origin—beer,
When the paper was down, Brotchie and this
ancient used to repair to Hansen’s- a night pub.
in Bourke-street, frequented by theatrical and
newspaper folk. And there beef and beer, of
heroic measure, were consumed, and thus the
seeds sown, the harvest of which was reaped in
later years. For beer is surely an agent for the
breeding of the accursed result called gout.
“ There’s one comfort,” sighed Brotchie, the last
time we spoke together—“it was British.” Ah !
he was a good fellow, and should make a first-
class angel. — Old Jack.

“ J.S.T.” to The Bulletin :
In “T’s” recent article, wherein he accuses “the
Baron ” of “ rats,” and incidentally wipes the floor with
“ Creeve Roe,” he denies the existence of any sex-
problen), and suggests that if the human race will go in
the altogether nobody would maunder about one. Quite
so, that would about settle the problem ; but most of
the population would have to be einploj'ed in the regis-
trar’s office. There is nothing prurient about natural sex
relations—that only comes in with the dalliances of
surfeit, and “ the problem ”is here. Sexual relations are
absolutely necessary to the health of any properly-
developed adult. The youthful savage had to hustle
round and learn the arts of the chase and war, and when
he reached manhood, and the sexual awakening came, he
cast round for a mate, took her off by force, and settled the
problem forthwith. Now we're civilised, a man muststifle
his desires at the expense of his health until such time as
he has accumulated sufficient oof to afford the luxury of
marriage, hence the hordes of dyspeptic men and anemic
women. Either this, or he gratifies vicariously at the
expense of his Respectability. Because this appetite has
been shooed at by Mrs. Grundy, wherever two or three
men are gathered together there the cerulean story is in
evidence. .You don’t find people gathering in clubs, or
the back rooms of pubs., to weave yarnlets round yester-
day’s hash, or the succulent viand of to-morrow. And
because of this conventional veil, drawn over what,
within limits, is right, every small boy’s mind is agape,
and he seeks to tear the veil aside at the first promptings
of a feeble adolescence, with the result that half the
male population, when they reach an age when
they should be most virile, are already physical
wrecks, on whom a swarm of don’t - you - feel - well-
young-man quacks fatten. With the decadence of
churchianity not Christianity and other hypocrisies,
the legitimacy of sex-hunger will be recognised, and may
result in the municipalisation of Flossie, in whose court
the poor man will be allowed to proceed in forma pauperis.
“T.” looks on the presence of the inevitable infant as
proof of the solution of the sex-problem, whereas the
probability of that damp, unpleasant entity, and the food
it will guzzle, and the clothes it will crawl out of, and the
general sin and blasphemy it will create, prevents its
solution. Anyhow, the ever-increasing poverty and the
practice of monogamy don’t dovetail, somehow ; and not
only have we a sex-problem, but it is intensifying yearly.
The Bulletin submitted this to “T.,” so that, if
he wanted very badly to howl in reply both howls
might appear together. “ T-” promptly howled
at great length, but his remarks are condensed as
follows ;
0) Poverty isn’t increasing; at least if there is any
virtue in all available statistics it isn’t. Instead, it has
been decreasing, taking the civilised world as a whole,
for many years. The remote field of 100 or 1000 years
back looks green ; that’s all. You can’t logically say
poverty is increasing because the Australian gold-boom is
past; that was merely a small local circumstance. (2)
There is no evidence that the sex-problem (so-called) is
intensifying at all. It is more talked about, but that is
quite a different thing. (3) “J.S.T.” holds that the
“ civilised ” man, being a miserable victim to Grundyism,
is becoming very largely a premature physical wreck.
Well he lives much longer than his ancestor, the old un-
Grundied savage, and is apparently healthier, so perhaps
the alleged man of to-day, who is an alleged wreck through
the alleged sex-problem, isn’t quite so awful a circum-
stance after all.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H* Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Cut from the inquiry by the Auditor'
General, Westralia, into the expense-and-allow-
ance account of Mr. Harry Wainscot, the senior
official liquidator :
1. The Auditor-General : I want to elicit certain infor-
mation connected with the statement of disbursements
you sent in on March 18 last. There are certain charges
made which I want explained. There is a charge—
“ Umbrella, Bs. 6d.” Will you kindly explain why you
charge that item ?—Mr. Wainscot: Because it was neces-
sary.
2. There are also the following items :—“ Tips, Gerald-
ton, 15s. ; ” “ tips, Cue, £2 ; ” “ liquor, £1 4s. ; ” “ water-
bags, 7s. ; ” “ milk-bags, 4s. ; ” “ man, for services, 3s. ; ”
“housemaid, Cue, 3s. 6d. “chemist, Bs. 6d.” “Why
do you charge clothes, 16s. 5d V ” —lt was necessary.
3. Were they clothes for yourself ?—Some for Mr. Kelly
and some for myself.
4. There are also other items —“ Utensils, ss. 6d. ;”
“ glasses, 3s. 6d. ; ” and “ overcharge on luggage, £4 11s.”
What is this last item ?—Luggage, paid for to Gascard.
They do not carry luggage for nothing.
5. There are other items —“tip to driver, 5s. J tip
to waiter, Lawlers, £1 ;’’ “liquor, 12s. 6d. and wash-
ing, 165.” Is it usual to charge washing- in your travel-
ling?—Most certainly. You must have washing done.
You cannot go all round these fields without. One has to
go round the circuit, and just fancy, can anyone pack
enough linen to last him the whole time he is travelling
the circuit? Allow me also to point this out : I ought to
have charged more than what is mentioned there. I
destroyed two or three suits of clothes on that journey.
Why should I have to provide new clothes for the benefit
of the service ?
6. Then come the items —“Tip at Menzies, 55.” ; “ tips,
Coolgardie, £1 45.” ; “driver, Niagara, 55.” washing,
£1 10s. 9d.” ; “ tips, 45.” ; “ medicine, 4s. 6d.” Is it usual
to charge medicine in your travelling expenses? —Most
certainly. If a man is sick, and his sickness is caused
through travelling, he ought to be repaid the amount
paid for medicine.

Marshall-Hall’s skilfully-contrived triumph
over the Church Party at Melb. University is
made only the more apparent by the howls of the
defeated ones. The other day the Congregational
Unionists of Melbourne spent hours in bemoaning
their discomfiture, and the end of their little
meeting was a resolution to the effect that the
University should take immediate steps to prevent
any future musical professor from speaking scorn-
fully of the unco guid. It isn’t the alleged “ im-
modesty and agnosticism of Marshall-Hall that
hurts them badly. It’s his anti-parsonic tone
they object to. “ Impiousness ”is want of re-
spect for the blessed “ cloth. ”

The manager of a West Coast (Tas.) mine
recently had a little joke at the expense of a
mining reporter, by telling him with an air of
mystery that “in No. 2 crosscut a body re-
sembling unpetrified coprolitc had been struck.”
The reporter expressed surprise at the metal being
discovered in Tasmania, and suggested that it
would open up a new era in mining on the coast.
‘ Do you know the metal, then ? ” asked the
manager. “Oh yes, I’ve seen it and handled it,”
said the cocksure scribe, “in fact I’ve got some of
it in my collection.” The manager congratulated
the reporter on his knowledge and the report duly
appeared. Then somebody advised the poor re-
porter to have a look in the dictionary, and a
wild guffaw rang all along the coast.
A Run on Eggs. We can hatch chickens with
incubators, but before you get your chick you must get
the egg. A good hen lays an egg a day. We saw in a
late issue of yours that a poultry-owner was the lucky
possessor of a hen which laid two eggs a day. The ex-
planation, no doubt, is that this poultry-man knows
what to feed them on. He has evidently paid a visit to
our Melbourne or Sydney warehouses, bought one of our
new green bone-cutters, and gets two from one. M‘LEAN
BROS. & RIGG LTD. have still a few of these egg-
producing mills for sale. Buy one before too late.*

mont again (the Wide World Magazine) :
One day I decided to go and explore one of the islands,
in search of wombats, whose skins I wanted to make into
sandals for myself. I knew that wombats haunted the
islands in countless thousands, because I had seen them
rising in clouds every evening at sunset.
What sort of bats ? Why, wom-bats ! But not a
word about the bandicoot bird or the long-winged
goanna.

Whilst a troop of Australian Horse out
Mudgee way was resting after drill, a private,
running his charger alongside one of the officers,
lighted his cigarette from that of his superior.
The latter took this in good part, but said : “ In
the British army you could not have done this,
Harry.” “ Right you are,” was the prompt reply,
“ but in the British army you would not be an
officer.” —Coonamble paper.

The Hot Weather
and a Malarial Climate
generate
FEVER AND AGUE,
Clements Tonic
RESCUES & RECUPERATES
A MAN REDUCED TO SKIN AND BONE.
(bt ottr special reporter.)
Having received information of the
singular success of Clements Tonic in curing
Mr. K. S. Gibb of intermittent fever, and
general debility arming out of that com-
plaint, our special reporter called on the
late sufferer at his residence, No. 21 Francis-
street, Sydney. Mr. Gibb was in, and said
he would be pleased to furnish full
Particulars of His Illness
and the manner in which Clements Tonic
expelled it from his system.
“Avery obstinaieand difficult complaint
to deal with is this fever,” said Mr. Gibb,
in answer to a question put to him by the
reporter; “not so much the illness itself,
but its after consequences are to be
dreaded.”
“Will you be good enough to tell me
where and how you first contracted this
disease ?”
“Certainly I will. It was in Queensland,
800 odd miles up country from Brisbane,
and in a malarial district. Almost any
country in that latitude is malarial, if there
is plenty of water about. I was a store and
book-keeper on a large sugar plantation in
the Northern District of Queensland, which
is somewhat low and marshy. Unfortu-
nately, being new to the district, and not
taking proper precautions against the night
air,
I Contracted Fever and Ague.
I was forced to go to bed immediately. It
was no use fighting against it. Everynow and
again I got a shivering fit, which made me
shake like an
Old Man with the Palsy.
Then would come a state of high fever,
when my temperature would goup atquitean
alarming rate. The local doctor lived some
distance off, but drove over to see me and
ordered me the usual medicines, including a
large quantity of quinine. He also gave
very special directions as to my diet and
general treatment.”
“ How did you get on?”
“ Well, as you see, the fever didn’t kill
me, but it left me in
A Very Bad State.
I got over the first heat of the disease, but
the second stage of my illness was worse
than the first, and I was a
Victim to General Debility.
I was so thin and weak that it was
quite as much as I could do to move my
limbs. I had taken so much quinine that I
positively couldn’t take any more—it made
me sick and gave me an intense headache
and drumming in the ears. Everyone up
there had a different remedy to recommend
me. but none were of any use. I tried hot
baths with plenty of eucalyptus leaves
soaked in the water. I tried gentian,
cardamoms, and nux vomica ; and perhaps
some of these things might have strung me
together for a little time, but they were of
No Lasting Benefit.
When the fever had left me I thought
myself convalescent, and expected to
have had an appetite. But just the
contrary was the case; I had to force
myself to eat, and just lie on the bed staring
at the ceiling with no hope or desire for
anything, and without sufficient strength to
Brush a Fly Away
from my face.”
“ What did the medical man say ?”
“ He said mine was a very long and pain-
ful case, and as soon as I was better, I ought
to go away to a hilly district for a change
of air One day an old bushman who came
to see me, offered to tell me oisoinething that
would give me an appetite ; he said he had
Known it Pull a Man To-
gether when Nothing Else
Would do Him any Good.
At the time I took little interest in any-
thing, but I had just enough curiosity to
ask him what it was. He didn’t tell me
then, but next day he brought me half
A Bottle of Clements Tonic.
He told me how to take it, and gave me the
first dose himself.”
“ Did you notice anything ?”
“ No, I didn’t, for I forgot all about it.
But my friend came next day, and asked me
how I felt, and I had to tell him that I had
forgotten to take any more of the medicine.
‘You Look a Little Brisker,
anyhow,’ he replied, and I certainly did feel a
trifle brighter. He then took a parcel and
placed it on the table by the bedside. It con-
tained half-a-dozen bottlesof Clements Tonic,
which he had taken the trouble to get
expressly for me. ‘ Now,’ he said, ‘ I hope
you won’t have to take the lot, but there
"he bottles are if you should require them.’”
“ How did you get on?”
“From that moment
I Took No Other Medicine,
nor did I try any of the ‘local treat-
ments,’ as I used to call them, which
were continually being recommended to me
1 think I told you I was little else than
Skin and Bone.
Well, after a few doses of Clements
Tonic I started to eat. I didn't seem
able to get enough of anything, as the
people round me were afraid to give me too
much at a time, owing to my great weak
ness. 1 began to
Feel Quite Cheerful,
too, and took a lively interest in the news-
papers. Whole nights I had used to lie awake,
but now I slept like a top from 10 o’clock to 8
o’clock the next morning. I was able to
get up and
Walk Short Distances,
and a marked improvement was noticed in
me every day. The way 1 put on flesh was
wonderful. People who saw me said miiK
was a case of
The Quickest Recovery
from the after effects of fever that they had
seen, and thesame people had prophesied that
I wouldn’t get well for a year or two, but
they were all wrong. In two or three
months I was knocking about the plantation,
and getting fatter and
Stronger Djiy by Day.
I told them what had cured me. I don t
know what is in Clements Tonic, butit seems
a thorough
Specific Against Fever,

EVENING IN THE BUSH.
The sun is slowly sinking- in the west,
A mellow radiance fills the evening sky—
And clear from yonder mountain’s distant crest
The Jabberwock gives forth his plaintive cry.
The wombats, rising quickly into view,
Soar to the summits of the forest trees,
Where, in her nest, the gentle kangaroo
Guardeth the eggs her nimble foes would seize.
The oof-bird sitting on a golden reef
Sings loud of “ Debs,” and “ Prefs” and oountless gain,
While with a swiftness almost past belief
Fleet-footed parrots gallop o’er the plain.
The gentle boomerang with placid eye
Crops thoughtfully the dazzling azure mead •
And wallabies with eroakings seaward fly
To catch the larrikins on which they feed.
The octopus from out his leary lair
Makes twilight hideous with his fearsome howls.
And generally does his best to scare
The basking shark and other tender fowls.
Perhaps you’ll ask me how I came to know
About these wond’rous things I haven’t seen ;
I simply ask you, if you do, to go —
Well, somewhere else where I have never been.

Melb. Women’s Franchise League gathered
30 women and 12 men together in Russell-st. one
night lately to talk suffrage agitation. This is
part of what happened :
Mrs. Riekaby thought men were indebted to women for
everything—life, honor, the very air they breathed, and
the grand brains which they inherited from their
mothers. She had never met the man who had a better
brain than herself.
A Rude Man : “Dieken 1" (Loud laughter from the
chosen 12).
Mrs. Riekaby and MissSomebody-or-other(hysterically):
“ Shoo ! you wretch ! ”
The meeting broke up in disorder.
“E.F.” bewails “a failure in Australians”:
Now that we have the text of the played-out Mr.
Rougemont-Grien’s addresses, the wonder is that he was
not so plainly and unmistakeably confuted at once that
he would have had to retire instantly into confusion and
cannibalism. But the few Australians who tackled him
seem to have done it on petty details, instead of on the
broad and strong facts. Louis Becke, for instance.
Becke is the most wonderful man I ever met for knowing
the latitude and longitude of places. I believe he gener-
ally sleeps with an Admiralty chart under his pillow lest
he should wake up in the night and feel lonely. Instead
of pointing out to Grien that it was impossible for a man
to be aware of the existence of the old settlement of
Somerset on Cape York and yet know nothing about the
Gulf of Carpentaria ; and that when the same gentleman
passed through Apsley Strait and then sailed west,
he was going away from the coral regions into the
Indian Ocean (still, he found a coral atoll there
outside coral limits, never heard of before!) When
I knew Becke he could place nearly every rock in the
seven seas, but instead of using that knowledge to con-
found Grien, he condescends to enter into an argument
on the practical character of turtle - riding. Then
there’s Hogan, M.P., who (though he was never in
any more cannibalistic place than Collins-street,
Melbourne) wrote a kaleidescopic sort of romance
called “ The Lost Explorer,” and he says, according to
Pall Mall Gazette, “theN. W. corner of Australia, the
scene of the alleged adventures, is the one large tract of
the great Southern continent that is still in the main a
terra incognita to the white man.” Rot! The N.W.
corner may not be as well-known as the S.E., but it is no
more a terra incognita to white men than Hyde Park,
Sydney. Then there was Carr Boyd, who skirmished
round little matters of detail. Why did he not point out
that Grien could not possibly go overland from Cambridge
Gulf to Carpentaria without blundering on the telegraph
line, or coming across cattle-stations very nearly all the
way? Then there was Carnegie, in London, who lately
made the dashing trip to Kimberley and back through
the heart of the continent, which may be said to have
shut the book of Australian exploration. No, the Aus-
tralians should have simply tied Grien down to dates and
localities, then they could have opened their respective
year-books and said, “ You say that you were at so-and-so
in such a year. Well, just then there was a mining rush
there, or a station had been formed there, or a new lock-
up had just been put up”; that would at once have
reduced him to powder. Instead of which they gave him
every advantage, by engaging in loose arguments over
questions that were not at all vital, and left Rougemont’s
absurd “facts” unchallenged. No, the Australians did
not come out of it too well.

Perhaps you are one of those people, and are not
aware of it. You have something the matter
with you, but, not having any experience, treat it
lightly, while it gets worse and worse, untimately
destroying you.
Phe wise man will at once put himself in the
witness-box, and ask himself the following ques-
tions, as will the wise woman :
Do I sleep badly ; do I wake up in the morning,
even when I appear to have slept all night, fagged
and jaded, with lack-lustre eyes and an inclination
only to go to bed again. Do I eat badly, or, when
Ido eat “well,” does my food agree with and
nourish me ? Do Igo about my work dejectedly—
do I suffer from headaches, pains in either the
limbs or body, and indulge in depressing retros-
pections ? Do I, in short, suffer from a loss of
mental and physical elasticity—that exultant
appreciation of life which all healthy people must
have ?
If to even one of these questions the answer be
“Yes,” then that man realises that he wants a
tonic —something to give him an appetite, to
steady his nerves, and to make him strong and
active in the daytime, and afford him calm and
refreshing sleep at night.
Such a tonic is Minerva Tonic, the world’s
greatest and only permanent restorative, the
praise of which is general and unstinted.
Professor Macadam, Consulting Chemist to the
British Government, writes : —“ Minerva is a
judicious combination of iron, phosphorus, and
alkaloid compounds, which will be found highly
serviceable as a tonic remedy for strengthening
the system.”

the Cardinal Archbishop of Sydney, writes :

Everyone who has had experience of it pronounces
it admirable. ”
Mr. G. D. Clark, ex-M.L.A., Grand Lodge of

clearing the brain, stimulating thought, and mak-
ing work a pleasure.”
Mr. Wm. Stevenson, Manager of the Presby-

able for the Australian climate. ”
