[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
«Thank heaven, we’re off at last! ” and Fynne,
the supercilious, lighted a fresh cigarette as the
mail-steamer’s huge mass began to slide slowly
past the valedictory crowd upon the wharf.
“What miserable humbug this handkerchief-
waving business is ! ”
Mrs. Thickeredge, who was waving her hand-
kerchief, didn’t answer, and Herbert Fynne
looked as black as was possible to his demoiselle-
like features and wax-dolly complexion.
“It’s really quite touching,” he said close
to her ear—“ this parting display of conjugal affec-
tion. You don’t act the devoted wife at all
badly ; but don’t you think the farce has lasted
long enough ? ”
Mrs. Thickeredge—a tawny-haired woman of
the highly-vitalised stamp—flashed round at him
a glance of lightning. “ Farce ? ” she echoed in a
tone that told him he had gone too far ; and, as
he walked away, she looked swiftly from him back
to the grey-tweeded figure of her husband, merg-
ing now into the general mass of the crowd of
farewellers.
“Poor old Ned! ” She said to herself. “ After
all, he’s thrice the man Bertie Fynne is ; no pretty
little nainby-pambyisms about Ned. And yet—
what a strange thing a woman is ?—with all this
boy’s airs and graces, and trumpery little cyni-
cisms, he can stir my heart as Ned could never
stir it. Heart ’—stuff ! I haven’t any heart! But
I suppose I must smooth down the lad’s ruffled
feathers.”
Fynne was again unfortunate in his observa-
tions. “ Got it over ? ” be asked, shading his eyes
with his hand. “ Dearest hubby no longer visi-
ble? What an ass a man does 100k —gazing, and
glowering, and waving farewells —when, all the
time ”
The conceited simper with which he broke off
called up no response upon the woman’s face,
grave and a little stern, as she answered, with
emphasis upon the second word : “My husband,
Mr. Fynne, is in every way your superior. I will
thank you to remember that you cannot afford to
sneer at him —yet! ”
Under-breath the last word was, but Fynne
heard it, and told himself, with an inward sneer,
that no doubt she thought this the correct thing to
say; it would hardly be decent to capitulate out
of hand. He was too callow to comprehend the
complications and contradictions possible to ill-
placed passion.
But, wise in his own little way, he professed im-
mediate penitence. Pladn’t dreamt of anything
like a sneer, he half-whispered ; but he was only
human, and it was rough on a fellow to see her—
well, perhaps she’d understand him better later
on. Didn’t she think the harbor looked all the
lovelier because they were leaving it behind them?
—and so on.
» * * *
“ Good evening, Mr. Thickeredge ! Has she
gone ? ”
Mr. Edward Thickeredge—loitering about the
Domain-gates—took out his cigar and turned
hastily.
“Hullo, Polly!—I expected you from the other
direction. Oh, yes ; she’s gone. The ‘ Benares ’
sailed at noon.” He gave her his arm and they
turned into the Domain. “ What a heavenly
night!— light as day ; just the thing for—lovers !”
A certain half-heartedness about the last word
made the girl look up quickly, showing to the
moonlight a dark cspidyle face that was in keeping
with the liberal curves of her figure. “ Are you
sure you’re glad she’s gone ? ” she asked.
“ Yes—oh, yes ! Certainly ! But, you see, my
dear girl, it’s this way. She’s a splendid little
woman, and she trusts me so thoroughly that—
that”—
The girl strove to withdraw her arm, but
Thiekeredge held it fast.
“Now, don’t be absurd, Polly ! Surely I can
do justice to her without any slight to you. I
can’t help respecting her, you see —even though ”
—he ended the sentence with a kiss.
“I see!’’ the girl said, not very confidently.
“But you haven’t told me who’s gone with her.
She’s in somebody’s charge for the voyage, I sup-
pose ? ”
“ Well, strictly speaking, in the captain’s. As
for the rest, she’ll be well looked after by a friend
of mine.”
“ Gentleman-friend ? ”
“Yes.”
“ Young or handsome, or both ? ”
“What a little inquisitor it is! Young, cer-
tainly ; and, as to looks, I don’t admire the style
of man, myself, but no doubt Fynne would be
considered handsome.”
“Fynne, did you say? Is his other name Her-
bert?' Is he in the Lands Department? ”
Thiekeredge stopped short and stared at her.
“ Why, yes ! I don’t know what his first name is,
but he is certainly in the Lands Department.
Gone to Europe on sick-leave. Do you know
him ? ”
She wouldn’t meet his eye. “ I did know him a
little —three years ago. He had rooms at mother’s.
And —you were saying your wife trusts you
thoroughly. Do you trust her thoroughly ?”
“Entirely!* But why do you ask the question
as if in connection with mention of Fynne ? You
don’t consider him dangerous, do you ?—a band-
boxy, scented little animal like that ! ”
“ There’s no accounting for a woman’s fancy,”
the girl said, with her head half-turned away from
him. “Nineteen out of twenty of us, perhaps,
would prefer you to him, but the twentieth might
love him, for all that, with a love you could never
inspire ! ”
“ Why, what the dev— deuce is all this, Polly ?
How dreadfully high-flown and romantic we’ve
grown all in a moment ! You almost speak as if
—tell me—was there anything between you and
Fynne ?”
The girl laughed constrainedly. “Why, I do
believe you’re a little bit jealous ! Is it about me,
or about your wife ? Her, you say, you trust en-
tirely.”
“ And I do ! I suppose I’ve deprived myself of
the right to blame her, if she—-well, if she should
do as I am doing ; but there’s no more chance of
such a thing than of that moon going backwards.”
* * * *
“ I should just as soon expect that moon to set
in the east ! ” was, curiously enough, what—much
about the same time—Mrs. Thiekeredge was
answering to a similar question about her husband.
“ No, no, Bert—Mr. Fynne, I mean —I’d stake
my existence on old Ned’s fidelity. And—and—
it’s more than I deserve.”
“ Nonsense, Amy—l may call you Amy, mayn’t
I, now?— and call me Bertie, as you were going to.
You deserve anything the world can give; and,
in any case, Thiekeredge could never have appre-
ciated you as I do. You were a ‘ jewel of gold
in ’ ”
“ Eh ? ” sharply.
“Well —er —no! I didn’t exactly mean to call
the poor chap names; especially, dearest, as you
have now found the right man and I the ri'ght
woman.”
It was poor stuff enough—and she knew it—but
she loved him, or thought she did, and it was very
pleasant, in that quiet corner of the moonlit pro-
menade-deck, to sit and listen to such amorous
murmurings mingling with the soft rush of water
from the cleaving stem.
But this halcyon sort of thing didn’t last—
couldn’t, in the nature of things—for Mrs.
Thiekeredge had the more than average brain
which often accompanies the animalism that is
not animalism for its own sake. And, in the over-
continuous intimacy of board-ship existence, she
couldn’t help discovering that inside Fynne’s
handsome head there was nothing to compensate
for the sub-feminine characteristics that had, even
from the first, been as a jarring note to the typical
womanliness of her own personality. There is a
love that can love even where it cannot respect.
She had thought hers was such, and the discovery
that it wasn’t brought her idol with surprising
rapidity down off his pedestal. Feet of clay she
had suspected all along, but she wasn’t prepared
to find that her god was mud throughout, and the
result of the discovery became immediately
patent to the concentrated world of the Benares.
“Dropped him, sir,” said one observer to an-
other, “ like a hot potato ! ”
To which Number Two—“ Dropped him!
Dropped it, you mean ! Women of that stamp
may play for a time with a boy or a barber’s-block,
but they always want a man, in the long run, to
take the taste out of their mouths ! ”
* * * *
Meantime the man she had left behind her was
experiencing his little disillusionments. The girl
he had thought so deliciously unworldly and in-
genuous had developed an unpleasantly-practical
solicitude about the whys and the wherefores of
the situation.
“ She’ll be coming back some time, I suppose ?”
Polly Banbury asked, one night about the time
the Benares was feeling her way up Channel in a
fog.
“Coming back? Why, of course, my dear; I
never pretended otherwise.”
“No ; I know you didn’t. But—what I want
to know, Ned, is—how is it to be ? Are you goin’
in for a divorce, or what ? ”
“Coin’ in for a divorce!” The expression
jarred horribly upon Thiekeredge—a not unrefined
and somewhat convention-ridden man—and it was
half-a-minute before he answered.
“ I have no grounds, my dear girl, for divorce
proceedings. Couldn’t we—couldn’t we go on just
as we are ? ”
“What! —when she’s back? Why, sooner or
later, she’d be sure to smell a rat!—and then the
fat would be in the fire, and no mistake ! ”
“Smell a rat! Fat in the fire!” he mentally
repeated. “ Really, after all, there is half the
battle in being a lady ! Now, this little girl”—
“This little girl” lost no time in making
matters worse.
“ You see, Ned, you and me will just have to
understand each other! It’s either her or me—
and I want to know which ? ”
“ My dear girl- be reasonable ”
“ To know which ? ”
“ Polly, I ”
“Which?”
“ Well, then— you ! ”
“ I don’t believe you ! No man who meant it
would have taken so long to make up his mind.
Not that I care very much, after all—now T under-
stand you’ve only been fooling with me. You
stick to your wife, old man, and I’ll stick to ”
“ Fynne, I suppose ? ” A faint sense of jealousy
mingled oddly in Tliickeredge’s mind with a cer-
tain feeling of relief, as the girl, without another
word, walked off and left him.
“Better so!” he said. “The game wasn’t
worth the candle. Hanged if I don't wish Amy
were back again ! ”
And Amy, at the other end of the earth, had
already wished the same.
* * » *
Mr. and Mrs. Thickeredge, when a twelve-
month had re-united them, sat one evening in the
Botanical Gardens and retrospected.
“Only fancy Fynne being married,” quoth,
languidly, the lady. “He hasn’t lost much time
since his return. Miss Panbury ! who is— or was
—Miss Panbury—any idea, Ned ? ”
“I—ah—fancy I’ve seen her somewhere.” He
had sat with her a score of times on that very
bench! “By the way ’’ hastily —“ what did
you think of Fynne? Thrown a good deal into
his company on the voyage, of course.” r. . J
He looked carefully away across the harbour as
he spoke, and didn’t see the flush that sprang to
her cheek as—also hastily—she answered : —“Too
much ! There’s nothing in him, Why, you good
old faithful Ned, there’s nobody like yourself,
after all ! ”
Her good old faithful Ned squirmed as if the
seat had been red hot. He could almost have
thought she suspected and was “ rubbing it in,”
“Faithful ! ” he said, without venturing to look
at her. “Why, my dear Amy, who could be
otherwise—knowing that the first thought in his
far-away wife’s loyal little heart was all the time
of him ! ”
It was her turn to wince. Could he have heard
anything? But, no—the dear old felloiv judged
others by his honest, single-hearted self.
She sque.ezed his arm. “ How quite too utterly
delightful it is,” she whispered, “for each to have
such perfect trust in the other ! ”
“ Darling! ” he was beginning, with responsive
pressure, when from behind came noiselessly over
the springy grass a couple who glanced down as
the Ihickeredge pair looked up ; then four aston-
ished eyes gazed into four no less astonished for
half-a-dozen seconds before Mrs. Thickeredge rose
to the situation and her feet.
“How d’you do, Mr Fynne? Really, now,
this is an agreeable surprise! And this, I pre-
sume, is Mrs. Fynne? Pray present me. So
charmed to meet you, Mrs. Fynne! Let me
introduce my husband ; that is, if you don’t
remember him. He was saying just nbw that he
thought he had met a Miss Panbury somewhere.”
Mrs.—Polly Panbury—Fynne hooked -on in
turn to the situation. “ Yes ; I rather fancy I
remember Mr. Thickeredge’s face. (The wretch ! ”
—mentally—“ why, it was on ;this very seat he
gave me the first kiss).”
“Sorry to have disturbed your matrimonial con-
fidences, Thickeredge.” Fynne’s sarcastic drawl
belied his words. “ Delightful thing, conjugal
felicity—especially after separation.”'
He flashed his teeth at the husband, but his
gaze, resting upon the wife’s face, asked unmis-
takably “Mum?” and “Mum!” Her blue eye
answered as distinctly as did Mrs, Fynne’s bright
black orb to Thickeredge’s unspoken question to
the same effect.
And thus to Sydney’s myriad “Mums” was
added yet another.
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.


Correspondent Colebatch, who too-freely
wired the report of the Westralian Parliamentary-
fight that didn’t take place, is chess champion of
the province—and a chess champion may do almost
anything. For a comparative youngster, and
especially a diffident one, Colebatch has figured
pretty considerably, his first important public
appearance being as chief lay witness for the
Crown in the Broken Hill strike-conspiracy cases
—he happened to have the most complete sheaf of
notes of the “ trolly ” speeches.
When the big American troop-ship Arizona
was about to sail from Seattle for Manila, the man
who was to have commanded her suddenly de-
clined to become responsible—with a Chinese crew
—for the safety of 1500 lives and 1,000,000 dollars.
The Sydney friends of Capt. John Barneson,
formerly trading here in the George Thompson,
will be interested.to know that he promptly filled
the breach and took the vessel to her destination.
Capt. Barneson is member of a Seattle shipping
firm and is a nephew of the octogenarian Capt.
James Brennan of Sydney.

“ Too Little Blood.”
ANOTHER PALE AUSTRALIAN GIRL
MADE ROSY-CHEEKED AND STRONG.
Miss Rose Kennerdale, of Carnarvon-street,
North Auburn, Sydney, suffered severely from
“ too little blood.” She says : “About 18 months
ago I began to lose the good health and spirits I
had previously possessed. I became quite pale
and bloodless. My blood was being slowly drained
from me, and I could hardly lift one foot after the
other. I also suffered severely from headaches. I
gradually became worse and worse. The hot
weather tried me, for I would feel the greatest
lassitude during the whole day—in fact, I would
toss about bed at night and would gasp for breath,
while to walk fast or ascend a hill was simply tor-
ture. Sometimes, after walking, my heart would
beat alarmingly, and I would be obliged to stand
still for a few minutes, with my hands to
my sides, to obtain a little relief before
making another start. I really used to think
that I would suffocate. I lost all my
color, and my hands were bloodless and shrunken.
I hadn't the strength to eat. A doctor told me I
was suffering from anaemia, or bloodlessness, that
my system was completely run-down, and that I
would have to undergo treatment for six months.

Weak and Depressed.
That is the way thousands of people feel during the Summer
months. It is one of nature's signs that humanity cannot
undergo months of indoor life in imperfectly-ventilated build-
ings with impunity. Those who feel thus may not realise that
there is anything seriously wrong with them. They doubtless
think “ Oh, I'll be alright in a few weeks."
“But will you?"
Breathing daily the poisonous gases arising from impure
air, your blood has become poor and watery, your nerves un-
strung, your appetite fickle, the least exercise tires you, and you
feel —as it is often expressed —“ all out of sorts." In this con-
dition your system is very susceptible to disease.
What you require is is a tonic to help nature fight your
battle for health. There is only one always reliable tonic,
and that is
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
These pills do not purge and weaken like other medicines.
They make rich, red blood , build up the nerves, and make weak,
depressed, and easily-tired people cheerful, active and strong.

The Cocky’s Handy Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The soldier has his glory, and the sailor has his
joy,
And we’ve heard in song and story of the little
cabin-boy;
The shearer’s mostly beering when he isn’t on the
strike;
The city clerk’s careering on the “ time-ex-
tended’’bike;
Their web of life they spin it on a fast and fevered
plan,
But for fun they aren’t in it with the “cocky’s
handy man.”
Then it’s feed the squealing “dinnis,” and it’s
yard the milkers up ;
The sun’s behind Maginnis in—“Hi ! Patsy,
chain that pup!
Put up thim rails behind you, there, you good-for-
nothinglout;
’Tis often I remind you—Holy Smoke ! the pig is
out;
Here, Tiger—heel him—heel him! head him,
Patsy, at the fince ;
Hurr—hiss, Tiger 1 wheel him, there, you gaping
want-o’-sinse.”
And “dinnis ” listens gravely to the wild halloo
they raise,
Then runs the gauntlet bravely into Dan Ma-
lowny’s “ pays. ”
The carrier is jolly, and the drover doesn’t care ;
The navvy’s full of folly, and a demon on the
“ tear ”;
The miner has his “ moments,” and the syndicate
the mine ;
The “ push ” a'ruction foments when it’s out upon
the wine;
But for rorty.joy and rapid under Heaven’s
spacious span'
The chance you cannot cap it of the “cocky’s
handy man. ”
Then it’s git up, Captain—Punch, there—that’s a
coo-ee from the stack-;
They’ve got the blanky lunch there —but Kitty’ll
save my wliack;
Her eyes are black and blazing, but for me they’re
ever kind,
And in their depths a-gazing I can read a willing
mind.
Gee-off [—this blessed lurching knocks the neatest
load awry,
And clouds of flies are searching in recess of nose
and eye.
Way—woh—look-out! it’s over. Oh, condemn
the crimson hole !
Pm booked an early “rover”—hear Maginnis
bless my soul!
There are places and positions worth' the while of

And phases and conditions with the shining sheen

BEN SUN.
Two Derelicts.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAD known Lydia Sylvester as an actress of old
women in various travelling companies. When
salaries were regular, or landlords trusting—strong
waters moved her to confidences. These were
either bitter bewailing over a spoilt career, ending
in barnstorming, or pathetic hopes of a future yet
to be hers. But as she had never asked me to
carry a bag for her, or wished me to make love to
her, I had kindly recollections of the warm-
hearted, if maudlin, old lady. Therefore, when
the secretary of the Association told me she had
applied for relief I offered to call and report.
On Poverty Point I met a number of actors be-
guiling the tedium of awaiting engagements by
laughing over one of Ricky Cumberland’s stories.
Somebody had told me that Ricky was once a
star Romeo, but I had never met an old actor who
was not. I mentioned my errand, and the Rabe-
laisian grin died off Ricky’s ribald lips.
“Dying!” he repeated in the tone of one re-
ceiving a terrible reminder.
♦•• * * '
“It was I introduced her to the business.
Forty years ago you’d have said she couldn’t die.
There was no dying in those days.”
Ricky had offered to accompany me—the slow-
ness of his hobbling gait being compensated for
by the swift succession of his reminiscences. :
“ In the sixties I might have started down this
street sober, but I’d never get so fax without
wanting a cab. ' They’d simply drag me into the
.houses. In women and waistcoats I tell you Mr;
Richard Cumberland headed the bill. But now—-
I’ve almost forgotten what it’s like to be called
mister. I say, young man, this is a most God-
abandoned city,” he said, stopping impressively.
I thought he alluded to a highly-colored damsel
who had just passed, discharging a battery of
ogles.
“ It’s an awful city. I declare to you that I
have stood on that Point this morning, sustaining
a brilliant flow of anecdote, and not a man asked
me could I construe a long beer.” ;
Luckily, I had sixpence—it was only two days
since my last treasury—and so was able to rise
to the occasion. After we came out of the bar,
Ricky’s garrulity became intensified.
“Ah! we knew how to drink in those days. It
was that made us the men we are. Never mind
what Cassio says. Shakespeare was probably
drunk when he wrote it. I’ve always had to be
when I’ve played it. There’s something in blank
verse that demands beer. In those days the actors
drank, the critics drank, the audience drank, and
one helped the other. I remember once going in
front with a skinful to see Brooke as Sir Giles.
Knew the piece backwards, of course—had played
in it with him. But this night he gripped my
soul with Satan’s .tongs ; at his last exit he had
acted every drop of liquor out of me, and I was
.sober, Sir—preposterously sober. Just thinking of
that, performance makes me shiver even now.
Could he have done that if I had had nothing but
tea and lettuce in me? I tell you the reason you
have no great actors nowadays is that your
modern teetotal please-and-thank-you fashionable
audiences haven’t got enough ginger ; they’d only
be languidly interested if they were sitting
o.ut the Day of Judgment in tableaux. I re-
member a theatre in Geelong—then a big show-
town —kept by a publican, where a sober
man couldn’t get an engagement. Once,
when I was on the spree, the boys shipped me on
the boat, and I was landed on the pier perfectly
unconscious, with my card pinned on my back, and
driven up on a lorry to the theatre. The manager
—so I was told afterwards—chalked a cross on my
back, had me put to bed till I was sober, and then
gavemea threemonths’engagement. Ah, they were
men in those days. . . . Ranters, eh ! My boy,
you don’t know—your generation can’t conceive
what they were. They’d act your hair into ring-
lets and out again. None of your modern London
waterflies—making up with napkins under their
chins and voices only fit to soothe a baby to sleep
with. ’Spose some of them are passable —why, it’s
only in trousers. Put ’em in tights—where are
they then ? ”
By this time we were turning down a narrow
side-street—once populated and attractive in the
gala days of the boom —now with just enough
squalid life in it to supply trade to the public-
house on the corner.
“ If you had Lydia Sylvester’s name, or mine,
on your boxes you could go anywhere in Aus-
tralia and every landlord would boast of your
staying with him. There used to be strings of
cabs waiting at the stage-door for her. She’d
dodged them out the front till they tumbled, and
at last she used often put on my wardrobe and a
beard and get out through the fellows that way.
Because she used to string ’em all on and say no
to none. There was a fellow offered her .£5O to
be allowed to touch her ankle—and when he wanted
more she told him to add another nought ; he did,
and then she tore up his cheque and told him not
to be a fool. Hah ! She used to bathe in milk—
yes, and champagne.
“Here’s the place, Ricky,” I said, stopping
before a decrepit weatherboard cottage with its
verandah-posts out of plumb, and a. French horn
emitting melancholic groans somewhere in its in-
terior.
Our knocking brought a worried-looking woman
to the door, together with a smell of chops. I ex-
plained my mission, when she grew' somewhat more
gracious.
“ She’s gone quite ratty—goes on h’acting and
wantin’ to dress up. But I had to pawn the last
of her dresses yesterday. She owes ine six weeks
rent. I only got a shilling on it—a blue-spangled
tights thing. Lord knows how she ever got into
it. ’Twouldn’t go round her neck now.” She
then showed us in, down a passage ending in a
perspective of a kitchen and yard beyond, where
two dirty children w ? ero making mud-pies. Then
—to the accompaniment of a roulade by the
unseen horn-player—she opened a door. Ricky
and I entered.
* * * *
Lydia—a toothless, wizened hag, with death in
every feature—was half up in the bed, one hand
clutching a small hand-mirror and the other feel-
ing in a small box on the quilt.
“ My God ! ” whispered Ricky, “ she’s making-
up ! ”
A few ends of grease-paint were in the box, but
she had not strength to sustain her arm. A few
ghastly dabs of rouge had been all she could
accomplish. The landlady explained that the
dying woman had insisted on her make-up box
being gi ven to her; then attendance on the chops
carried her out of the room.
* * * *
“ Hallo, Ricky ! You’ve come, then. I knew
you would forgive me.”
She recognised Ricky, and I grew suddenly
conscious that I ought not to be present. But the
room was small, and Ricky blocked my exit.
“ Ricky, I treated you badly, old man; didn’t
I ? Kiss me ! ”
My back was turned to them—and they ap-
peared quite unconscious of me. I was staring at
an old box with many labels on it, but I distinctly
saw, as well as heard, Ricky’s dry, withered lips
meeting hers. Mixed up with the horn-player’s
scales, the dirty children’s cries, the rattle of the
landlady’s pots, I heard Lydia wandering on, in
and out of delirium, while Ricky, for the first
time for many years, tried to attune his voice to
tones of tenderness.
“But I loved you all the time, Ricky. The
house is full and he’s in front; he has offered me
five thousand a year, but he says he can't marry
me yet—oughtn’t Ito take it? Damn it! don’t
take all the stage —who the hell wants to look at
you? . . . Good-night, Ricky. . . . Number 3001,
Church of England department, old Melbourne
Cemetery. . . . You’ve forgotten it. . . . I know.
. . . Go and have a look at it for me. . . . Listen,
there’s my entrance-music. Lights up !—damn it !
lights up. . . . Ah, Ricky, if the boy had only
lived, I must have been true to you.”
* * * ♦
When I got Ricky outside, he made for the
public-house, instinctively. When he swallowed
his rum, he began to look more like his old self.
Then he started to moralise, and, after another
rum, to tell me a long, disreputable story of
Lydia, a naval officer, and a yachting-trip. The
old dirty lights were rekindled in his eyes, as he
warmed to it—but the picture of him wiping the
rouge off the face of the dying woman was still
with me, and I left without hearing the end.
